McDougall v. Schanz


No. 107956, No. 110707


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


461 Mich. 15; 597 N.W.2d 148; 1999 Mich. LEXIS 2067


May 5, 1999, Argued  
July 30, 1999, Decided  
July 30, 1999, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] (McDougall) Wayne Circuit Court, Paul S. Teranes, J. Court of Appeals, FITZGERALD, J., and P. D. HOUK, J. and TAYLOR, P.J. 218 Mich App 501, 554 N.W.2d 56 (1996) (Docket No. 178042). (Sobran) Oakland Circuit Court, Robert C. Anderson, J. Court of Appeals, JANSEN, P.J., and SAAD and M. D. SCHWARTZ, JJ. (Docket No. 185581).
McDougall v. Eliuk, 218 Mich. App. 501, 554 N.W.2d 56, 1996 Mich. App. LEXIS 266 (1996)
SOBRAN v. ALASDAIR, 1997 Mich. App. LEXIS 2810 (Mich. Ct. App., Apr. 11, 1997)



DISPOSITION: In McDougall, Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversed and the trial court's grant of summary disposition in favor of defendant Eliuk reinstated. In Sobran, Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Leave was granted to appeal actions from the Wayne Circuit Court, and Oakland Circuit Court (Michigan), to determine whether Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.2169, Mich. Stat. Ann. 27A2169, which provides requirements for admission of expert testimony in medical malpractice cases, impermissibly infringed the Michigan Supreme Court's constitutional authority to promulgate court rules.


OVERVIEW: Leave to appeal was granted to determine whether Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.2169, Mich. Stat. Ann. 27A2169 (§ 2169), which provided strict requirements for the admission of expert testimony in medical malpractice cases brought against specialists, impermissibly infringed the Michigan Supreme Court's exclusive authority under Mich. Const. art VI, § 5, to promulgate practice and procedure court rules. The court determined that it appeared that the legislature envisioned and intended that the § 2169 would often compel different qualification determinations than the Mich. R. Evid. 702 when applied to a given case. Because § 2169 and Mich. R. Evid. 702 clearly conflicted, the court had to determine whether the statute impermissibly infringed upon the court's constitutional authority to enact rules governing practice and procedure. The court concluded § 2169 was an enactment of substantive law, thus it did not impermissibly infringe the court's constitutional rule-making authority over "practice and procedure."


OUTCOME: In the first action, the judgment of the court of appeals was reversed and the trial court's grant of summary disposition in favor of defendant was reinstated. In the second action, the judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed, because the statute restricting the admission of expert testimony did not impermissibly infringe the court's constitutional rule-making authority over "practice and procedure."

People v. Stevens


No. 110866


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


460 Mich. 626; 597 N.W.2d 53; 1999 Mich. LEXIS 1903


March 10, 1999, Argued  
July 20, 1999, Decided  
July 20, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied February 22, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 1170.


PRIOR HISTORY: Detroit Recorders Court, Michael F. Sapala, J. Court of Appeals, DOCTOROFF, P.J., and MARKMAN and O'CONNELL, JJ. (Docket No. 180914). Supreme Court vacated the order, and remanded the case to the Court of Appeals. 453 Mich 904 (1996). On remand, the Court of Appeals (Docket No. 199175).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeals reversed and this case remanded to the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prosecution challenged the order of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the grant of appellee's motion to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant prosecution challenged grant of appellee's motion to suppress evidence. Appellee, accused of criminal wrongdoing, filed the motion after evidence was seized from his home in violation of the knock and announce rule set forth in Mich. Comp. Laws § 780.656 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1259(6)). On appeal, the court determined that the inevitable discovery exception to the exclusionary rule applied because police officers had a valid search warrant. The court reasoned that the discovery of evidence inside appellee's house was "inevitable" once the police arrived with a warrant. Therefore, the court reversed the grant of appellee's motion to suppress, regardless of the illegalities concerning the police officers' entry into appellee's home.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; appellee's motion to suppress was denied, because an exception to the exclusionary rule applied where discovery of the evidence was inevitable.

 

3 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Consumers Power Co. v. PSC


Docket Nos. 111482, 111483, 111486, 111487, 111719-111726.


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


460 Mich. 148; 596 N.W.2d 126; 1999 Mich. LEXIS 1379


March 11, 1999, Argued  
June 29, 1999, Decided  
June 29, 1999, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Michael. J. KELLY, P.J., and REILLY and JANSEN, JJ. 227 Mich App 442 (1998) (Docket Nos. 187387, 187388, 189396, 189397, 189480, 189481, 189503, 189504).



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversed and the PSC order implementing the program vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sought review of an order by the Michigan Court of Appeals which affirmed a decision of the Michigan Public Service Commission (PSC) ordering plaintiffs to engage in "retail wheeling."


OVERVIEW: In 1992, the Michigan Public Service Commission (PSC) was petitioned to implement a retail wheeling program. After examining the statutory framework, the appointed referee concluded that the PSC could not compel a utility to provide retail wheeling services, but may establish tariffs setting the rates and conditions of a voluntarily provided service. Rejecting that conclusion, the PSC implemented retail wheeling pursuant to its alleged authority under the electric transmission act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 460.551 et seq., public service commission act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 460.1 et seq., and railroad commission act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 462.2 et seq. Plaintiffs utility companies appealed. The Michigan Supreme Court reversed, concluding that the plain language of the statutes did not grant the PSC authority to order retail wheeling. Absent a statute clearly conferring on the PSC such power, the decision to provide transmission services lies with the utility's management, not the PSC.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the Michigan Court of Appeals reversed and the Public Service Commission (PSC) order implementing the program vacated. The PSC lacked the statutory authority to order retail wheeling.

Federated Publs., Inc. v. Michigan State Univ. Bd. of Trustees


No. 109663


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


460 Mich. 75; 594 N.W.2d 491; 1999 Mich. LEXIS 2069


January 22, 1999, Argued  
June 15, 1999, Decided  
June 15, 1999, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Ingham Circuit Court, James R. Giddings, J. Court of Appeals, FITZGERALD, J., and NEFF, P.J. and C. A. NELSON, J. 221 Mich App 103, 561 N.W.2d 433 (1997) (Docket No. 177264).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals decision reversed and the trial court's order granting summary disposition for defendant reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state university board of trustees sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) in favor of plaintiffs, reversing summary judgment entry for defendant in suit filed by plaintiffs challenging defendant's use of a presidential selection procedure under the Open Meetings Act (OMA), pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 15.261 et seq. and whether application of the OMA for the selection of university presidents was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant state university board of trustees formed a presidential search committee to assist it in selecting a new president for the university. The school newspaper published a confidential list of nominees under consideration. In response, defendant continued the presidential search in private. After narrowing the search, defendant released names of candidates to the public. Plaintiff newspapers filed suit against defendant on the basis that defendant violated the Open Meetings Act (OMA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 15.261. Both parties filed cross-motions for summary judgment. The trial court granted defendant's motion, which was reversed on appeal. Defendant sought review of the decision. The court reversed summary judgment in favor of defendant because application of the OMA to the internal operations of the university in selecting a president infringed on defendant's constitutional power to supervise the institution under Mich. Const. 1963, art. 8, § 5. Further, the court held that under Mich. Const. 1963, art. 8, § 4, defendant's presidential selection process was not required to be open to the public except when conducted at a formal session of the governing board.


OUTCOME: The court reversed summary judgment for plaintiff newspapers, ruling in favor of defendant state university board of trustees, in suit filed by plaintiffs challenging defendant's presidential selection process under the Open Meetings Act (OMA). The court held that application of the OMA to committees formed by governing boards of public universities to assist in the selection of university presidents was unconstitutional.

City of Rochester Hills v. Schultz


No. 110294


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


459 Mich. 486; 592 N.W.2d 69; 1999 Mich. LEXIS 807


January 20, 1999, Argued  
April 27, 1999, Decided  
April 27, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Updated Copy December 14, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: 52nd District Court, James P. Sheehy, J. Court of Appeals, MARKEY, P.J., and BANDSTRA and HOEKSTRA, JJ. (Docket No. 193500). 224 Mich App 323; 568 N.W.2d 832 (1997).



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversed, and remanded to the trial court so that it may proceed in a manner consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city appealed a decision of the Michigan Court of Appeals affirming a ruling that Rochester Hills, Mich., Ordinance 200, § 4.02, which prohibited signs in residential areas to advertise home businesses, was unconstitutional because it was more extensive than necessary to advance the interest of protecting the character of residential neighborhoods. Plaintiff claimed that a total ban on such signs was required to advance that interest.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff city ticketed defendant for violating Rochester Hills, Mich., Ordinance 200, § 4.02 by erecting a sign in a residential area to advertise her home business. Defendant moved to dismiss the ticket, claiming the ordinance unconstitutionally abridged her right to commercial speech under U.S. Const. amend. I. Trial court granted defendant's motion and appellate court affirmed, holding that the ban on home occupation signs was more extensive than necessary to advance plaintiff's asserted interest in protecting the character of residential neighborhoods. Plaintiff appealed, claiming that the ordinance was necessary to that interest. State supreme court reversed. It explained that the ordinance was not concerned with the content of the message because it left open ample means for a home business to convey its commercial message to customers. Additionally, stopping short of a total ban on home occupation signs would not advance plaintiff's interest because it might still allow blurring of the line between residential and commercial districts. As a result, supreme court held that the ordinance was constitutional as a legitimate restriction on defendant's commercial speech rights.


OUTCOME: State supreme court reversed lower court rulings that a city ordinance banning signs in residential areas to advertise home businesses was unconstitutional. Supreme court held that the ordinance was constitutional as a legitimate restriction of defendant's commercial speech rights because it was necessary to advance plaintiff city's legitimate interest in protecting the character of residential neighborhoods.

Straus v. Governor


No. 112401


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


459 Mich. 526; 592 N.W.2d 53; 1999 Mich. LEXIS 805


November 9, 1998, Argued  
April 27, 1999, Decided  
April 27, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Updated Copy December 14, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: Ingham Circuit Court, Carolyn Stell, J. O'CONNELL, WHITBECK, JJ., and FITZGERALD, P.J. (Docket No. 204457). 230 Mich App 222; 583 N.W.2d 520 (1998).



DISPOSITION: Decision of the circuit court reversed and, pursuant to MCR 7.216(A)(7), a judgment of no cause for action granted in favor of the defendant. We also give this decision immediate effect pursuant to MCR 7.215(E)(2). We do not retain jurisdiction. No taxable costs pursuant to MCR 7.219, a question of public significance being involved. [230 Mich. App. 224-242, 583 N.W.2d 520.] 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the governor, appealed from the Ingham Circuit Court (Michigan), which entered an injunction and declaratory judgment, in plaintiff's suit challenging the actions of the governor in promulgating two executive orders.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, elected members of the State Board of Education, challenged, as being in excess of the authority vested in the governor by the state constitution, the actions of the governor in promulgating certain executive orders. The orders transferred various powers of the board to the Superintendent of Public Instruction. The circuit court entered a declaratory judgment and permanent injunction against the implementation of these orders by the governor. On appeal, the court reversed. The court found that the governor was able, under the constitution, to alter the current education scheme, and delegate power to the superintendent. Because the legislature could have delegated such powers to the superintendent initially, the governor was free to redistribute those powers within the Department of Education in any manner in which the legislature could have assigned such authority originally. The court also found that neither of the orders facially infringed any of the board's constitutional powers or prerogatives, and were within the scope of the governor's authority.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the finding in favor of plaintiff, because the court found that the orders did not infringe any of the State Board of Education's constitutional powers or prerogatives under the constitution, and the governor had the power to delegate various powers of the Board to the Superintendent of Public Instruction.

Tyler v. Livonia Pub. Schs


No. 109196


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


459 Mich. 382; 590 N.W.2d 560; 1999 Mich. LEXIS 445


October 7, 1998, Argued  
March 23, 1999, Decided  
March 23, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Updated Copy December 14, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: Worker's Compensation Appellate Commission Court of Appeals, GRIFFIN, P.J., and MACKENZIE, and SAWYER, JJ. (Docket No. 169388). Supreme Court remanded the case to the Court of Appeals 447 Mich. 970 (1994). Court of Appeals, MARKEY and P. J. SULLIVAN, JJ., SMOLENSKI, P.J. (Docket No. 179336). 220 Mich App 697; 561 N.W.2d 390 (1996).



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff disabled worker was granted leave to appeal from the Court of Appeals (Michigan) to determine whether the Michigan Worker's Disability Compensation Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.354(14), excepted public disability pension payments from coordination with worker's compensation payments and, if not, whether the resulting reduction in worker's compensation violated Mich. Const. art 9, § 24.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that the exception to coordination with pension plans for worker's compensation benefits found in Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.354(14) did not apply to public pension plans. The Michigan Legislature had enacted the exception primarily with the negotiation of collective bargaining agreements by the private sector in mind rather than public pension plans established by statute. Further, the application of different rules to private and public plans mirrored the federal legislation that dominated the field of pensions. Construing the statute to require coordination did not diminish or impair an accrued financial benefit under a state pension plan in violation of Mich. Const. art. 9, § 24 because it was worker's compensation payments that were reduced by coordination. Benefits awarded under the worker's compensation statutes did not create rights protected by the Contract Clause of either the state or federal constitutions. Therefore, although it was not necessary to determine whether plaintiff's disability pension benefit had accrued as of the date of the statutory enactment as the lower court had done, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower court that a statutory exception to coordination with worker's compensation benefits did not apply to public pension plans was affirmed because the exception was intended to apply only to private pension plans and coordination was not unconstitutional because it did not reduce payments under a state pension plan.

Judicial Attys. Ass'n v. State


Nos. 111782, 111785


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


459 Mich. 291; 586 N.W.2d 894; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 3240


October 7, 1998, Argued  
December 28, 1998, Decided  
December 28, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Updated Copy December 14, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: Wayne Circuit Court, Robert J. Colombo, Jr., J. Court of Appeals, O'CONNELL and SMOLENSKI, JJ., and M. J. Kelly, P.J. Court of Appeals, MCDONALD and FITZGERALD, JJ. and MARKMAN, P.J. (Docket No. 201852). 228 Mich App 386; 579 N.W.2d 378 (1998).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the state and the county, sought review of a decision from the court of appeals (Michigan), holding that Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.593a, making defendant county the employer of plaintiff unions' judicial and recorder employee members, was an unconstitutional violation of the Separation of Powers Clause of Mich. Const. art. 3, § 2 (1963).


OVERVIEW: Defendants, the state and the county, challenged the court of appeal's decision holding that a statute that changed the employer of local judicial employees from the state to the county unconstitutionally violated Mich. Const. art. 3, § 2 (1963)'s Separation of Powers Clause. Plaintiff employee unions challenged the change of employers, which occurred when the former employer, the state judicial council, was statutorily abolished. Granting leave to appeal, the court held that the judicial branch had inherent authority to manage its own administrative affairs, including its own employees. As part of the executive branch, defendants could not interfere with the judiciary's prerogative to manage its own employees. The court acknowledged that in the past, some chief judges had thwarted defendants' efforts to control court operating costs; therefore, under separate order, the court extended the provisions of its own administrative order No. 1997-6 to address such problems. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeal's decision to the effect that the separation of powers clause of the state constitution was violated by a state statute that named defendant county as the employer of plaintiff unions' local judicial employee members because the judicial branch had inherent authority to manage its own employees.

Bolt v. City of Lansing


No. 108511


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


459 Mich. 152; 587 N.W.2d 264; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 3239


October 6, 1998, Argued  
December 28, 1998, Decided  
December 28, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Updated Copy December 14, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of Appeals, SAAD, P.J., and WAHLS, J. and MARKMAN, J. (Docket No. 912944). 221 Mich App 79; 561 N.W.2d 423 (1997).



DISPOSITION: Decision of the Court of Appeals reversed and case remanded to that Court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner appealed a decision of the court of appeals (Michigan), which held that a storm water service charge imposed by defendant city did not violate the Headlee Amendment, Mich. Const. art. 9, §§ 25-31, because it constituted a valid user fee.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff property owner filed suit against defendant city, alleging that a storm water service charge imposed by Lansing, Mich., Ordinance 925 was unconstitutional under the Headlee Amendment, Mich. Const. art. 9, §§ 25-31. The court of appeals held that the storm water service charge did not violate the Headlee Amendment, because the charge constituted a valid user fee. Plaintiff appealed. On de novo review, the court reversed, holding that the storm water service charge was a tax and not a valid user fee, thereby remanding for further proceedings. The court distinguished between a fee and a tax, and concluded that the charges imposed did not correspond to the benefits conferred, and that the charge applied to all property owners, not just those who actually benefitted. Furthermore, the charge lacked any element of volition, also supporting the conclusion that the charge was a tax.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of defendant city, which held that a storm water service charge was a fee, and held that the charge constituted a tax which violated the state constitution, and remanded for further proceedings.

 

10 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


McCready v. Hoffius


No. 108995, No. 108996


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


459 Mich. 131; 586 N.W.2d 723; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 3234


October 7, 1998, Argued  
December 22, 1998, Decided  
December 22, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Updated Copy December 14, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: (McCready) Jackson Circuit Court, Edward J. Grant, J. (Baiz) Jackson Circuit Court, Alexander C. Perlos, J. Court of Appeals, CORRIGAN, P.J., and TAYLOR and D. A. JOHNSTON, JJ. (Docket Nos. 185152, 185314). 222 Mich App 210; 564 N.W.2d 493 (1997).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, unmarried couples, appealed a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Michigan). The court of appeals affirmed the judgment of two trial courts, which granted summary disposition to defendant landlords after plaintiffs claimed that defendants violated the Civil Rights Act, Mich. Comp. Laws. § 37.2502(1), by refusing to rent to plaintiffs.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, unmarried couples, sought to rent residential property from defendant landlords. Defendants refused to rent to plaintiffs, claiming that unmarried cohabitation violated their religious beliefs. Plaintiffs filed two separate actions. Both trial courts held that unmarried cohabitation was unprotected conduct under the Civil Rights Act, Mich. Comp. Laws. § 37.2502(1), and granted summary disposition to defendants. The court of appeals affirmed. The court reversed. Plaintiffs' marital status, not their conduct in living together, was what defendants found fundamentally objectionable. The Act, which prohibited discrimination on the basis of marital status, protected married and unmarried couples alike. The Act did not require defendants to violate their religious beliefs; it required only that if defendants wanted to participate in the real estate market, they had to comply with the statute. Even if the Act burdened defendants' beliefs, this burden was justified by the compelling state interest in eradicating housing discrimination. Defendants had not shown that the state could have accomplished this goal by less obtrusive means.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary disposition in favor of defendant landlords. The court remanded for entry of an order granting summary disposition in favor of plaintiffs, unmarried couples who sought housing from defendants. Defendants' refusal to rent to plaintiffs solely because they were unmarried constituted marital status discrimination in violation of the Civil Rights Act. The statute did not violate defendants' religious freedom rights.

People v. Neal


No. 108016


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


459 Mich. 72; 586 N.W.2d 716; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 3195



December 15, 1998, Decided  
December 15, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Updated Copy December 14, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: Genesee Circuit Court, Judith A. Fullerton, J. Court of Appeals, D. E. HOLBROOK, JR., P.J., and SAAD and W. J. GIOVAN, JJ. (Docket No. 174643).



DISPOSITION: This case remanded to the circuit court for transcription of the jury voir dire. After the defendant receives a copy of that transcript, he may within twenty-eight days file in the Court of Appeals an appeal of right relating only to issues, if any, arising from the voir dire. In all other respects, leave to appeal is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Genesee Circuit Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed his conviction for possessing less than 50 grams of heroin with intent to deliver, Mich. Comp. Laws § 333.7401(2)(a)(iv); Mich. Stat. Ann. § 14.15(7401)(2)(a)(iv). Defendant argued that it was error to deny him a transcript of the jury voir dire for purposes of appeal.


OVERVIEW: Following a raid on his house, defendant was charged with possessing less than 50 grams of heroin with intent to deliver pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 333.7401(2)(a)(iv); Mich. Stat. Ann. § 14.15(7401)(2)(a)(iv). Subsequently, a jury convicted him of the charged offense. Defendant, after obtaining new counsel, appealed. The lower court affirmed his conviction. Defendant then appealed the lower court's judgment, arguing that it was error to deny him a transcript of the jury voir dire for purposes of his appeal. The court held that it was error to deny defendant a transcript of voir dire because his appellate counsel was not defendant's appointed trial counsel. Therefore, in order to faithfully discharge his duty to assert all claims of error supported by the record, defendant's appellate counsel had to have access to transcripts of all proceedings so that all issues of legal merit could be raised. As such, the court remanded the case for transcription of the jury voir dire, and further required defendant to file an appeal within 28 days of receiving the transcript.


OUTCOME: The court remanded defendant's appeal for transcription of the jury voir dire, holding that it was error to deny defendant a transcript of voir dire because his appellate counsel was not defendant's appointed trial counsel. As such, in order to discharge his duty to assert all claims of error supported by the record, defendant's appellate counsel had to have access to transcripts of all proceedings.

Klinke v. Mitsubishi Motors Corp.


Nos. 107730, 107731, 107732


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


458 Mich. 582; 581 N.W.2d 272; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 1956; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P15,313


April 7, 1998, Argued  
July 31, 1998, Decided  
July 31, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Livingston Circuit Court, Daniel A. Burress, J. Court of Appeals, MCDONALD, P.J., and MARKMAN and C. W. JOHNSON, JJ. (Docket No. 168384). 219 Mich. App. 500; 556 N.W.2d 528 (1996).



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals affirmed and case remanded to the trial court for recalculation of damages, consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, father of decedent, appealed the decision of the Livingston Circuit Court, Court of Appeals (Michigan), which held that the seat belt statute in the Michigan Vehicle Code, Mich. Comp. Laws § 257.710e(5), did not apply to product liability actions.


OVERVIEW: Decedent, the 23-year old woman, died in a one car roll-over accident. Her father filed suit against the manufacturer of the automobile, alleging defects in the seat belt design and defective manufacture of a portion of the steering mechanism. After a jury trial, the jury returned a verdict of $ 5,104,000 against the manufacturer, but found that the decedent was 90 percent negligent for not properly using her seat belt. The trial court found that a statutory five-percent comparative negligence cap for nonuse of a seat belt applied, and awarded the father $ 4,848,000. The court of appeals reversed the award, holding that the five-percent cap for non-use of a seat belt did not apply to product liability actions against a manufacturer. The court agreed. The court, in affirming the lower court, found that the seat belt statute in the Michigan Vehicle Code, Mich. Comp. Laws § 257.710e(5), was not intended by the legislature to apply to civil liability of manufacturers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision that the seat belt statute did not apply to the father's product liability action, and remanded for the recalculation of damages.

 

13 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


City of Kentwood v. Sommerdyke Estate


No. 109646, No. 109646, No. 109646


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


458 Mich. 642; 581 N.W.2d 670; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 1959


March 3, 1998, Argued  
July 31, 1998, Decided  
July 31, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Kent Circuit Court, H. David Soet, J. Supreme Court granted the city leave to appeal before decision by the Court of Appeals. 456 Mich. 868 (1997).



DISPOSITION: Decision of the circuit court reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city sought review of the decision of the Kent Circuit Court (Michigan), which held that a highway created in accordance with the highway-by-user statute, Mich. Comp. Laws § 221.20 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 9.21), applied only to the extent of actual use, thereby awarding compensation to defendant landowners for land taken pursuant to the statute. The court authorized direct appeal.


OVERVIEW: The city brought a condemnation action to widen a street that affected the landowners' property. The city claimed that the entire area had become a highway-by-user under the statute while the landowners contended that the highway created was limited to the area of actual use. Michigan had the authority to condition the retention of property rights on the performance of conditions that indicated a present intention to retain the property interest within the statutory period, 10 years. The 10-year period was reasonable and provided ample opportunity for the property owner to rebut the presumption of dedication of the entire four-rod width. After dedication, landowners retained no interest for which they could claim compensation and, thus, there was no taking that implicated the Constitution. As neither the property owners nor their predecessors in interest took sufficient action during the running of the 10-year period to give notice of their intent to maintain possession, the dedication was implied for the entire four-rod width and not just the area of actual use.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the circuit court in favor of the landowners and found that no taking had occurred.

 

14 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Mudge v. Macomb County


No. 103985


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


458 Mich. 87; 580 N.W.2d 845; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 1616


November 5, 1997, Argued  
July 1, 1998, Decided  
July 1, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Macomb Circuit Court, Deborah A. Servitto, J. Court of Appeals, D. E. HOLBROOK, JR., P.J., and REILLY and G. W. HOOD, JJ. (Docket No. 140863). 210 Mich. App. 436; 534 N.W.2d 539 (1995).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, county, sheriff, and sheriff's department, sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed a trial court order summarily dismissing appellee accused's complaint against appellants that alleged appellants had obtained ex parte orders from the judge assigned to their respective criminal cases, seizing their bond monies for reimbursement of the expenses of their incarceration in the Macomb County jail.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, county, sheriff and sheriff's department, sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed a trial court order summarily dismissing appellee accused's complaint against appellants that alleged appellants had obtained ex parte orders from the judge assigned to their respective criminal cases, seizing their bond monies for reimbursement of the expenses of their incarceration in the county jail in violation of the Prisoner Reimbursement to the County Act (PRCA), Mich. Comp. Laws 801.81 et seq., and of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The court affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded holding that appellees were entitled to a hearing where a court could consider their support obligations, if any. Thus, the court rejected appellants' claim that appellees' 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action was barred by appellants' failure to exhaust post-deprivation state remedies. The court found that given that the 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 complaint was timely filed, the expiration of the limitation period for a county to bring a PRCA action, in and of itself, was not a bar to appellants seeking recoupment under the PRCA.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded the reversal of a trial court order summarily dismissing appellee accused's complaint against appellants, county, sheriff, and sheriff's department, alleging appellants had obtained ex parte orders seizing their bond monies for reimbursement of the expenses of their incarceration because appellees were entitled to a hearing to consider their obligations.

Glancy v. City of Roseville


No. 106283, No. 109241


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


457 Mich. 580; 577 N.W.2d 897; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 1327


March 3, 1998, Argued  
June 9, 1998, Decided  
June 9, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] (Glancy) Macomb Circuit Court, John B. Bruff, J. Court of Appeals, DOCTOROFF, C.J., and MCDONALD and J. B. SULLIVAN, JJ. (Docket No. 175958). (Ashton) Oakland Circuit Court, Robert C. Anderson, J. Court of Appeals, D. E. HOLBROOK, JR., P.J., and WHITE and A. T. DAVIS, JJ. (Docket No. 182876). 216 Mich. App. 390; 549 N.W.2d 78 (1996).
Glancy v. City of Roseville, 216 Mich. App. 390, 549 N.W.2d 78, 1996 Mich. App. LEXIS 122 (1996)
ASHTON v. CITY OF HAZEL PARK, 1997 Mich. App. LEXIS 2690 (Mich. Ct. App., Apr. 4, 1997)



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals decisions in both Glancy and Ashton affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured parties appealed orders of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed the circuit courts' grants of defendant municipal corporations' motions for summary judgment in two consolidated suits for personal injuries arising out of falls on sidewalks.


OVERVIEW: Two injured parties who sustained falls on sidewalks filed suit against two municipal corporations for negligence. In both cases the trial courts granted summary judgment for the municipal corporations, and each case was reversed by the court of appeals. The common issue was whether Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.1407(1) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.996(107)(1)), which affirmed governmental immunity as it existed before July 1, 1965, codified the "two inch" rule regarding municipal liability for sidewalk defects, despite the 1972 judicial abolition of the rule. On appeal, the court held that the governmental tort liability act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.1401 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.996(101) et seq.), specifically Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.1402(1) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.996(102)(1)), subjected governmental agencies to liability when they failed to maintain highways in "reasonable repair." The "two inch" rule, which provided that defects of two inches or less constituted "reasonable repair" as a matter of law, was a rule about negligence, and was not a common law principle of governmental immunity that the second sentence of Mich. Stat. Ann. § 691.1407(1) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.996(107)(1)) codified.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of the court of appeal.

Massey v. Secretary of State


No. 112047


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


457 Mich. 410; 579 N.W.2d 862; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 1114



May 26, 1998, Decided  
May 26, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wayne Circuit Court, Michael F. Sapala, J.



DISPOSITION: Decision of the Wayne Circuit Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant voters sought review of a judgment from the Wayne Circuit Court (Michigan), which granted appellee Secretary of State (Secretary) summary disposition on the voter's action that alleged the submission to the electors of a proposed constitutional amendment, Mich. Const. art. 4, § 54 (1963), which imposed term limitations, was defective under Mich. Const. art. 12, § 2 (1963).


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to Mich. Const. art. 12, § 2 (1963), electors approved a constitutional amendment, Mich. Const. art. 4, § 54 (1963), which limited the number of terms persons could be elected to the office of state senator and the office of state representative. Thereafter, the voters brought an action against the secretary, claiming the election had failed to comply with the requirements of Mich. Const. art. 12, § 2 (1963). After the trial court granted the Secretary summary disposition, the voters sought review, and the court affirmed. In rejecting the contention that the proposal had failed to include the existing constitutional provision to be altered and abrogated, the court held Mich. Const. art. 4, § 54 (1963) had not altered the list of qualifications under Mich. Const. art. 4, § 7 (1963) because: 1) the list was not exhaustive; and 2) the qualifications had not been directly affected. The court also rejected the contention that the ballot had contained a deficient statement of the purpose of the proposed amendment because: 1) there had been no confusion among electors; 2) the amendment was presumptively legal; and 3) there was no potential that electors had been misled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that granted the Secretary summary disposition on the voter's action that alleged the submission to the electors of a proposed constitutional amendment, which imposed term limitations, was constitutionally defective.

North Ottawa Community Hosp. v. Kieft


No. 105156


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


457 Mich. 394; 578 N.W.2d 267; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 1105


March 4, 1998, Argued  
May 19, 1998, Decided  
May 19, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Ottawa Circuit Court, Wesley J. Nykamp, J. Court of Appeals, M. J. KELLY and A. J. FERRARA, JJ., MARKEY, P.J. (Docket No. 173302). 214 Mich. App. 518; 543 N.W.2d 37 (1995).



DISPOSITION: Decision of the Court of Appeals reversed and this case remanded to the circuit court for entry of judgment in favor of defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, hospital, brought a collection action against defendant widow whose deceased husband received its medical services. The trial court granted the order of summary judgment in favor of the hospital. The widow defended the action on the basis of a provision of the married women's property act (MWPA), which the Court of Appeals found to be unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The circuit court granted summary judgment in favor of the hospital and held that the wife was liable for the medical necessities of her husband. The court of appeals affirmed, ruling that the MWPA was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court held that the legislature enacted the most recent version of the MWPA, declaring that a wife's separate property was not subject to her husband's debts. The court found that the provisions of the MWPA and Michigan Constitution precluded the hospital from recovering from the widow, health care expenses incurred by her deceased husband. The court also found that the provisions did not violate the equal protection clauses of the Michigan and federal constitutions. However, as traditionally formulated, the common-law necessaries doctrine violated equal protection principles, and the court remedied the unconstitutionality by abrogating the doctrine, holding that neither a husband nor a wife was liable, absent express agreement, for necessaries supplied to the other.


OUTCOME: The order of the court of appeals was reversed and remanded to the circuit court for entry of judgment in favor of the widow.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), in an action brought by plaintiffs, former operators and employees of massage parlors. The court of appeals, on prior remand, affirmed the circuit court's finding that two sections of a city's ordinance regulating massage parlors were violative of the state and federal constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs brought suit challenging the constitutionality of defendant's ordinance governing the operation of massage parlors. Oakland County Bd. of County Rd. Comm'rs v. Michigan Prop. & Cas. Guar. Ass'n


Docket Nos. 106446, 107251


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


456 Mich. 590; 575 N.W.2d 751; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 1075


January 8, 1998, Argued  
March 24, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 217 Mich. App. 154; 550 N.W.2d 856 (1995) affirmed. 218 Mich. App. 342; 553 N.W.2d 700 (1996) affirmed.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county road commission challenged an order of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the decision of the trial court granting the motion of defendant Michigan Property and Casualty Guaranty Association for summary disposition pursuant to Mich. Ct. R. 2.116(C)(8), (10). The commission sought to recover under the Property and Casualty Guaranty Association Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.7901 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The Association was a statutorily created association of property and casualty insurers that had a duty to pay certain obligations of insolvent insurers that came within the Property and Casualty Guaranty Association Act. The county road commission maintained general liability insurance with a company that was deemed insolvent. The commission sought to recover from the Association for claims made against it. Recovery was denied. On appeal, the court determined that according to the plain language of the Act, the "net worth" exclusion applied to the commission as the insured and not to third-party claimants. The court found that the state was a person within the meaning of the Act as the state came within the ordinary and generally accepted meaning of legal entity. The court held that the commission, which was a public entity whose existence was solely dependent upon the legislation that created it, and whose claim was not based on any absolute right, could not assert an equal protection claim against its creator, the state. The court found that the "net worth" exclusion applied to both public and governmental entities and thus applied to the commission. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Spiek v. DOT


Docket No. 104096, No. 104096


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


456 Mich. 331; 572 N.W.2d 201; 1998 Mich. LEXIS 11


November 6, 1997, Argued  
January 21, 1998, Decided  
January 21, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Court of Claims, William E. Collette, J. Court of Appeals, HOOD, P.J., and SAWYER and E. J. GRANT, JJ. ( Docket No. 151606). Supreme Court, 453 Mich. 857 (1996) On reconsideration, 454 Mich. 905 (1997) 212 Mich App 565; 538 N.W.2d 74 (1995).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals reversed and the trial court's order granting defendant's motion for summary disposition reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Michigan Department of Transportation appealed a decision of Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed summary judgment granted pursuant to Mich. Ct. R. 2.116(C)(8) against plaintiff property owners in their claim for inverse condemnation.


OVERVIEW: The landowners alleged that they were entitled to compensation for damage to their property due to a service drive that caused noise, dust, vibrations, and fumes. The landowners argued that the damages constituted a taking of property that differed in degree from other property owners near highways. The instant court held that because the trial court did not review matters outside of the pleadings on summary judgment, the case was properly reviewed under Mich. Ct. R. 2.116(c)(8) rather than Mich. Ct. R. 2.116(c)(10). Under the common-law doctrine of absque injuria, property owners were not entitled to compensation for highway noise that is necessarily incident to proximity to a legalized nuisance such as a highway. Property owners affected in the same manner as others in proximity to the legalized nuisance were not entitled to recovery, even if they were affected to a greater degree. Property owners could only recover if they were affected in a unique or peculiar manner. Because the landowners' damage was the same kind suffered by all others in proximity to a highway and not unique, they were not entitled to just compensation.


OUTCOME: The instant court reversed the lower court's opinion and reinstated summary judgment against property owners.

People v. Gahan


No. 105515


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


456 Mich. 264; 571 N.W.2d 503; 1997 Mich. LEXIS 2893


October 8, 1997, Argued  
December 23, 1997, Decided  
December 23, 1997, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Kent Circuit Court, Dennis C. Kolenda, J. (Docket No. 172159). Court of Appeals, GRIFFIN and A. L. GARBRECHT, JJ. SMOLENSKI, P.J. (Docket No. 172159).
People v. Gahan, 1996 Mich. App. LEXIS 2395 (Mich. Ct. App., Jan. 16, 1996)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which ordered that defendant did not have to pay restitution to some victims, under the Crime Victim's Rights Act, because specific criminal acts committed against some of these victims were not the basis of defendant's conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered into consignment sales agreement with customers. Defendant would sell the vehicles for one price and tell the customers that he sold them for a lower price and he would keep the difference. Defendant was found guilty by a jury of embezzlement with regard to one of the consignment deals. The presentencing investigation report, Mich. Comp. Laws § 771.14 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1144), found that the state had 48 counts against defendant involving similar transactions. Defendant was ordered to pay restitution to all of the victims. The lower court vacated the order of restitution. The court held that the Crime Victim's Rights Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 780.766 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1287 (766)), provided a sentencing court with the authority to order such restitution and reinstated the trial court's order of restitution. The court noted that the statute clearly states restitution may be ordered with respect to any victim. The court reasoned defendant's repeated scheme to defraud his customers in the same or similar manner was within the broad meaning of "course of conduct" contained in the statute. The court also noted that the statute provided adequate process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower court and reinstated the order of restitution owed by defendant because the sentencing court did not exceed its statutory authority in ordering restitution intended to compensate victims who were defrauded by defendant's course of criminal conduct, even though these losses were not the specific factual predicate of defendant's conviction.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] (Durant) Court of Appeals, DANHOF, C.J., and CAVANAGH and D. R. FREEMAN, JJ. ( Docket No. 51431). On remand, the Court of Appeals, DANHOF, C.J., and CAVANAGH and MACKENZIE, JJ. ( Docket No. 63901). Special Master, Macomb Circuit Judge George R. Deneweth (second remand) Court of Appeals, DANHOF, C.J., and GILLIS and MACKENZIE, JJ. ( Docket No. 91271). Court of Appeals consolidated Durant and Schmidt (Docket Nos. 93547-93577, 93658, 125691) Court of Appeals, DOCTOROFF, C.J., and MACKENZIE and CORRIGAN, JJ., Wayne Circuit Judge James E. Mies as Special Master to succeed Judge Deneweth ( Docket No. 161321). 213 Mich. App. 500; 541 N.W.2d 278 (1995).



DISPOSITION: 213 Mich. App. 500 affirmed except as to remedy; remedy affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, state department of education, state department of management and budget, and state treasurer, appealed the judgment of the Michigan State Court of Appeals, which held in favor of plaintiff taxpayers and school districts. The taxpayers and school districts brought suit alleging violations of the Headlee Amendment, Mich. Const. art. 9, §§ 25 through 34.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers and school districts brought suits against the state department of education, state department of management and budget, and state treasurer. The cases were consolidated. The court resolved five questions relating to the Maintenance-of-Support Clause of Mich. Const. art. 9, § 29, part of the Headlee Amendment. The court held that special education and special education transportation were state-mandated activities or services within the meaning of art. 9, § 29. The "state match" payment for school lunches was part of the "state financed proportion" for the purpose of computing compliance with art. 9, § 29. Payments that were required of the state by art. 9, § 29 were funds constitutionally dedicated for specific purposes and exempt from executive order reduction under Mich. Const. art 5, § 20. The taxpayers' attorney fees in the case were part of the costs that could be recovered under Mich. Const. art. 9, § 32. The appropriate remedy for the violation of art. 9, § 29 was a money judgment for school districts for the full amount of underfunding for certain years, to use for refunds for taxpayers, tax relief, or other public purposes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals, except as to remedy. The remedy of the court of appeals was affirmed in part.

Michigan State AFL-CIO v. Civil Serv. Comm'n


Docket No. 102567.


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


455 Mich. 720; 566 N.W.2d 258; 1997 Mich. LEXIS 2185; 155 L.R.R.M. 3029


April 8, 1997, Argued  
July 31, 1997, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff unions appealed an order from a court of appeals (Michigan), which upheld a civil service commission rule that prohibited use of union leaves of absence for partisan political activity in the unions' action for preliminary and permanent injunctions as well as declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: The civil service commission rule prohibited political activity when a state employee was on actual duty. Under the rule, actual duty included leave time for union activities. The unions asserted that the civil service commission rule violated union members' rights under the Political Freedom Act (political freedom act), Mich. Comp. Laws § 15.401 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 4.1702(1) et seq.). Under the political freedom act a state employee was allowed to engage in partisan political activity except during those hours when he was being compensated for the performance of his duties as a public employee. The court held that the revised rule violated the political freedom act with regard to union leave. The two-prong test of (1) compensation of the union member for (2) the performance of his duties was not satisfied by union leave. Where the state was reimbursed by the union for the members' wages and fringe benefits the compensation prong was not satisfied. And, where the state paid fringe benefits during a leave, the "performance of duties as a public employee" was not satisfied. Union activity was not part of the duties of a public employee.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision upholding the validity of the civil service commission rule on political activity.

DISPOSITION: Remanded to the trial court for it to vacate its injunction and order the Department of Treasury to make payments to the public school academies that were operating under 1993 PA 362.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, educational organization and individuals, appealed from the order of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) that affirmed a trial court order, which declared that the Charter Schools Act, 1993 Mich. Pub. Acts 363, violated Mich. Const. art. VIII, §§ 2, 3 (1963) and enjoined the distribution of any public funds under the act.


OVERVIEW: After the legislature passed the Charter Schools Act, 1993 Mich. Pub. Acts 362, which provided for the creation of public school academies, plaintiffs, educational organization and individuals, brought an action to enjoin defendants, governor, treasurer, department of treasury, and department of education, from distributing public funds to these academies. The trial court declared that the statute violated Mich. Const. art. VIII, §§ 2, 3 (1963) and enjoined the distribution of public funds. The academies were public because the state was not required to retain exclusive control, the state exercised control over the application-approval process, and the state controlled the money. Under Mich. Const. art. VIII, § 3 (1962), the academies were also subject to defendant department of education's supervision to the same extent as were all other public schools. Further, the court held that the repealer in 1994 Mich. Pub. Acts 416 was valid and enforceable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order and ruled in favor of defendants, governor, treasurer, department of treasury, and department of education. The Charter School Act did not violated the Michigan Constitution. The case was remanded to the trial court with instructions that it vacate its injunction and order defendant department of treasury to make payments to the public school academies that were operating under the challenged act.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, People of the State of Michigan, challenged the decision of the Delta Circuit Court (Maryland), which reversed the trial court and held that the trial court erred when it refused to enter the defendants' guilty pleas.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with separate crimes and their appeals were consolidated. In both cases the trial court rejected the defendant's guilty plea. Rental Prop. Owners Ass'n v. City of Grand Rapids


No. 102642


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


455 Mich. 246; 566 N.W.2d 514; 1997 Mich. LEXIS 1586


November 13, 1996, Argued  
July 15, 1997, Decided  
July 15, 1997, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Kent Circuit Court, Michael R. Smolenski, J. Court of Appeals, NEFF, P.J., and SAWYER and MURPHY, JJ. (Docket No. 174452). 209 Mich. App. 391; 531 N.W.2d 731 (1995).



DISPOSITION: Decision of the Court of Appeals reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the order of a court of appeals (Michigan), which found a nuisance abatement ordinance enacted by the city to be unconstitutional, in an action brought by appellee landlord association.


OVERVIEW: The city, a home rule city, adopted an ordinance that authorized the city to declare a property a public nuisance if it was found to be used repeatedly for illegal drugs or prostitution and to padlock the property for up to one year. The landlord association sought a judgment declaring the ordinance to be unconstitutional, and filed a motion for summary judgment. The lower court denied the motion and the request for declaratory relief, finding that the ordinance provided an adequate hearing procedure. The court of appeals reversed the lower court's order, holding that the padlocking of property required the exercise of a court's equitable powers and that the ordinance violated the separation of powers clause of the Michigan Constitution. Further, the nuisance abatement statute did not intend to occupy the field of nuisance abatement and the ordinance did not directly conflict with the statute, therefore, the ordinance was not preempted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals and held that the ordinance was, on its face, constitutional and a valid exercise of municipal police power.

People v. Nix


No. 103072


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


453 Mich. 619; 556 N.W.2d 866; 1996 Mich. LEXIS 3156


October 8, 1996, Argued  
December 30, 1996, Decided  
December 30, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Oakland Circuit Court, Fred M. Mester, J. Court of Appeals, MICHAEL J. KELLY, P.J., and SHEPHERD and FITZGERALD, JJ. (Docket No. 143014). After remand, the Court of Appeals, TAYLOR, P.J., and GRIFFIN and W. G. SCHMA, JJ. (Docket No. 156242). 208 Mich. App. 648; 528 N.W.2d 208 (1995) affirmed.



DISPOSITION: Decision of the Court of Appeals affirmed and case remanded to the circuit court for entry of the order granting defendant's motion for directed verdict.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of an order from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed the defendant's conviction for felony murder as a double jeopardy violation.


OVERVIEW: The defendant's boyfriend kidnapped a woman and placed her in the trunk of her own car. The boyfriend and the defendant drove the car for six days. The kidnapped woman ultimately died. The defendant was tried for first-degree premeditated murder and felony murder. During the trial, the defendant filed a motion for a directed verdict, which was granted by the trial court, but immediately stayed so that the State could appeal the ruling. The appellate court found that the defendant could be retried for felony murder. The trial court empaneled a new jury and the defendant was convicted. The defendant sought review, and the appellate court reversed the conviction and held that it violated her right against double jeopardy. The State sought review. The court found that the trial court erred in staying entry of its order granting the directed verdict pending appeal, that such a device by the trial court could not be used as a means of avoiding the defendant's protection against double jeopardy, and that the directed verdict, based on the factual evidence presented at trial, barred any further proceedings relative to the charges brought against the defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision.

Michigan State AFL-CIO v. Employment Rels. Comm'n


No. 103918, Nos. 103937, 103938


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


453 Mich. 362; 551 N.W.2d 165; 1996 Mich. LEXIS 1929


April 11, 1996, Argued  
August 2, 1996, Decided  
August 2, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals decision upheld except with respect to 1994 PA 112, § 17.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated complaints against defendants, the governor of Michigan and the Michigan Employment Relations Commission, plaintiffs, associations of public school employees, alleged the unconstitutionality of several provisions of 1994 Mich. Pub. Acts 112 (the Act), which amended the Public Employment Relations Act (PERA). The circuit court upheld all but two provisions of the act; the court of appeals affirmed. The associations appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Act amended the PERA to address collective bargaining and strike activities by public school employees separately from other public employees. The circuit court invalidated two provisions of the Act. The associations claimed that other provisions of the Act violated freedom of association and equal protection by prohibiting statewide education associations from exercising a veto-like power over local bargaining units after the local negotiators reached a tentative collective bargaining agreement and by prohibiting collective bargaining over certain subjects. The governor and Commission claimed that the legislation was rationally related to the legitimate purpose of reducing strikes by public school employees. The court held that none of the provisions challenged on appeal violated constitutional rights. The court was divided on whether the provision regarding local bargaining units violated the First Amendment's protection of freedom of association. Three judges found it unconstitutional; one wrote that the issue was premature. Three judges found the provision constitutional if construed to permit education associations to advise local bargaining units.


OUTCOME: The court unanimously upheld the judgment of the court of appeals, except as to the Act's provision regarding prohibiting statewide education associations from exercising a veto-like power over local bargaining units. As a majority did not vote to overturn that provision, it remained effective, but the court of appeals' judgment that affirmed the circuit court's finding of constitutionality as to that provision was vacated.

People v. Mateo


No. 96079


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


453 Mich. 203; 551 N.W.2d 891; 1996 Mich. LEXIS 1921


October 12, 1995, Argued  
July 31, 1996, Decided  
July 31, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.



DISPOSITION: Defendant's conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with assault with intent to murder and felonious assault. After trial, defendant was convicted as charged. On review, the court of appeals (Michigan) affirmed. The court then granted leave to determine the standard of review on appeal of preserved error that did not involve a constitutional claim.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with assault with intent to murder, Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.83, felonious assault, Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.82, and as a second felony offender, Mich. Comp. Laws § 769.10, after defendant allegedly threatened the victim, who was temporarily residing in defendant's home, with a pistol and defendant slashed the victim. Defendant was convicted as charged and the appellate court agreed and found that any error in the admission of a witness' testimony was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant filed a delayed application for leave to appeal with the court, which the court granted limited to whether the trial court erred in permitting the witness to testify, what the appropriate standard for nonconstitutional error was, and whether any error in the admission required reversal. The court held that where the error asserted was the erroneous admission of evidence, the court engaged in a comparative analysis of the likely effect of the error in light of the other evidence. The evidence of defendant's guilt was overwhelming and the court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for assault with intent to murder and felonious assault. Although the trial court might have erred when it admitted testimony from a witness who testified that defendant was very violent, the court found that the error was nonconstitutional and that there was overwhelming evidence of defendant's guilt.

Quinton v. GMC


No. 100787


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


453 Mich. 63; 551 N.W.2d 677; 1996 Mich. LEXIS 1925


April 10, 1996, Argued  
July 30, 1996, Decided  
July 30, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.



DISPOSITION: The January, 1994, decision of the WCAC should be reversed, and the case remanded to the WCAC for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.354(17)-(20) was enacted to remedy erroneous judicial interpretation of § 354 (Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.354) of the Worker's Disability Compensation Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.101 et seq., and a magistrate ordered appellee employer to pay an employee, represented by appellant co-executors, all past coordinated benefits. The Michigan Worker's Compensation Appellate Commission (WCAC) reversed. The co-executors appealed.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to a hearing referee's open award of worker's compensation benefits to the employee, the employer reduced the benefits to reflect a coordination of the employee's after-tax pension benefit. The employer took the action in response to the enactment of § 418.354, which directed that worker's compensation benefits be reduced by the amount of certain retirement payments. The legislature responded to caselaw interpreting § 418.354 and indicated its disagreement with the interpretation. The legislature responded by enacting § 418.354(17)-(20), which directed that there be no coordination for persons like the employee in the instant case whose injury was before March 31, 1982. With respect to workers whose weekly benefits were held to be subject to coordination pursuant to the erroneously decided caselaw, the court held that the doctrine of separation of powers did not preclude application of the legislation that responded to the erroneous interpretation. Further, § 418.354(17)-(20) was applicable to the weekly benefits of all workers injured before March 31, 1982, without regard to whether there was a prior adjudication pursuant to the erroneous caselaw.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of WCAC and remanded to the WCAC for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner appealed from a judgment of the appellate court, which reversed the decision of the Oakland Circuit Court (Michigan), which had concluded that defendant city's zoning ordinance had effected an unconstitutional taking of landowner's property.


OVERVIEW: Landowner claimed that city's zoning ordinance as applied to its property effected an unconstitutional taking. The court stated that landowner might have been eligible for alternative relief from the provisions of the ordinance had it petitioned for a land use variance.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court, which reversed the judgment of the trial court in favor of landowner, was affirmed.

People v. Adair


No. 101286


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


452 Mich. 473; 550 N.W.2d 505; 1996 Mich. LEXIS 1637


March 6, 1996, Argued (Calendar No. 12)  
July 16, 1996, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the court of appeals (Michigan) denying a portion of the trial court order in a sexual assault case allowing defendant under the rape-shield statute, Mich. Comp. Laws 750.520j(1)(a); Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.788(10)(1)(a), to introduce evidence of the complainant's sexual conduct with him within 30 days of the alleged assault.


OVERVIEW: The defendant's wife complained that he sexually assaulted her while they lived together pending their divorce. Defendant sought to introduce evidence of specific instances of his wife's sexual conduct with him both before and after the alleged assault. The trial court limited evidence of subsequent consensual sexual relations to those occurring within 30 days of the assault. The court of appeals vacated that part of the order. The Michigan Supreme Court reversed. The Court held that "past" conduct referred to conduct that occurred before the evidence was offered at trial. The Court further held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in precluding defendant from introducing evidence of prior consensual digital-anal sexual activity, given its highly prejudicial nature and its nonexistent probative value. The evidence had a fair amount of probative value, but the danger of misleading the jury was significant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the court of appeals' decision.

Brown v. Manistee County Rd. Comm'n


No. 99566


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


452 Mich. 354; 550 N.W.2d 215; 1996 Mich. LEXIS 1672


December 6, 1995, Argued  
July 16, 1996, Decided  
July 16, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff motorcyclist, who was injured when he lost control of his motorcycle trying to avert a pothole, sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which upheld a trial court grant of summary judgment in favor of defendant county road commission because the motorcyclist had not complied with the 60 day notice requirement of Mich. Comp. Laws § 224.


OVERVIEW: The motorcyclist filed a negligence suit against the commission. The appellate court upheld the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of the commission. The motorcyclist appealed. The court reversed the appellate court and held that: (1) there was an exception to the government's immunity from tort liability under Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.1402 for failure to maintain state roads, and under Mich. Comp. Laws § 224.21 for county roads; (2) the two statutes were governed by two different notice periods; 60 days for county commissions under Mich. Comp. Laws § 224.21, and 120 days for state entities, including county road commissions, under Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.1404; (3) the two different notice periods were suspect under the Equal Protection Clause because they governed identical causes of action; (4) the 60 day notice period under Mich. Comp. Laws § 224.21 was unconstitutional; (5) the motorcyclist's suit was governed by the 120 days notice provision; (6) the commission was not prejudiced by the motorcyclist's failure to serve notice within 120 days because the road had been repaved before the expiration of the notice period; and (7) a prior court opinion was reaffirmed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the decision of the appellate court, which held that a 60 day notice requirement applied to lawsuits against the commission and that the commission had been prejudiced by the motorcyclist's failure to comply with the notice provision.

Airlines Parking v. Wayne County


No. 98890


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


452 Mich. 527; 550 N.W.2d 490; 1996 Mich. LEXIS 1661


April 11, 1996, Argued  
July 16, 1996, Decided  
July 16, 1996, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff airline parking corporation appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which granted summary judgment for defendants, the city, county, and state, in the action concerning the nature of the Airport Parking Tax Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 207.371 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 7.559(101) et seq.)


OVERVIEW: The airline parking corporation brought an original action in the court of appeals pursuant to Mich. Const. art IX, § 32 against the city, the county, and the state. The airline parking corporation argued that the airport parking tax was a "local tax" subject to § 31of the Headlee Amendment, Mich. Const. art. IX, §§ 25-34 and was therefore subject to prior approval by a majority of local voters. The court held that the tax was a state tax, had the structural attributes of a state tax, served a state purpose, was enacted by the legislature and was administered by the state. The court further held that the crucial inquiry, for purposes of analyzing tax limitations, was which entity was responsible for levying the tax not the purposes for which the tax was levied. The court further concluded that although the county was the only county in Michigan that had an airport meeting the statutory definition, the statutory language did not restrict application of the tax to one locale as would the language of a local tax act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals which granted the city, the county, and the state summary judgment..

People v. Lardie


No. 101640, No. 102742


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


452 Mich. 231; 551 N.W.2d 656; 1996 Mich. LEXIS 1629


March 5, 1996, Argued  
July 9, 1996, Decided  
July 9, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The Court of Appeals decisions in Lardie and Hudick affirmed..


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of their felony convictions and sentencing by the Court of Appeals (Michigan) under Mich. Comp. Laws § 257.625(4) for driving while intoxicated and for operation of a motor vehicle, causing the death of another.


OVERVIEW: Defendants sought review of their felony convictions and sentencing of up to a maximum of 15 years by the trial court under Mich. Comp. Laws § 257.625(4) for driving while intoxicated and by operation of a motor vehicle, causing the death of another. The court affirmed the convictions and sentencing, holding that the statute in question did not violate defendants' due process rights and that the statute was constitutional. The court found that the statute in question was similar to common-law involuntary manslaughter, except that it eliminated the people's need to prove gross negligence. By doing so, the legislature instead required that the people prove that defendants had a general intent to commit the wrongful act of drinking and driving. Thus, the statute did not impose strict liability. Moreover, the statute required that the culpable mental state, the mens rea, have a causal relation to the harm that the statute sought to prevent. The court, therefore, concluded that the statute did not violate defendants' right of due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions and sentences for driving while intoxicated and for operation of a motor vehicle, causing the death of another, under a statute, which made the alleged crime a felony with a maximum sentence of up to 15 years. The statute did not impose strict liability and required that the mens rea had causal relation to the harm that the statute sought to prevent.

Frame v. Nehls


No. 102139


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


452 Mich. 171; 550 N.W.2d 739; 1996 Mich. LEXIS 1623


November 7, 1995, Argued  
July 3, 1996, Decided  
July 3, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] We reverse the decision of the Court of Appeals, affirm the grant of summary disposition, and remand this case to the trial court for entry of judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of an order from a Michigan Court of Appeals, which held that the grandparent visitation provisions of the Child Custody Act (Act), Mich. Comp. Laws § 722.27b, Mich. Stat. Ann. § 25312(7b), violated the Equal Protection Clauses of the United States and Michigan Constitutions, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, Mich. Const. art. 1, § 2.


OVERVIEW: The mother had a child out of wedlock. When the relationship ended, the mother filed a complaint to determine paternity. The trial court entered an order of filiation, and awarded custody of the child to the mother. The father was awarded regular visitation. The child's paternal grandfather petitioned the trial court for visitation. The trial court ruled that an action for determination of paternity was not a child custody dispute under the Act and granted summary judgment in favor of the mother. The court of appeals reversed the trial court's ruling on the ground that the Act violated the Equal Protection Clauses under the federal and state constitutionn, U.S. Const., amend. XIV, Mich. Const. art 1, § 2. The court ruled that a paternity dispute was not a child custody dispute and that the grandfather was not authorized to seek visitation under the Act because the child's father was alive. The court also ruled that the Act did not distinguish between children born in wedlock from those born out of wedlock and that the classification did not involve a suspect class or a fundamental right.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the Court of Appeals and affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of the mother. The court ruled that the classifications created by the Act did not involve a suspect class and that there was a rational basis for limiting the situations in which a grandparent could seek visitation.

Feaster v. Portage Pub. Sch.


No. 103255


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


451 Mich. 351; 547 N.W.2d 328; 1996 Mich. LEXIS 1002



May 14, 1996, Decided  
May 14, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended November 21, 1996.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of the Court of Appeals and the circuit court reversed, and case remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff student sought review from a decision of the appellate court (Michigan) that affirmed the order entered in the trial court that granted summary judgment in favor of defendant school district. The school district refused to allow the student to continue enrollment in the school district on the basis that the student was not a resident of the school district.


OVERVIEW: The student argued that the school district's policy violated the language of Mich. Comp. Laws § 380.1148; (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 15.41148) which, provided that, if the student met the requirements for residency within the school district, admission was required. The court agreed with the student and reversed the appellate court and the trial court decisions. The court noted that educational residency was not the equivalent of legal domicile. It also noted that the state's school laws were to be liberally construed consistent with the state's public policy of encouraging free public education. The court then pointed out that the school code did not require the appointment of a legal guardian as a condition of educational residency and that, the school district's rule requiring a court appointed guardian, effectively deleted one of the statutory definitions of educational residency. The court concluded that although the school district did not want to be forced to make a factual determination regarding the purpose of the placement of a child in the home of a relative, it did not have the authority to rewrite the statutory requirements for educational residency.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments of the appellate court, which affirmed the trial court's order that granted summary judgment to the school district in the student's action, and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.
