Taxpayers Allied for Constitutional Taxation v. Wayne County


No. 98987


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


450 Mich. 119; 537 N.W.2d 596; 1995 Mich. LEXIS 1811


April 6, 1995, Argued  
August 22, 1995, Decided  
August 22, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 203 Mich. App. 537; 513 N.W.2d 202 (1994).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed the decision from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the trial court's decision to grant defendant county's motion for summary disposition on the basis that the limitations period had been violated. The taxpayer filed suit contending that an increase in the real property transfer tax rate was unconstitutional, and he sought an injunction, a refund, and declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: Wayne County raised the tax rate without voter approval. Section 31 of what was popularly known as the Headlee Amendment, Mich. Const. 1963, art. IX, § 31, stated that any increase had to be voter approved. Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.308a(3) had a one-year limitations period. On appeal, the court found that the lower courts ruled incorrectly. The one-year statute of limitations was constitutional. However, it was not violated because a cause of action for a refund of a real property transfer tax accrued at the time the tax was due and paid, not the time the new tax went into effect. The wrong giving rise to right to a refund had not yet occurred. Injunctive relief was available to prevent a future imposition of the tax. The limitations period did not bar declaratory relief for future claims. However, it did bar relief for taxes already paid more than one year prior to the suit.


OUTCOME: The summary dismissal of the taxpayer's suit on a time limitations issue was reversed and remanded. The cause of action accrued when the tax was due and paid, not when the new tax went into effect. Injunctive relief was available. Declaratory relief was available except for a claim based on a tax actually paid more than one year before the suit was filed.

 

42 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Reed


No. 96936


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


449 Mich. 375; 535 N.W.2d 496; 1995 Mich. LEXIS 1469


November 1, 1994, Argued  
July 25, 1995, Decided  
July 25, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 198 Mich. App. 639; 499 N.W.2d 441 (1993).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of felony murder. The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed defendant's conviction. Defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted defendant of felony murder and the lower appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that defendant's trial counsel was not ineffective in failing to object on the issue of intent because, at the time of his trial, the relevant case law had not been decided yet. Further, read as a whole, the prosecutor's use of "we know" did not show an attempt to place the credibility of his office behind the case or a suggestion that he possessed extrajudicial information on which the defendant should be convicted. Rather, the prosecutor asserted the "we know" on the basis of the evidence presented at trial and inferences drawn therefrom. Also, objecting could have invited an overruling by the trial judge and risked jury disapproval. The trial counsel's failure to object was a quintessential example of trial strategy. Thus, trial counsel's performance was constitutionally adequate and defendant's counsel on direct appeal did not render ineffective assistance by failing to raise the issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant for felony murder.

Kivela v. Dep't of Treasury


No. 97196


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


449 Mich. 220; 536 N.W.2d 498; 1995 Mich. LEXIS 1470


March 7, 1995, Argued  
July 18, 1995, Decided  
July 18, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 200 Mich. App. 545; 505 N.W.2d 11 (1993).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state department of treasury sought review of the judgment of a Michigan court of appeals, which reversed a tax tribunal's summary judgment for the treasury and found that evidence seized from appellee taxpayer in an improper police search could not be used in a separate and independent civil jeopardy tax assessment proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Financial records relating to narcotic sales that were seized by police in an illegal search of the taxpayer's home were turned over to the treasury, which then issued a jeopardy tax assessment based on the drug sales. The treasury sought review of the appellate court's holding that the unlawfully seized records could not be used in the civil tax case against the taxpayer. The court agreed with the treasury and reversed the ruling. The court noted that the federal law did not apply the exclusionary rule to the use of financial records obtained through invalid warrants as evidence in civil tax assessment proceedings. The court then pointed out that Mich. Const. art. 1, § 11, did not provide greater protection than the Fourth Amendment to the federal constitution, absent a compelling reason to apply a different interpretation. The court found no such compelling reason in the taxpayer's arguments and held that, barring a showing by the taxpayer that there was bad faith, collusion between agencies, or unethical behavior on the part of the law enforcement agents, the exclusionary rule did not apply to civil tax proceedings that were independent of criminal prosecutions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court and reinstated the order of the tax tribunal that the exclusionary rule did not protect illegally seized financial records from being used in independent civil tax assessment proceedings. The court held that the exclusionary rule would not apply in the absence of evidence of bad faith, collusion between the agencies, or unethical behavior on the part of law enforcement agents.

 

44 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Weems v. Chrysler Corp.


No. 97725, No. 97736, No. 97737


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


448 Mich. 679; 533 N.W.2d 287; 1995 Mich. LEXIS 844


December 7, 1994, Argued  
May 31, 1995, Decided  
May 31, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 201 Mich. App. 309; 505 N.W.2d 905 (1993).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, plaintiff claimants, surviving spouses, appealed the orders of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the worker's compensation decision that the claimants were partially dependant upon the deceased employees of defendant employer, and only entitled to the minimum benefits under Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.321 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(321)), and Mich. Comp. Laws § 428.356(2) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(356)(2)).


OVERVIEW: The employees were killed in the course of their employment with employer. Because the claimants regularly contributed to the household income, the determination was that they were only partially dependent upon the employees, which subjected them to the Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.321 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(321)) benefit reduction. The appeals court upheld the determination but found that the claimants were subject to the minimum weekly benefits. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The claimants' earnings were sufficient to render them only partially dependent upon the employees, because they were regular and substantial. However, the court reversed the benefit calculations. The court the determined the correct formula was the deceased employee's annual after-tax earnings divided by the total relevant annual family income, which was then multiplied by 80 percent and multiplied by the deceased employee's after-tax weekly wages. The resulting percentage was then multiplied by the amount a wholly dependent person would receive. The formula incorporated the maximum and minimum rates of compensation, therefore it was error to apply the limitations again.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court that the claimant was partially dependant upon the employee, but reversed the benefit calculation and adopted a new formula for calculation of death benefits for partially dependent claimants, and reversed the determination that a partially dependent person's weekly benefit was directly subject to the maximum and minimum rates of compensation.

People v. Cervantes


No. 98917


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


448 Mich. 620; 532 N.W.2d 831; 1995 Mich. LEXIS 840


January 11, 1995, Argued  
May 17, 1995, Decided  
May 17, 1995, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed defendant's conviction for breaking and entering but remanded the enhanced sentences imposed on defendant for breaking and entering and a felonious assault conviction on the ground that the trial court's sentences, which exceeded state sentencing guidelines limits, violated the principle of proportionality.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of breaking and entering with intent to commit larceny and felonious assault. The trial court sentenced defendant as a habitual offender to 10 to 20 years for breaking and entering and 4 to 8 years for felonious assault. Defendant appealed only the breaking and entering conviction and the enhanced sentences imposed for breaking and entering and felonious assault. An intermediate court affirmed the conviction and remanded for resentencing. The State appealed. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the trial judge did not abuse his discretion in sentencing because defendant's past crimes had escalated in severity and were such that he had established his inability to be rehabilitated. The sentencing guidelines did not apply to the sentencing of habitual offenders. The intermediate court erred in trying to create a mathematical formula to determine whether the sentence imposed on defendant was proper. The trial judge was correct in considering defendant's extensive criminal history and his potential for rehabilitation because, under the indeterminate sentencing framework, sentences were based more on an assessment of the offender than the offense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the reversal of the trial court's sentencing of defendant to enhanced sentences as a habitual offender for breaking and entering with intent to commit larceny and felonious assault.

 

46 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Musselman v. Governor of Michigan


Nos. 97915, 97322


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


448 Mich. 503; 533 N.W.2d 237; 1995 Mich. LEXIS 841


November 1, 1994, Argued  
April 25, 1995, Decided  
April 25, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Granted June 19, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Mich. LEXIS 1246. Opinion on Rehearing February 12, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Mich. LEXIS 291.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, school employees, sought review of a Michigan Court of Appeals, which denied plaintiffs' petition for mandamus and ruled that it was without authority to order the relief requested by plaintiffs in an action against defendants, governor, legislature, and associated individuals.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, school employees, received various health care benefits. Defendant governor issued an executive order that reduced the appropriation to plaintiffs requirement system by $ 54 million by amending the controlling statute. The appellate court denied plaintiffs' petition for mandamus in its action against defendants, governor, legislature, and associated individuals. The court held that the state was obligated to refund health care benefits under Mich. Const. art. 9, § 24. However, because the court had no authority to order defendants, governor and legislature, to appropriate funds, mandamus was denied. Given that plaintiffs failed to show that there was a pool of funds available to be transferred to the reserve for health benefits, the requested relief necessarily involved funds from the state treasury. The only defendant with authority to appropriate funds from the treasury was defendant legislature. In that context, the court lacked the power to require defendant legislature to appropriate funds.


OUTCOME: The court found in favor of defendants, governor, legislature, and associated individuals, and denied plaintiffs, school employees, requested writ of mandamus because the court had no authority to order defendants, governor or legislature, to appropriate funds.

 

47 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Chrysler Corp. v. Skyline Indus. Servs.


No. 97396


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


448 Mich. 113; 528 N.W.2d 698; 1995 Mich. LEXIS 156


December 7, 1994, Argued  
March 7, 1995, Decided  
March 7, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 199 Mich. App. 366; 502 N.W.2d 715 (1993).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which held that the indemnification clause holding plaintiff harmless for its own negligence in a construction contract with defendant corporation was unenforceable because Illinois law, not Michigan law, applied.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporation brought an action against defendant corporation for breach of contract and indemnification. The trial court granted plaintiff's motion for summary disposition on the issue of indemnification, holding that Michigan law was applicable and that the provision holding plaintiff harmless from its own negligence was valid. The appellate court reversed, holding that Illinois law was applicable and that the indemnification clause was unenforceable, and plaintiff appealed. The court held that in making choice of law decisions, the concern was to balance the expectations of the parties to the contract with the interests of the states involved. The court held that the appellate court erred in applying Illinois law, that Michigan law applied, and that the indemnification clause was valid because the parties chose Michigan law to govern their contractual relationship and there was no compelling evidence that Illinois had a materially greater interest in the issue than Michigan. The judgment of the appellate court was reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded, holding that the appellate court erred in applying Illinois law to plaintiff corporation's indemnification action against defendant corporation. The court held that Michigan law, which the parties chose to govern their construction contract, applied, absent compelling evidence that Illinois had a materially greater interest, and that the indemnification provision was valid under Michigan law.

People ex rel. Wayne County Prosecutor v. Bennis


No. 97339


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


447 Mich. 719; 527 N.W.2d 483; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 3029


October 5, 1994, Argued  
December 30, 1994, Decided  
December 29, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 200 Mich. App. 670; 504 N.W.2d 731 (1993).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of gross indecency. The State sought to abate defendant's vehicle as a public nuisance pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.3801 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 27A.3801). The trial judge held the vehicle was a nuisance and it was abated. The United States Court of Appeals reversed, and the State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's vehicle, which the prosecutor sought to abate after defendant's conviction for gross indecency, was co-owned by defendant's wife, who had no knowledge that her husband used their vehicle in violation of the nuisance abatement statute. The court held that lewdness, incidental to an act of prostitution, was an activity squarely within the purview of the nuisance abatement statute. Alternatively, the court concluded that proof of an exchange of money was not necessary where, as here, it was clear from the totality of the circumstances that the sexual act was in exchange for payment. Additionally, the court upheld the abatement of this vehicle because defendant entered a neighborhood that was a known place for prostitution and used his vehicle to engage in illicit activity, thereby contributing to the existing nuisance. Finally, pursuant to the clear and unambiguous language of the statute, the court held that knowledge or consent was not required to abate the interest of a co-owner.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals.

Fun 'N Sun RV v. State (In re Certified Question)


No. 99562


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


447 Mich. 765; 527 N.W.2d 468; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 3025


November 1, 1994, Argued  
December 30, 1994, Decided  
December 30, 1994, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to the authority of Mich. Ct. R. 7.305(A), the Governor of Michigan requested that the court determine the constitutionality of Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.701a (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(701a)), which made consideration from the sale of the State Accident Fund the property of the state rather than permitting any surplus or profit to be distributed to the policyholders.


OVERVIEW: Two policyholders filed an action against the State that sought a declaration that all of the policyholders of the State Accident Fund (fund) were owners of any surplus accumulated by the fund and were entitled to distribution of the surplus or profit from the sale of the fund by the State. While that action was pending, §701A of the Worker's Disability Compensation Act (WDCA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.101 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(101) et seq.), was enacted which made the profits from the sale of the fund the property of the state. The Governor then issued an executive message in which he requested that the court determine the constitutionality of Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.701a (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(701a)). The court found the statute constitutional and held that there was no explicit expression by the Legislature that the excess premiums would be refunded to the policyholders. The policyholders had a vested contractual right to liability coverage, but they failed to show that their contractual right was impaired by operation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.701a (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(701a)).


OUTCOME: The court determined that the statute in question did not violate either the state or federal constitution.

People v. Lino


No. 92352, No. 95687


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


447 Mich. 567; 527 N.W.2d 434; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 3026


December 1, 1993, Argued  
December 28, 1994, Decided  
December 28, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 190 Mich. App. 715; 476 N.W.2d 654 (1991), 197 Mich. App. 672; 496 N.W.2d 385 (1992).



DISPOSITION: Lino, reversed. Brashier, reversed in part, affirmed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State appealed a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed the conviction of defendant transvestite for gross indecency between males under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.338. State appealed another judgment, which reversed defendant adult's motion to quash informations for procuring sexual conduct with a person under the age of consent under the statute.


OVERVIEW: Defendant transvestite was convicted of gross indecency between males under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.338. Defendant adult moved to quash informations for procuring or attempting to procure sexual conduct with a person under the age of consent under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.338.When the appellate court reversed defendant transvestite's conviction and the denial of defendant adult's motion, state appealed. The court reversed for defendant transvestite and affirmed conviction. Fellatio performed in a public place clearly fell within the ambit of a gross indecency statute, Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.338. The court reversed for defendant adult in part and remanded for trial. Procuring or attempting to procure the sexual conduct with a person under the age of consent could support a conviction under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.338, regardless of whether the conduct was performed in public. Defendant transvestite's vagueness challenge failed. Defendant transvestite had fair notice that public fellatio between males was prohibited by the statute. The statute did not create a risk of arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed for defendant transvestite and affirmed conviction because fellatio performed in a public place clearly fell under a gross indecency statute. The court reversed for defendant adult in part and remanded for trial because procuring or attempting to procure sexual conduct with a person under the age of consent could support a conviction under the indecency law.

People v. Kevorkian


No. 99591, No. 99674, No. 99752, No. 99758, No. 99759

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 205 Mich. App. 180; 517 N.W.2d 293 (1994). 205 Mich. App. 194; 518 N.W.2d 487 (1994). People v Roberts, 211 Mich 187, 178 N.W. 690 (1920).

One defendant filed a cross-appeal with regard to a federal constitutional issue.


OVERVIEW: The prosecution appealed the court of appeals' determination that an assisted suicide statute under which defendants were charged was enacted in violation of Mich. Const. 1963, art. IV, § 24. The court held (1) the assisted suicide statute embraced only one object and was thus validly enacted, (2) the statute was not enacted in violation of the Change in Purpose clause of the state constitution, (3) the United States Supreme Court would not find a liberty interest in suicide, let alone assisted suicide, that was protected by the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend. OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals with regard to the claimed state constitutional violation and remanded the case to the circuit court.

People v. Vaughn


No. 97279


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


447 Mich. 217; 524 N.W.2d 217; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 2406


April 7, 1994, Argued  
August 31, 1994, Decided  
August 31, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 200 Mich App 611; 505 NW2d 41 (1993) 



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) which reversed defendant's conviction for kidnapping based on the trial court erring in failing to adequately instruct the jury that the asportation necessary for kidnapping was not be merely incidental to commission of the underlying offense of first-degree criminal sexual conduct.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree criminal sexual conduct and of the separate offence of kidnapping. The court of appeals reversed defendant's separate kidnapping conviction because it deemed the trial court's jury instruction insufficient to convey the essential point that the asportation necessary for kidnapping could not be merely movement incidental to commission of the criminal sexual conduct. The failure to adequately inform the jury about an essential element of kidnapping, the court of appeals reasoned, resulted in manifest injustice and required reversal. The court concurred that the jury instructions given were erroneous insofar as they failed to adequately explain that forcible-confinement kidnapping demands asportation incidental to the kidnapping, and not movement merely incidental to commission of the underlying offense of first-degree criminal sexual conduct. The court, however, reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and reinstated the jury conviction because the jury, properly instructed, could not have reached a different verdict had the error not occurred given the nature and duration of complainant's forcible confinement in defendant's automobile.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and reinstated the jury verdict convicting defendant of kidnapping. The court determined that, although the jury instructions were erroneous in failing to adequately explain that kidnapping demands asportation incidental to the kidnapping, the error was harmless, because the jury, even if properly instructed, could not have found the asportation incidental to the criminal sexual conduct.

 

54 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pegenau


No. 96696


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


447 Mich. 278; 523 N.W.2d 325; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 2405


April 6, 1994, Argued  
August 31, 1994, Decided  
August 31, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 199 Mich App 161; 501 NW2d 211 (1993) 



DISPOSITION: Reversed and reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of unlawful possession of Valium and Xanax in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 333.7403(2)(b); Mich. Stat. Ann. § 14.15(7403)(2)(b). The Michigan Court of Appeals reversed the conviction on the grounds that the prosecution should have been required to prove absence of a prescription beyond a reasonable doubt and because defendant was denied a fair trial. The prosecution appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the pills had been prescribed by doctors, but conceded that he was unable to remember the names of any of his treating physicians or to produce a written prescription or other documentation. The appellate court held that because defendant had met his initial burden by coming forward with some evidence of a prescription, the prosecution should have been required to prove absence of a prescription beyond a reasonable doubt. On appeal, the court held that Mich. Comp. Laws § 333.7531; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 14.15(7531) did not violate due process because it required defendant to bear the burden of proof regarding presence of a prescription, which was an affirmative defense rather than an element of the crime. The court further held that the burden placed on defendant was one of production rather than persuasion, that his bare assertion of a prescription was insufficient to meet the burden, and that his witnesses' testimony was not competent evidence of a prescription. Defendant was not prejudiced by the trial court's failure to instruct the jury regarding § 7531 because it was instructed that the prosecution bore the burden of proving lack of a prescription.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's decision and reinstated defendant's conviction.

People v. Stanaway


No. 92269, No. 96823


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


446 Mich. 643; 521 N.W.2d 557; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 2047


January 13, 1994, Argued  
August 29, 1994, Decided  
August 29, 1994, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Stanaway, reversed and remanded. Caruso, vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted of sexual offenses. On appeal from the court of appeals (Michigan), defendants challenged privileges under Mich. Comp. Laws §§ 330.1750, 600.2157a(2), 339.1610, and 722.826 - 722.829, which prevented them from obtaining pre-trial access to records of a psychologist, a sexual assault counselor, a social worker, or a juvenile diversion officer regarding a witness.


OVERVIEW: In consolidated appeals, defendant challenged various privilege laws, which prevented them from obtaining records concerning witnesses. The court upheld the general purposes of the statutory privileges to prevent routine disclosures that would undermine therapeutic relationships, but recognized that in certain circumstances an in camera review of the records was necessary so as not to undermine confidence in the outcome of a trial. Remand of one case was ordered because defendant demonstrated a good faith belief, grounded in articulable fact, that there was a reasonable probability that the privileged records contained information that was material and favorable necessary to his defense. In the second case, it was an abuse of discretion requiring reversal for the trial court, despite defense objections, to allow the improper impeachment of a prosecution witness with hearsay testimony that was highly prejudicial.


OUTCOME: Both cases were remanded. One conviction was reversed, because there was trial error in allowing improper impeachment by prejudicial hearsay testimony, and the other vacated, because there was a realistic and substantial possibility that the privileged material requested might contain information necessary to the defense.

 

56 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Kosiel v. Arrow Liquors Corp.


No. 96356


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


446 Mich. 374; 521 N.W.2d 531; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 2030


January 12, 1994, Argued  
August 26, 1994, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee received worker's compensation for an injury she received while she was working for defendant employer. An arbitrator modified the original award, and the rate of compensation paid to the employee's spouse for nursing care was increased. The employer and its insurer appealed to the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed the increase in compensation. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court reviewed the administrative proceeding, which led to the employee's original award of worker's compensation. The court determined that the referee's decision that the employee was totally and permanently disabled, which was affirmed by the Worker's Compensation Appeal Board, was a final order. The doctrine of res judicata precluded a redetermination of the extent of the employee's disability absent a showing of change in the employee's physical condition. However, as to the amount to be paid for nursing care, the referee placed a temporal limitation on the applicability of the amount. The employee's spouse was to be paid $ 5 per day until further order of the department. This was not a final determination of the value of the nursing services provided by the employee's spouse; rather, the referee contemplated the need to adjust the amount at some point in the future. The rate of compensation, including the reasonable costs of medical services and nursing care were subject to fluctuation depending on economic circumstances. Thus, the referee's order, which allowed for future redetermination of the reasonable value of necessary nursing services, was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the appellate court's reversal of the increase granted to the employee's spouse for nursing care. The referee's order was not final as to the rate of compensation to the employee's spouse for the nursing care that he provided to her. Thus, the doctrine of res judicata did not apply.

Peterman v. Department of Natural Resources


No. 95972


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


446 Mich. 177; 521 N.W.2d 499; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 2045


March 8, 1994, Argued  
August 23, 1994, Decided  
August 23, 1994, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners filed an action against appellee Michigan Department of Natural Resources (DNR), alleging intentional trespass, intentional damage, intentional interference with use of their property, nuisance, and a taking without just compensation. The Michigan Court of Appeals reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of the property owners. The property owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: The property owners contended that the DNR's construction of a boat launch ramp on adjacent property resulted in the loss of their beach as well as the erosion of their fast land, or property above the highwater mark. Further, the property owners alleged that the DNR's actions constituted an unconstitutional taking of property without due process of law and without just compensation in violation of Mich. Const. art. X, § 2. The court held that while compensation was usually not necessary for the erosion of beach under the highwater mark caused by navigational improvements by the State, compensation was due for the destruction of the property owners' fast lands. Moreover, because the DNR's unscientific construction of the boat launch unnecessarily caused the destruction of the property owners' beach, the court determined that compensation was required for the loss of the beach. The court, however, held that the filtration of sand from the bay, which led to the erosion of fast land, did not fall within the trespass-nuisance exception to sovereign immunity. Therefore, the DNR's actions did not constitute a compensable trespass-nuisance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment with regard to the eminent domain action and reinstated the damages awarded by the trial court. The court affirmed the judgment with regard to the trespass action.

Middlebrooks v. Wayne County


Nos. 96078, 96086, 96090


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


446 Mich. 151; 521 N.W.2d 774; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 2034; 9 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1513


December 3, 1993, Argued  
August 23, 1994, Decided  
August 23, 1994, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff worker filed an action alleging violations of his rights under the Fourth Amendment and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. Summary disposition was granted to defendants, county, clinic, and laboratory. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed on the § 1983 and Fourth Amendment claims against the county. It remanded to determine whether the worker stated such claims against the clinic and the laboratory, who, along with the county, filed an appeal.


OVERVIEW: The worker applied for a permanent position as a general service laborer with the county. Pursuant to a preemployment physical examination, the worker's urinalysis test was positive for opiates and cocaine. The county determined the worker was not qualified for the position sought, which involved the operation of lawn mowers and other mechanical equipment near roads. The worker claimed that the urinalysis test violated his Fourth Amendment right to be free from unreasonable search and seizure. The lower court concluded that the urinalysis test was unreasonable as a matter of law. The court reversed the decision. The worker had a reduced expectation of privacy as a result of his application for a position with the county that involved duties fraught with such risks of injury to others that even a momentary lapse of attention could have had disastrous results. There was no evidence that the county failed to provide notice to the worker of the urinalysis testing. Nor did the evidence indicate that the clinic unduly intruded on the worker's privacy or that the laboratory employed unreliable methods in its analysis. The testing in the instant action did not violate the Fourth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court on the summary disposition.

Jennings v. Southwood


Nos. 96277, 96330


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


446 Mich. 125; 521 N.W.2d 230; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 2042


March 9, 1994, Argued  
August 2, 1994, Decided  
August 2, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 198 Mich. App. 713; 499 N.W.2d 460 (1993) vacated.



DISPOSITION: VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted applications for leave to appeal in the two cases and consolidated them. In the first case, plaintiff personal representative sought review of the decision of the Michigan Court of Appeals, which affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendant emergency medical service provider. In the second case, both parties sought review of a portion of the decision of the Michigan Court of Appeals.


OVERVIEW: In two consolidated cases, the court evaluated whether the common law definitions of gross negligence and wilful and wanton misconduct remained viable against the backdrop of the emergency medical services act (EMSA), Mich. Comp. Laws 333.20901 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann § 14.15(20901) et seq.). The court held that it was appropriate to use the definition of gross negligence as found in § 7 of the government tort liability act (GTLA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.1401 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.996(101) et seq.) as the standard for gross negligence under EMSA. Because the provisions of the GTLA and EMSA shared the common purpose of immunizing certain agents from ordinary negligence and permitting liability for gross negligence, the provisions were to be read in pari materia. The court further held that the estate representatives, who alleged wilful misconduct under the EMSA, had to allege that the people providing emergency medical services intended to do harm. The court applied these holdings to the consolidated cases before it.


OUTCOME: In the first case, the court vacated the trial court's original order and remanded the case as to gross negligence. The court held that the grant of summary disposition as to wilful misconduct was proper. In the second case, the court affirmed the court of appeals' holding that the dismissal of defendant municipality on grounds of governmental immunity was erroneous. The court remanded for consideration of whether certain errors were harmless.

 

60 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Carpentier


No. 97268


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


446 Mich. 19; 521 N.W.2d 195; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 2048


April 6, 1994, Argued  
August 2, 1994, Decided  
August 2, 1994, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which ordered defendant's case remanded for consideration of defendant's collateral challenge of a juvenile adjudication used to determine the instant sentence for armed robbery pursuant to Mich. Ct. R. 6.508. The Court of Appeals concluded that defendant had alleged a jurisdictional defect, thereby removing the need for the "good cause" requirement of Rule 6.508.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged that he should be resentenced because the circuit court considered juvenile adjudications that were constitutionally infirm to determine his sentence, in that he was not represented by counsel and did not waive the right to representation. The court determined that the "good cause" and "actual prejudice" prerequisites of Mich. Ct. R. 6.508(D)(3) did not apply where a defendant properly alleged a jurisdictional defect in a prior proceeding that resulted in a conviction and sentence. Further, an alleged Gideon defect would constitute a jurisdictional defect that could be collaterally attacked by a convicted defendant. Thus, the appellate court's reasoning, that defendant's claim created a jurisdictional defect, was correct. However, defendant was not entitled to relief because he presented neither prima facie proof that his prior juvenile adjudications were procured without counsel, nor that his request for such proof was refused or ignored by the sentencing court where his juvenile records had been properly expunged. Thus, defendant failed to satisfy his initial burden of showing that the circuit court erred in relying on the prior juvenile adjudications.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reasoning of the court of appeals but reversed its order remanding to the circuit court. The court denied defendant's request for relief from judgment.

City of Detroit v. Walker


Nos. 96594, 96595, 96596, 96597, 96598, 96599


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


445 Mich. 682; 520 N.W.2d 135; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 1561


April 6, 1994, Argued  
July 26, 1994, Decided  
July 26, 1994, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff City of Detroit sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the lower court's grant of summary judgment for defendant delinquent taxpayers, who were sued in both their individual and estate representative capacities for the collection of taxes. Plaintiff contended that the Detroit, Mich. City Charter allowed the incorporation of the General Property Tax Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 211.47.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff City of Detroit filed tax collection suits against defendant delinquent taxpayers, both in their personal and estate representative capacities, pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 211.47. The lower court granted summary judgment to defendants, which was affirmed on appeal, and which held that no method of tax collection was permitted under the city charter, except for the real property foreclosure method. Plaintiff sought further review, and the court reversed. The court held that the Detroit, Mich. City Charter incorporated tax collection remedies embodied in the General Property Tax Act, and any amendments thereto, including Mich. Comp. Laws § 211.47. The court further held that it was permissible to apply the amendment retroactively because it did not create a new right for plaintiff, where the right to collect taxes remained the same. Further, that there was no new liability created for defendants, because defendants were already obligated under a preexisting tax debt. The court found that it merely added a procedural device for plaintiff to secure taxes owed, and as such, there was no vested right at stake and retroactive application was permitted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment affirming the summary judgment to defendant delinquent taxpayers. The court held that plaintiff City of Detroit was permitted to collect unpaid taxes by new methods encompassed within statutory law, where such was not expressly excluded by the City Charter. Further, that such methods could be applied retroactively. The matter was remanded for further proceedings.

Michigan Bell Tel. Co. v. Department of Treasury


Nos. 95347, 95349


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


445 Mich. 470; 518 N.W.2d 808; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 1325


December 3, 1993, Argued  
June 21, 1994, Decided  
June 21, 1994, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner telephone company sought review of a decision from the Michigan Court of Appeals affirming the decision of a tax tribunal concluding that intangible property of petitioner was properly included in the valuation and assessment of its property subject to taxation under 1905 Mich. Pub. Acts 282; Mich. Comp. Laws § 207.1 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 7.251 et seq.).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner telephone company filed suit against respondent Department of Treasury contending that petitioner's intangible personal property was improperly considered in the valuation and assessment of its property subject to taxation under 1905 Mich. Pub. Acts 282; Mich. Comp. Laws § 207.1 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 7.251 et seq.). The court affirmed the court of appeals and tax tribunal's judgment and held that intangibles were appropriately taken into account for purposes of assessing and taxing petitioner's property. Given that the usual and customary meaning of property included intangibles and noting that the legislature deliberately chose not to use the limiting term tangible personal property, the court concluded that the legislature intended intangibles to be covered by the definition of property under Act 282. The court ruled that an intangibles tax act did not preclude consideration of intangibles in valuing and assessing property for purposes of taxation under Act 282. The court held that the taxation of intangible personal property was constitutional since previous amendments to the state constitution did not distinguish between tangible and intangible property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals and tax tribunal's judgment and concluded that the intangible property of petitioner telephone company was properly included in the valuation and assessment of its property subject to taxation under an act providing for the annual assessment of property. The court held the act authorized for taxation of intangible property and ruled the act was constitutional under the state constitution.

 

63 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Rowell v. Security Steel Processing Co.


No. 94644, No. 95000


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


445 Mich. 347; 518 N.W.2d 409; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 1326


October 5, 1993, Argued  
June 14, 1994, Decided  
June 14, 1994, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and Remanded


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court reviewed the conclusions of two different courts of appeal (Michigan), which reached contrary results as to the use of fractional weeks to calculate worker's compensation benefits under Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.371 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(371), where the employee had worked less than 39 weeks.


OVERVIEW: The court held that in calculating the average weekly wage, the percentage or fraction of work performed in a partially worked week was to be included in the denominator where a week was partially worked because the day of hiring or the day of injury fell during the week. Such an interpretation was in accordance with the overriding purpose of § 371(3) of the Worker's Disability Compensation Act, which was to provide for a computation of an average weekly wage that fairly represented the employee's earning capacity as fixed at the time of injury. When the Legislature employed the phrase "weeks actually worked," it intended total wages earned to be divided by the whole number of full weeks worked plus a fractional number representing the partially worked weeks. Application of such a construction led to a calculation of an employee's average weekly wage that would most closely parallel a mathematical average. Read together, the provisions of § 371 illustrated that the Legislature attempted to draft a statute that would be sensitive to accuracy as the basis for the determination of weekly wage loss in a variety of employment situations.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed in each case, and the cases were remanded to the Worker's Compensation Appellate Commission to determine the correct amount of weeks worked in determining compensation.

 

64 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Payne v. City of Muskegon (In re Payne)


No. 94486


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


444 Mich. 679; 514 N.W.2d 121; 1994 Mich. LEXIS 501


October 5, 1993, Argued  
March 29, 1994, Decided  
March 29, 1994, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed a trial court order and held that defendant city could discharge plaintiff if there was any competent evidence that after she was warned, she failed to perform her work completely.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city's civil service commission (commission) denied plaintiff employee's appeal after she was discharged following warnings. The appellate court reversed a trial court order of superintending control that remanded the case to the commission for a less severe punishment. The court reversed the judgment below, holding that the trial court erred in substituting its judgment for that of the commission. The court held that a reviewing court was required to accept the findings of a municipal civil service commission that were supported by substantial evidence. Therefore, because plaintiff herself admitted her non-performance, the trial court should have accepted the commission's findings. The court also held that the commission could establish rules for employee conduct on the job. The commission's rules allowed it to terminate plaintiff under the facts she conceded, and this determination was not subject to substantial evidence review. Additionally, nothing in state law required defendant to retain an employee who failed to perform work after being warned. The court also ruled that the substantial evidence test satisfied constitutional requirements for judicial review.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court below and remanded the case to the trial court for an entry of judgment for defendant city because its decision to discharge plaintiff employee was supported by substantial evidence. Plaintiff admitted that she failed to complete her work despite warnings, and defendant's civil service commission lawfully established rules for employee conduct and discharge.

Booth Newspapers v. University of Mich. Bd. of Regents

April 1, 1993, Argued  
September 28, 1993, Decided  
September 28, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 192 Mich App 574; 481 NW2d 778 (1992) 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Board of Regents sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals of Michigan, which rendered judgment in part for plaintiff newspaper in an action involving the university presidential selection committee.


OVERVIEW: The issues for the court were whether the presidential selection procedure adopted by the Board of Regents violated the Open Meetings Act (OMA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 15.261 et seq., or the Freedom of Information Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 15.231 et seq. The court held that the Board of Regents was a public body that made closed session deliberations and decisions and held private interviews in violation of the OMA. Additionally, as evident from Freedom of Information Act (FOIA), under a plain-meaning analysis of § (13)(1)(a), two factors had to exist to exempt information from public exposure. First, the information sought had to be of a personal nature and the disclosure of such information had to constitute a clearly unwarranted invasion of privacy. Therefore, the court held that FOIA's § (13)(1)(a) exemption did not apply because the travel expense records failed to survive scrutiny under the first prong of the two-part test. Based on an examination of the OMA and FOIA, the court ruled that both acts were violated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in part and reversed it in part, ruling that both acts were violated. The case was remanded to the circuit court for entry of a judgment providing injunctive relief and compelling disclosure in conformity with the opinion.

Sitz v. Department of State Police


Docket No. 93851


Supreme Court of Michigan


443 Mich. 744; 506 N.W.2d 209; 1993 Mich. LEXIS 2218


March 2, 1993, Argued  
September 14, 1993, Decided  
September 14, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 193 Mich App 690; 485 NW2d 135 (1992) 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state police director challenged the judgment of the Michigan Court of Appeals, which held that sobriety checkpoints violated Mich. Const. art. I, § 11.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, licensed drivers of Michigan, filed a declaratory judgment action and sought injunctive relief challenging the constitutionality of sobriety checkpoints. After the United States Supreme Court determined that the checkpoints did not violate U.S. Const. amend. IV, the lower court held that they violated Mich. Const. art. I, § 11. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the checkpoints were unconstitutional under the Michigan Constitution because there was no support in Michigan's constitutional history for the proposition that police were permitted to engage in warrantless and suspicionless seizures of automobiles in order to enforce criminal laws. The court's review of cases construing Mich. Const. art. II, § 10 (1908) showed no support for the proposition that police could engage in warrantless, suspicionless seizures of automobiles. The court also held that the Michigan Constitution offered more protection than the United States Constitution. The court also found that seizures with the primary goal of enforcing the criminal law generally required some level of suspicion.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because there was no support in Michigan's constitutional history for the proposition that the police could engage in warrantless and suspicionless seizures of automobiles for the purpose of enforcing the criminal law.

House Speaker v. Governor


Docket Nos. 94631, 94632, 94633


Supreme Court of Michigan


443 Mich. 560; 506 N.W.2d 190; 1993 Mich. LEXIS 2210


May 6, 1993, Argued  
September 2, 1993, Decided  
September 2, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 195 Mich App 376; 491 NW2d 832 (1992).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Governor and Department of Natural Resources (DNR) appealed an order from the court of appeals (Michigan) holding that plaintiff nonprofit corporations had standing, their suit with state legislators did not raise non-justiciable political issues, the Governor exceeded his legislative authority under the Mich. Const., and enjoining enforcement of certain executive orders. The nonprofit appealed the validity of an executive order.


OVERVIEW: The Governor issued executive orders abolishing the existing DNR and creating a new DNR and related advisory body. The nonprofit corporations and legislators sued, alleging the Governor exceeded his limited legislative authority under the state Constitution and the Executive Organization Act. The Michigan Supreme Court concluded that the nonprofit corporations had standing to pursue the lawsuit to prevent illegal expenditure of state funds and that the issues were justiciable political questions. But the court held that under the Mich. Const. of 1963, art. 5, § 2, the Governor had authority to transfer all the authority, powers, duties, functions, and responsibilities of a legislatively-created principal department to a gubernatorially-created principal department. The court also upheld the constitutional validity of the executive orders. The executive orders made the kinds of changes in the organization of the executive branch contemplated by art. 5, § 2 and did not wrongfully delegate rule-making authority.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals' decision and dissolved the injunction permanently enjoining the Governor from enforcing the executive order.

Molter v. Dep't of Treasury


Docket No. 93747


Supreme Court of Michigan


443 Mich. 537; 505 N.W.2d 244; 1993 Mich. LEXIS 2208; 17 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 1585


March 2, 1993, Argued  
September 2, 1993, Decided  
September 2, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 193 Mich App 421; 484 NW2d 702 (1992).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) in favor of defendant state treasury, which affirmed a decision that his deferred compensation earned in Michigan was taxable in Michigan when distributed and that state income tax withholding on his deferred compensation did not violate equal protection.


OVERVIEW: Taxpayer was a Michigan state employee and contributed to an I.R.C. § 457 deferred compensation plan. Taxpayer retired, moved to Florida, and began receiving distributions from his plan. Taxpayer contended that the distributions were not taxable to Michigan because he was a nonresident and that withholding from public deferred compensation plans, but not private plans, violated equal protection. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. Because nonresidents were taxed on personal services performed in the state under § 110(2) of the Michigan Income Tax Act (MITA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 206.1 et seq. and the taxpayer's contributions to the plan were from his Michigan wages, the court concluded that the distributions attributable to contributions were taxable in Michigan. However, the court determined that the distributions attributable to interest on the contributions were not taxable in Michigan under § 113 of the Michigan Income Tax Act. Because the burden of taxation on all public or private plans was the same regardless of the withholding, the court agreed that there was no equal protection violation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that the taxpayer's contributions to a deferred compensation plan from Michigan wages was taxable to Michigan although taxpayer was now a nonresident and that the income withholding from the public plan was not a violation of equal protection. The court reversed that portion of the judgment that held that the interest on the contributions was taxable to Michigan, and the court remanded for further proceedings.

In re Forefeiture of $176,598


Docket No. 93248


Supreme Court of Michigan


443 Mich. 261; 505 N.W.2d 201; 1993 Mich. LEXIS 2215


March 2, 1993, Argued  
August 17, 1993, Decided  
August 17, 1993, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed the trial court's denial of appellee forfeiture claimant's motion for summary disposition and entered a forfeiture order. The claimant alleged ownership of the seized cash and the prosecutor filed a petition for civil forfeiture pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 333.7501 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 14.15(7501) et seq.).


OVERVIEW: The court held that the police officers' entry into the claimant's residence was constitutional pursuant to the exigent circumstances exception to the warrant requirement. The court noted that the police were immediately responding to a residential security alarm after midnight. When they arrived at the scene, a light was on inside the house. The officers found a broken window, and the security bars located inside the window frame were pushed away. One of the officers testified that the alarm was still sounding. These preeminent indications of a recently committed criminal entry were sufficient to give the police officers probable cause to believe that a crime recently had been committed on the premises and that the premises contained evidence or the perpetrators of the suspected crime. The same indicia of a criminal entry constituted specific and objective facts indicating the existence of an actual emergency. However, the court declined to employ the community caretaking exception under the facts of the present case. The court noted the search was not totally divorced from the detection, investigation, or acquisition of evidence relating to the violation of a criminal statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and remanded the cause to the appellate court for consideration of additional issues raised by the claimant.

People v. Hana


Docket No. 94268


Supreme Court of Michigan


443 Mich. 202; 504 N.W.2d 166; 1993 Mich. LEXIS 1943


April 1, 1993, Argued  
August 3, 1993, Decided  
August 3, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Certiorari Denied by the Supreme Court of the United States on February 22, 1994, 510 U.S.    (1994).



DISPOSITION:  Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed orders of the state appellate court (Michigan) which reversed the probate court's waiver of jurisdiction over juvenile defendant on charges for possession of more than 650 grams of a substance containing cocaine, delivery of more than 225 but less than 650 grams of a substance containing cocaine, conspiracy to induce a minor to commit a felony, and bribery of a public official.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was charged, the prosecution filed a motion with the probate court to waive jurisdiction over defendant. At defendant's phase II dispositional hearing, the court determined the best interest of the public and defendant would be served by its jurisdictional waiver over defendant to an adult court. Defendant appealed. The circuit court affirmed and the appellate court reversed. On appeal, the court reversed the appellate court, holding that the waiver was not akin to a criminal disposition, because there was no determination of guilt, and the asserted constitutional protections had not yet become relevant. The court held that legislative purpose mandated the conclusion that the probate court's discretion and full investigation requirement at the disposition phase of a waiver hearing remained unfettered by evidentiary and constitutional requirements necessary for later phase criminal proceedings. The court distinguished the case law asserted by defendant as either not applicable to the dispositional hearing phase of the proceedings, or that the necessary protections had already been afforded to defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the appellate court's reversal of the probate court's jurisdictional waiver over defendant. The court remanded other issues asserted by defendant to the appellate court.

People v. Hernandez


Docket No. 92923


Supreme Court of Michigan


443 Mich. 1; 503 N.W.2d 629; 1993 Mich. LEXIS 1932


October 13, 1992, Argued  
July 13, 1993, Decided  
July 13, 1993, filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which granted defendant's motion to remand pursuant to Mich. Ct. R. 7.211(C)(1)(a), to allow defendant to file a motion for resentencing. In seeking further review, the state asserted that the court of appeals erred in remanding the case.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to assault with intent to commit murder in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.83 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.278) following the beating of a victim with a baseball bat. Defendant challenged his sentencing score in a motion to the court of appeals to remand the case to the circuit court to allow him to file a motion or resentencing. The court of appeals remanded the case only because it believed it was compelled to do so. The court reversed holding that remand was not mandatorily required of the court of appeals because mandatory remand would require that every case be remanded no matter how frivolous. The court held that if defendant missed all previous opportunities to raise the scoring challenge, then Mich. Ct. R. 7.211(C)(3) allowed defendant to file a motion in the court of appeals to remand to the trial court for a resentencing hearing, but the procedure required discretion by the court of appeals to determine if remand was appropriate. The court found that defendant had several opportunities to raise the challenge and the circuit court judge's scoring of 50 points for excessive brutality was supported by the record.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals granting defendant's motion for remand to allow him to file a motion for resentencing because the court held that defendant had several opportunities to raise the scoring challenge and that the record supported the circuit judge's scoring of 50 points for excessive brutality.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff township challenged an order of the Court of Appeals of Michigan, which reversed a judgment that held that defendant landowners did not establish a prior nonconforming use of their property as a mobile home park before the passage of a referendum that rejected defendants' rezoning proposal.


OVERVIEW: The intermediate court reversed a trial court judgment that determined that defendant landowners did not establish a prior nonconforming use of their property as a mobile home park before the passage of a referendum in plaintiff township that rejected defendants' rezoning proposal. The court reversed the intermediate court's judgment on appeal and remanded the case, finding that defendants failed to prove the requisite tangible change in the property to establish a prior nonconforming use. OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and remanded. Clonlara, Inc. v. State Bd. of Educ.

Comp. Laws § 388.551 et seq., Mich. Stat. Ann. § 15.1921 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff school, which provided services to parents for home schooling and a parent who home schooled her children, challenged the constitutionality of the board's compliance procedures which required, for home schooling, that teachers be certified, that social studies and science be taught, and a 180-day school year. 75 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bennett


Docket No. 91480


Supreme Court of Michigan


442 Mich. 316; 501 N.W.2d 106; 1993 Mich. LEXIS 1403


November 10, 1992, Argued  
May 25, 1993, Decided  
May 25, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 188 Mich App 447; 470 NW2d 433 (1991).



DISPOSITION: Vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents challenged the judgment of a court of appeals (Michigan), which held in favor of the state and affirmed the parents' convictions for failing to send their children to school. The parents' challenge did not involve religious convictions, but rather alleged a Fourteenth Amendment violation of their right to direct a child's education.


OVERVIEW: The parents contended that the Fourteenth Amendment guaranteed them the fundamental right to direct the education of their children, even when the desire to direct education did not stem from any religious belief. The court vacated the convictions and held that before they could be prosecuted, the parents were entitled, as administrators of a private home school, to the hearing provided by the Private and Parochial Schools Act, Mich. Comp. Laws §§ 388.551 to 388.558 (Mich. Stat. Ann. §§ 15.1921 to 15.1928). The court held that a parent's Fourteenth Amendment right to direct a child's education was not one of those rights described by the United States Supreme Court as fundamental, and, thus, the strict scrutiny test was unwarranted. Only a minimal scrutiny test was required. The court held that the state could reasonably regulate education, including the imposition of teacher certification and curricula requirements on home-school programs, in order to advance the legitimate interest of compulsory education.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the parent's convictions and held that they were entitled to a hearing before they could be prosecuted for violation of compulsory education laws. The court held that there was no fundamental right to direct a child's secular education and that the state's regulation was judged by a rational relationship test.

People v. Dejonge


Docket No. 91479


Supreme Court of Michigan


442 Mich. 266; 501 N.W.2d 127; 1993 Mich. LEXIS 1402


November 10, 1992, Argued  
May 25, 1993, Decided  
May 25, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 188 Mich App 447; 470 NW2d 433 (1991).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, parents who conducted home schooling for their children, appealed the decision of the Michigan court of appeals, which affirmed, on appeal and rehearing, their convictions for instructing their children without state certified teachers in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 388.553 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 15.1923).


OVERVIEW: The court held that the historical under-pinnings of the First Amendment of the United States Constitution and the case law in support of it compelled the conclusion that the imposition of the certification requirement upon defendants violated the Free Exercise Clause. The court so concluded because it found that the certification requirement was not essential to nor was it the least restrictive means of achieving the state's claimed interest. Thus, the court reaffirmed "that sphere of inviolable conscience and belief which is the mark of a free people." The court held that the certification requirement was an unconstitutional violation of the Free Exercise Clause of the First Amendment as applied to families whose religious convictions prohibited the use of certified instructors. Such families, therefore, were exempt from the dictates of the certification requirement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' convictions.

Allstate Ins. Co. v. Hayes


Docket No. 92228


Supreme Court of Michigan


442 Mich. 56; 499 N.W.2d 743; 1993 Mich. LEXIS 1080


October 13, 1992, Argued  
April 13, 1993, Decided  
April 13, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 190 Mich App 499; 476 NW2d 453 (1991).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant injured party sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) which affirmed the trial court's entry of a summary judgment in favor of plaintiff insurer. The summary judgment was based on the default of the codefendant insured in the insurer's action for a declaratory judgment that its policy did not cover the accident leading to defendant's injuries.


OVERVIEW: After the insured defaulted in the declaratory judgment action brought by the insurer, the trial court granted the insurer's motion for summary judgment against the injured party, finding that it had no standing to contest the applicability of the insurance policy to the accident which caused the injuries and that the default judgment entered against the insured was binding upon it. In contesting the affirmance of that judgment, the injured party claimed that he had standing in the declaratory judgment action and that the default of the insured should not preclude his contest to the coverage issue. The court found that the only way the insured could bind the injured party to the declaratory judgment was to make it a party to the action, which it had. However, by doing so, the court found that the insurer had consented to the injured party's participation in the coverage dispute and thus could claim that the injured party lacked standing to participate therein. The court also found that the insured's default only bound it and did not impair the injured party's right to litigate the issue of coverage.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment against the injured party and remanded the case to the appellate court for consideration of remaining issues.

House Speaker v. State Administrative Bd.


Docket No. 92072


Supreme Court of Michigan


441 Mich. 547; 495 N.W.2d 539; 1993 Mich. LEXIS 262


October 14, 1992, Argued  
January 15, 1993, Decided  
January 15, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 190 Mich App 260; 475 NW2d 440 (1991).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State Administrative Board (Board) challenged the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which found that appellees, a group of Michigan Legislators, had standing to bring the action and ruled that the intradepartmental transfer in question was invalid on the ground that the statutory authority relied upon by the Board had been impliedly repealed by subsequent legislative acts.


OVERVIEW: The legislators challenged the authority of the Board under Mich. Comp. Laws § 17.3; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.263, to transfer appropriated funds from one program to another within a department of state government. After finding that the legislators had standing to bring the action, the lower court of appeals ruled that the intradepartmental transfer in question was invalid on the ground that the statutory authority relied upon by the Board had been impliedly repealed by subsequent legislative acts. On further review, the court agreed that the legislators had standing, but disagreed that the Board's transfer authority was repealed by implication. There was nothing in the Management and Budget Act (Act) that suggested intent to eliminate the Board's power to transfer funds within a department. Although the Act granted transfer power to the budget director, the director and the Board had separate duties. Each used different decision-making procedures and operated under different levels of accountability.


OUTCOME: The lower appellate court's holding that the Board's transfer authority was repealed by implication was reversed. There was nothing in the Management and Budget Act that suggested an intent by the legislators to eliminate the power of the Board to transfer funds within a department.

Schmidt v. Department of Educ.


Docket No. 90858


Supreme Court of Michigan


441 Mich. 236; 490 N.W.2d 584; 1992 Mich. LEXIS 2671


November 7, 1991, Argued  
September 29, 1992, Decided  
September 29, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied post, 1202.



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff school districts and plaintiff taxpayers appealed an order from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which dismissed plaintiffs' complaint against defendants, state, departments, and treasurer, for a violation of § 29 of the Headlee Amendment, Mich. Const. art. 9, §§ 25-34 (1963), and denied a rehearing.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff school districts and plaintiff taxpayers filed a complaint against defendants, state, departments, and treasurer, contending that defendant state violated § 29 of the Headlee Amendment, Mich. Const. art. 9, §§ 25-34 (1963), by reducing the state financed portion of necessary costs incurred by plaintiff school districts in providing certain state-required activities and services. A lower court dismissed plaintiffs' complaint and denied a rehearing. Plaintiffs appealed. The court reversed and remanded, holding that the statewide-to-local ratio for calculating defendant state's funding obligation for existing services and activities under § 29 preserved voter intent by securing a minimum funding guarantee while simplifying calculations and avoiding inequitable anomalies. The court further held that social security coverage was not a state-required activity or service within the meaning of the Headlee Amendment. The court determined that that Headlee amendment required that when defendant state mandated a program one factor in its determination of whether to require the activity or service had to be the proportionate share of funding that the state had to bear.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded a lower court's order dismissing a complaint by plaintiff school districts and plaintiff taxpayers against defendants, state, departments, and treasurer, for a violation of the Headlee Amendment, holding that the statewide-to-local ratio for calculating defendant state's funding preserved voter intent, and that social security coverage was not a state-required activity within the meaning of the Headlee Amendment.

People v. Kirby


Docket Nos. 91803, 91959


Supreme Court of Michigan


440 Mich. 485; 487 N.W.2d 404; 1992 Mich. LEXIS 2004


May 7, 1992, Argued  
August 4, 1992, Decided  
August 4, 1992, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of judgments from the Court of Appeals (Michigan) that reversed orders of the trial courts granting defendants a waiver of jury trial even tough the prosecution in each case objected.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, in separate prosecutions, requested that their cases be tried by the trial court without a jury. They signed a written form for waiver of trial by jury. The prosecutor in each case objected to the waiver and demanded a jury trial. The trial court accepted defendants' waivers and found Mich. Comp. Laws § 763.3 (1988) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.856 (1988)), and by implication Mich. Ct. R. 6.401, which required the prosecutor's consent to a waiver of jury trial, unconstitutional. The lower court reversed those judgments. On appeal, the court held that while defendants' right to trial by jury was guaranteed by the Michigan and federal constitutions, there was no corresponding constitutional right to waive a jury, and any rights a defendant might have to waive a jury were solely those granted by the legislature. Therefore, the trial court could not hold unconstitutional the requirement of prosecutorial consent to a waiver of jury trial where the right to waive a jury trial was not a constitutionally protected right.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgments that reversed the trial court's orders allowing defendants to waive jury trial over the prosecutors' objections and held that the right to a jury trial was a constitutionally protected right, but that the right to waive a jury was a right that arose only by statute.

Caterpillar, Inc. v. Dep't of Treasury


Docket No. 90999


Supreme Court of Michigan


440 Mich. 400; 488 N.W.2d 182; 1992 Mich. LEXIS 2003


December 5, 1991, Argued  
July 31, 1992, Decided  
July 31, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied by the Supreme Court of the United States on November 30, 1992, 506 U.S.    (1992).


PRIOR HISTORY: 188 Mich App 621; 470 NW2d 80 (1991).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The parties cross-appealed a decision of the court of appeals (Michigan), which granted plaintiff out-of-state corporation a limited tax refund in its suit against defendant Department of Treasury (department) challenging the constitutionality of the capital acquisition deduction (CAD) of the Small Business Tax (SBT), Mich. Laws § 208.23(a)(c), but severed only the language that produced the discriminatory effect.


OVERVIEW: The corporation, which conducted a portion of its business in Michigan, sought a refund of SBT taxes paid, claiming that the CAD provision of the SBT was unconstitutional. The corporation contended that the lack of symmetry between the three-factor formula for calculating SBT tax base, and the two-factor formula under CAD burdened interstate commerce by discriminating against out-of-state companies. The court of appeals affirmed the court of claims' decision to grant prospective relief, but severed from the SBT only the language that produced the discriminatory effect. The Michigan Supreme Court reversed, holding that the CAD did not violate the Commerce Clause. The court found that CAD satisfied all four prongs of the Complete Auto test because the SBT through CAD provisions: 1) had a substantial nexus to the taxing state, 2) was fairly apportioned, 3) did not discriminate against interstate commerce, and 4) was fairly related to the services provided in the state.


OUTCOME: Court of appeals' and court of claims decisions' reversed.

 

83 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Quinn


Docket No. 91667


Supreme Court of Michigan


440 Mich. 178; 487 N.W.2d 194; 1992 Mich. LEXIS 2006


March 4, 1992, Argued  
July 14, 1992, Decided  
July 14, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 189 Mich App 225; 471 NW2d 654 (1991).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff State appealed a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which vacated defendant's conviction and sentence for transporting or possessing a loaded firearm other than a pistol in or upon a vehicle in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.227c; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 23.424(3), and dismissed the charge against defendant.


OVERVIEW: In vacating defendant's conviction, the court below had determined that knowledge that the firearm was loaded was a necessary element of the offense and that the State had failed to prove that defendant knew the firearm was loaded. On appeal, the reviewing court disagreed and accordingly reversed the decision and reinstated the conviction. The reviewing court found that there was nothing in the language or legislative history of the statute which evidenced the legislature's intent to create a "double knowledge" requirement that would have required proof not only of knowledge of the presence of the firearm, but also that defendant knew that the firearm transported was loaded. The reviewing court further noted that contrary to defendant's assertion, the legislature was entitled, as part of its police powers, to define an act to make it criminal without defining the actor's knowledge as an element of the offense. Thus, the reviewing court concluded that defendant was properly convicted, as the legislature intended to hold a person liable for knowing transportation of a firearm that was in fact loaded, irrespective of whether defendant actually knew that fact.


OUTCOME: The reviewing court reversed the judgment under review and reinstated defendant's criminal conviction.
