People v. Bullock


Docket Nos. 89661, 89662


Supreme Court of Michigan


440 Mich. 15; 485 N.W.2d 866; 1992 Mich. LEXIS 1738


October 10, 1991, Argued  
June 16, 1992, Decided  
June 16, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied in Hasson, post, 1203.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following separate jury trials, both defendants were convicted of knowingly possessing 650 grams or more of cocaine in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 333.7403(2)(a)(i), and sentenced to life in prison without any possibility of parole. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed both defendants' convictions. The prosecution appealed.


OVERVIEW: After a police dog signaled the presence of illegal drugs in defendant passenger's suitcases, the police stopped defendant driver's automobile and conducted a warrantless search of the entire car. The police found traces of cocaine in the glove compartment and defendant driver's purse, and over 15 kilograms of cocaine in defendant passenger's luggage. This cocaine was admitted as evidence at trial over defendants' objections. The state supreme court held that the evidence of cocaine was admissible. With respect to defendant passenger, the police had probable cause to search the luggage, and the automobile exception permitted a warrantless search. The police could search the entire passenger compartment of the automobile and any containers found therein, including glove compartments and purses, contemporaneous with the lawful custodial arrest of defendant passenger. Nevertheless, defendants' sentences were struck for being so grossly disproportionate as to constitute "cruel or unusual" punishment under Mich. Const. art. 1, § 16. However, the most appropriate remedy under the circumstances was to ameliorate the no-parole feature of the penalty.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment and reinstated defendants' convictions, but struck down the sentences imposed on both defendants as "cruel or unusual" under the Michigan Constitution.

In re Apportionment of State Legislature


Docket No. 92092


Supreme Court of Michigan


439 Mich. 715; 486 N.W.2d 639; 1992 Mich. LEXIS 1530



June 15, 1992, Filed  
June 15, 1992, Released


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] See Order Entered April 1, 1992, ante, p 251.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state constitution required the state legislature be apportioned following each decennial census. However, the legislature was unable to enact an apportionment plan. Therefore, the court appointed special masters to create an apportionment plan in accordance with certain directions previously issued by the court. The court adopted the proposed plan with certain modifications and entered a memorandum opinion to explain its actions.


OVERVIEW: Although the Michigan Constitution required the legislature to develop a new apportionment plan following each federal census, on several prior occasions the legislature was either unable to enact a plan or the plan they agreed upon failed to pass constitutional muster. Following the 1990 census the legislature failed to apportion itself and an action was filed that invoked the court's jurisdiction under Mich. Const. of 1963 art. 4. The court appointed special masters who developed plan after reviewing other proposals and considering the criteria previously issued by the court. The court adopted the plan with modification and entered a memorandum opinion to communicate the basis of its decision to adopt with modifications the apportionment plan presented by the special masters. The court justified the use of the 16.4 percent population divergence after finding that some deviations were permitted to allow the state to pursue other objectives such as honoring jurisdictional boundaries. After considering the totality of the circumstances, the court also concluded that the plan neither diluted nor concentrated minorities in violation of the Voting Rights Act of 1965.


OUTCOME: The court entered the memorandum opinion explaining it's reasons for adopting with modification the apportionment plan submitted by the masters. Specifically the court addressed the argument that the plan violated the Voting Rights Act of 1965 and justified a population divergence of more than 10 percent.

Doe v. Department of Social Services


Docket Nos. 91092, 91093


Supreme Court of Michigan


439 Mich. 650; 487 N.W.2d 166; 1992 Mich. LEXIS 1527


November 7, 1991, Argued  
June 9, 1992, Decided  
June 9, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 187 Mich App 493; 468 NW2d 862 (1991).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee indigent women filed an action against appellant state officials for a declaratory judgment and a permanent injunction prohibiting enforcement of Mich. Comp. Laws § 400.109a (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 16.490(19a) of the Social Welfare Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 400.1 et. seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 16.401 et seq.). The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed the trial court's decision that § 109a was constitutional. The state officials appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue before the court was whether § 109a, which prohibited the use of public funds to pay for abortions unless necessary to save the mother's life, violated the equal protection guarantee of Mich. Const. art. I, § 2. The court held that; (1) § 109a did not violate the state's equal protection guarantee because an indigent pregnant woman was treated the same as any other indigent woman regardless of whether or not she wanted an abortion or childbirth in that she was offered reimbursement for the expenses of childbirth, but not for the expenses of an abortion; (2) the state's offer to fund childbirth did not impermissibly influence the procreative decisions of an indigent woman, and thus, did not impinge upon a fundamental right under Mich. Const. art. I, § 2; (3) § 109a was rationally related to a legitimate governmental purpose; and (4) the proper standard of review was the rational basis test because § 109a did not impinge on the exercise of a fundamental right, and because the state equal protection guarantee did not offer greater protection than the federal equal protection guarantee. Thus, the appeals court erred when it subjected § 109a to strict scrutiny.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appeals court's judgment that the relevant provision of the Social Welfare Act was unconstitutional.

Adams Outdoor Advertising v. City of E. Lansing


Docket Nos. 89026, 89027, 89028


Supreme Court of Michigan


439 Mich. 209; 483 N.W.2d 38; 1992 Mich. LEXIS 947


October 10, 1991, Argued  
March 27, 1992, Decided  
March 27, 1992, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the City of East, Lansing, Michigan, sought review of a decision of a Michigan appellate court that affirmed the trial court's grant of appellee corporations' motion for partial summary disposition on the ground that appellant did not have the statutory authority to eliminate existing signs owned by appellees without compensating appellees.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted appellee corporations' motion for partial summary disposition after finding that the amortization provision of the sign code of appellant, the City of East Lansing, Michigan, was enacted without legislative authority. The appellate court affirmed and appellant sought review, arguing that Mich. Comp. Laws § 117.4i(5) gave it the statutory authority to forcibly terminate nonconforming signs over a reasonable period of time. The court agreed, holding that appellant's power to regulate billboards was not subject to the restrictions imposed on zoning regulation by the zoning enabling act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 125.581 et seq. The court therefore reversed the decision of the appellate court and remanded the case to the trial court for resolution of the remaining issues. In a separate opinion, a second judge agreed with the disposition of the case made by the first judge and held that a home rule city was authorized to require, without payment of compensation, compliance with reasonable sign and billboard regulations. However, the judge noted that if enforcement of the regulations effected a taking, the constitution required that just compensation be paid.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court because it held that appellant city had statutory authority to forcibly terminate nonconforming signs and billboards over a reasonable period of time.

Wade v. Dep't of Corr.


Docket No. 88656


Supreme Court of Michigan


439 Mich. 158; 483 N.W.2d 26; 1992 Mich. LEXIS 782


November 6, 1991, Argued  
March 23, 1992, Decided  
March 24, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 182 Mich App 519; 453 NW2d 683 (1990).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Department of Corrections appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed the trial court and held that the department was liable for injuries suffered by plaintiff inmate in a slip and fall action under the public building exception to governmental immunity.


OVERVIEW: An inmate slipped on grease in a prison building and sued the Department of Corrections. The trial court ruled in favor of the department. The appeals court reversed, holding that the public building exception to governmental immunity, Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.1406, applied to a slip and fall injury occasioned by an accumulation of grease or oil on a hallway floor. The court reversed the appeals court and held that the transitory condition was not caused by a dangerous or defective condition of the building itself, thus the public building exception did not apply. The court held the public building exception was to be narrowly construed and the inmate's claims were no more than mere allegations of negligence. No defect in the building itself was pleaded and the court therefore held that the inmate failed to state a claim upon which relief could be granted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appeals court.

People v. Juillet


Docket Nos. 76747, 85344


Supreme Court of Michigan


439 Mich. 34; 475 N.W.2d 786; 1991 Mich. LEXIS 2264


May 8, 1991, Argued  
September 30, 1991, Decided  
September 30, 1991, Filed  
October 1, 1991, Released


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 173 Mich App 202; 433 NW2d 404 (1988).



DISPOSITION: Brown, reversed and remanded. Juillet, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, in consolidated actions, appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals that affirmed their convictions on drug related charges and denied their respective motions regarding entrapment. Both defendants argued that they were induced into committing the crimes by the actions of the police.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with drug trafficking offenses based on evidence obtained by police informants. The first defendant was caught by a prostitute who relied upon a pre-existing criminal relationship to obtain evidence. The second defendant was caught by an undercover policeman who initiated a friendship with the second defendant, who had a low I.Q., and then used that friendship to have the second defendant obtain drugs for him in order to accommodate his new-found friend. Using the objective test as to whether the police conduct would have induced a normally law-abiding person into the criminal conduct, the court held that the first defendant was not entrapped because the investigators' conduct did not induce the criminal behavior, but merely presented an opportunity for the defendant to continue an illicit relationship. However, the court held that the second defendant was entrapped because the only reason for his delivery of drugs to the informant was the incessant requests and the other activities undertaken by the informant, a circumstance in which any normally law-abiding person might be induced into committing a crime to help a friend.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court as to one defendant but reversed as to the second defendant. The court held that the acts of the police in the first case did not and would not have induced criminal conduct in a normally law-abiding person, but merely took advantage of pre-existing illegal activity. The second defendant was entrapped because the informant, posing as a friend, manipulated the defendant into the criminal acts.

York v. Detroit


Docket No. 87229


Supreme Court of Michigan


438 Mich. 744; 475 N.W.2d 346; 1991 Mich. LEXIS 2104


December 4, 1990, Argued  
September 23, 1991, Decided  
September 23, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 182 Mich App 92; 452 NW2d 641 (1989).

 119 Mich App 512; 367 NW2d 333 (1985).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) that reversed the trial court ruling and ordered that plaintiff decedent's widow's was allowed the requested jury instruction concerning the standard for municipal liability under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 in her action for decedent's suicide death while in pretrial detention.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals found that plaintiff decedent's widow should not have been refused her requested instructions concerning the standard for municipal liability under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 in her action against defendant city for decedent's suicide death while in pretrial detention. The court reversed, finding that the requested instructions described an erroneous standard of negligence, and defendant's inaction would have constituted a policy or custom under § 1983 only if it evidenced a deliberate indifference to decedent's serious medical needs. The court held that the standard was not established by negligent failure to detect suicidal intent or to prevent suicide, and that the existence of rules to reduce the risk of jail suicides did not evidence defendant's knowledge of decedent's risk. In holding that defendant's failure to follow the rules did not alone establish deliberate indifference, the court overruled a court of appeals decision which held that the rules mandatorily applied to municipal lockups. The court found that at the time of the case and decedent's suicide, the department of corrections did not have authority to promulgate rules governing lockups.


OUTCOME: The judgment allowing plaintiff decedent's widow's requested instructions was reversed because they described an erroneous standard of negligence where defendant's failure to prevent decedent's suicide while in pretrial detention or to follow rules to reduce such a risk did not establish deliberate indifference to decedent's serious medical needs as was necessary to constitute a policy or custom sufficient for municipal civil rights liability.

People v. Toohey


Docket No. 88974


Supreme Court of Michigan


438 Mich. 265; 475 N.W.2d 16; 1991 Mich. LEXIS 2111


April 2, 1991, Argued  
August 27, 1991, Decided  
August 27, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 183 Mich App 348; 454 NW2d 209 (1990).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted for possession of cocaine. Defendant challenged his conviction claiming that the cocaine was discovered during an illegal inventory search of his vehicle. The court of appeals (Michigan) agreed with defendant and reversed his conviction. The prosecution challenged the order that reversed defendant's conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for driving while intoxicated and his vehicle was impounded and searched. Cocaine was found in defendant's vehicle and he argued that the search was unlawful. On appeal the court found that the search of defendant's vehicle was lawful. The court reasoned that under the inventory exception to the warrant requirement the police could lawfully search an impounded vehicle in order: to protect the owner's property; to protect of the police against claims or disputes over lost or stolen property; and to protect the police from potential danger. The court found that warrantless inventory searches satisfied the reasonableness requirement of the Fourth Amendment if they were conducted pursuant to standardized police procedures. The court found that Ann. Arbor, Michigan Ordinance 10:13 provided a standardized procedure of impounding and searching vehicles whenever the driver was taken into custody and the vehicle was left unattended. Defendant also argued that his vehicle was not left unattended. The court rejected this argument because the passenger was intoxicated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's order and reinstated defendant's conviction for possession of cocaine.

Shelby Township Police & Fire Retirement Bd. v. Shelby


Docket No. 86109


Supreme Court of Michigan


438 Mich. 247; 475 N.W.2d 249; 1991 Mich. LEXIS 2112


March 6, 1991, Argued  
August 27, 1991, Decided  
August 27, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 175 Mich App 163; 437 NW2d 352 (1989).

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff retirement board challenged an order of the Michigan Court of Appeals which affirmed the decision of the trial court which denied the retirement board its request for a writ of mandamus to compel defendants, township and its officials, to appropriate the funds necessary to maintain the actuarial integrity of the pension system pursuant to Mich. Const. art. 9, § 24.


OVERVIEW: The retirement board annually certified the amount due to fund the police and fire pension fund. The township then appropriated the funds based on its actuary's determination, which was less than the amount certified. The retirement board brought an action against the township and its officials for a writ of mandamus to compel them to appropriate the funds that it certified, pursuant to Mich. Const. art. 9, § 24. The trial court and the court of appeals denied the writ, and on further appeal the court affirmed the denial of the writ. The court also held that when the legislature delegated to the retirement board the authority to establish levels of funding necessary for proper funding, that exercise did not constitute an unlawful delegation of the township's power or a restriction on the township's ability to control the appropriation of its funds.

People v. Collins


Docket No. 86690


Supreme Court of Michigan


438 Mich. 8; 475 N.W.2d 684; 1991 Mich. LEXIS 2118


October 4, 1990, Argued  
August 22, 1991, Decided  
August 22, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 393 Mich 554; 227 NW2d 511 (1975).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed the judgment of the Michigan Court of Appeals, which affirmed the suppression of tape-recorded conversations between defendant and a police informant which were obtained with an invalid warrant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with obstruction of justice for offering a police informant $ 500 to present false testimony in his wife's criminal case. Tape-recordings were made of defendant's conversations with the informant with the informant's consent pursuant to a warrant. After determining that the warrant was invalid, the trial court suppressed the recorded statements. The appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court reversed. U.S. Const. amend. IV did not require a warrant here. The court determined that Mich. Const. art. I, § 11 did not require a greater restriction on law enforcement activities and, therefore, a valid warrant was not required here, and therefore the trial court erred by suppressing defendant's statements. The court overruled a prior case that held otherwise. The court found that practical considerations weighed heavily against the imposition of a constitutional warrant requirement for participant monitoring, including to establish probable cause and informant credibility, the impossibility of adhering to strict warrant requirements, and the particular need for warrantless participant monitoring in drug-related investigations.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded because the warrantless participant monitoring by the police informant did not violate defendant's reasonable expectation of privacy and the Michigan Constitution did not afford defendant greater protection that the United States Constitution which required no warrant.

Girard v. Wagenmaker


No. 85662


Supreme Court of Michigan


437 Mich. 231; 470 N.W.2d 372; 1991 Mich. LEXIS 961



May 7, 1991, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), that overturned the trial court's decision and found that appellee putative father had standing to dispute the paternity of the mother's child under the Paternity Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 722.711-730 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 25.491-510), or the Child Custody Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 722.21-28 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 25.312(1)-(8)).


OVERVIEW: A putative father filed suit under the Paternity Act and the Child Custody Act, claiming to be the father of a child conceived and born while the mother was married to her husband. The putative father sought a determination of paternity, and, if he was the biological father, an order of filiation, visitation, and support. The trial court found the putative father lacked standing to sue, but the appellate court reversed. The court reviewed the legislative history of the Paternity Act and held that in order to have standing to sue under the Paternity Act, the putative father had to have a judicial determination that the child was not the issue of the marriage or the child had to be have been born out of wedlock. As no action had ever been undertaken to determine the child's paternity, and as the child was born in wedlock, the putative father had no standing to bring a claim under the Paternity Act. The court similarly determined that the putative father had no standing under the Child Custody Act. He could not obtain a determination that he was the natural father of the child under the Child Custody Act; as a nonparent, the putative father's custody claims were barred.


OUTCOME: The order that the putative father had standing to dispute the paternity of the mother's child was reversed. The trial court's order summarily dismissing the putative father's claims was reinstated.

People v. Sutton


Docket No. 80844


Supreme Court of Michigan


436 Mich. 575; 464 N.W.2d 276; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 3061


March 9, 1988, Argued  
September 28, 1990, Decided  
September 28, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 437 Mich 1208.



DISPOSITION: Reversed; conviction reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed an order of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed defendant's conviction for second-degree murder and possession of a firearm during the commission of a felony on the grounds that U.S. Const. Amend. V precluded cross-examining defendant regarding when he told the police the shooting was an accident.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of murder and possession of a firearm after a trial where defendant indicated that he told the police the shooting was an accident. The police testified that defendant remained silent. Defendant appealed his conviction and the appellate court reversed. On appeal, the court held that impeachment of exculpatory testimony with the fact that defendant remained silent was permissible under the Michigan Constitution. Defendant testified at trial that he told the police that the shooting was an accident, when in fact, defendant had invoked his right to remain silent. Nothing in U.S. Const. amend. V or case law allowed defendant to so testify free from contradiction. Therefore, the state was not precluded from rebutting defendant's claims of post-custody statements consistent with his accident defense with evidence of defendant's silence. Additionally, defendant did not object to the state's question to the police regarding defendant's silence. Thus, defendant intentionally waived any plausible objection and invited impeachment with his inconsistent post-arrest, post-custody behavior.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate court, which reversed defendant's conviction in the trial court. The court reinstated defendant's convictions and sentences.

 

96 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Romein v. General Motors Corp.


Docket Nos. 83830, 83831


Supreme Court of Michigan


436 Mich. 515; 462 N.W.2d 555; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 3052


October 5, 1989, Argued  
September 28, 1990, Decided  
September 28, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 437 Mich 1202.


PRIOR HISTORY: 168 Mich App 444; 425 NW2d 174 (1988).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employees sought to recover workers' compensation benefits that defendant employers had coordinated with employer-funded pension plan payments pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws (MCL) § 418.354 (Mich. Stat. Ann. (MSA) § 17.237(354)) of 1981 Mich. Pub. Acts (PA) 203. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) found that MCL § 418.354(17)-(20) (MSA § 17.237(354)(17)-(20)) prohibited the coordination of benefits here. The employers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employees were injured before the effective date of 1981 PA 203. The employers contended that MCL § 354(17)-(20) was unconstitutional. The state supreme court held that MCL § 354(17)-(20) was a constitutional exercise of the state legislature's power to retroactively modify benefit levels for a legitimate purpose furthered by rational means. Moreover, the statute did not abrogate any vested rights of the employers. Thus, the statute did not violate either the state or federal Due Process Clauses. Furthermore, the employers' expectation interest was not protected by the Contract Clause. Finally, the statute did not violate separation of powers principles because it was a valid exercise of the legislature's authority to retroactively amend legislation perceived to have been misconstrued by the judiciary. Therefore, the employers' right to coordinate workers' compensation benefits was repealed by MCL § 418.354(17)-(20) through a constitutional exercise of legislative authority to regulate social and economic life. So long as it was not applied to impair coordination rights that were reduced to final judgment before its effective date, MCL § 418.354(17)-(20) was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision that prohibited the coordination of benefits.

 

97 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Perlos


Docket Nos. 86432-86436, 86524


Supreme Court of Michigan


436 Mich. 305; 462 N.W.2d 310; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 3050


May 10, 1990, Argued  
September 25, 1990, Decided  
September 25, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 177 Mich App 657; 442 NW2d 734 (1989).

 176 Mich App 334; 438 NW2d 908 (1989).



DISPOSITION: Perlos, Brown, Miller, Bentley, and Schomer, reversed and remanded to the district court. England, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Based upon the results of their blood alcohol tests obtained by the state pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 257.625a(9), one of the defendants was convicted of drunken driving, the other of involuntary manslaughter. In the drunken driving case, the appellate court (Michigan) ruled that the statute was unconstitutional, while the appellate court in the involuntary manslaughter case held the statute was constitutional. The parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were involved in car accidents and taken to the hospital. Blood tests, made for medical treatment, showed that defendants' blood alcohol contents were over the legal levels of intoxication. Pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 257.625a(9), the state, without obtaining a search warrant or defendants' consent, received the test results from the hospitals. On the basis of the results, one of the defendants was convicted of drunk driving and the other defendant was convicted of involuntary manslaughter. Defendants objected to the constitutionality of § 257.625a(9). On appeal, the court held: (1) the Fourth Amendment was not implicated because there was no state involvement where the blood was drawn for medical reasons by medical personnel; (2) defendants' Fourth Amendment privacy rights were not violated by the state's acquisition of the test results because the subjective expectation of privacy defendants had in their test results was outweighed by the legislature's and the public's strong desire to eradicate drunken driving; and (3) there was no equal protection violation because a fundamental right had not been violated and a reasonable basis, safety, underlay the statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's ruling in the drunken driving case that the statute was unconstitutional and affirmed the appellate court's ruling upholding the constitutionality of the statute in the involuntary manslaughter case.

 

98 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Rushing v. Wayne County


Docket No. 74724


Supreme Court of Michigan


436 Mich. 247; 462 N.W.2d 23; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 2507


May 10, 1990, Argued  
September 20, 1990, Decided  
September 20, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied by the Supreme Court of the United States on March 18, 1991, 499 U.S.   (1991).


PRIOR HISTORY: 138 Mich App 121; 358 NW2d 904 (1984).



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff pretrial detainee sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), affirming the trial court's grant of a directed verdict in favor of defendant county. Plaintiff contended that defendant county was liable under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 for constitutional deprivations she allegedly sustained while at defendant county's jail. She alleged that she was detained in a seminude state for four days.


OVERVIEW: At defendant county's jail, plaintiff pretrial detainee was left in a seminude state and was exposed to members of the opposite sex. Plaintiff brought suit against defendants, county, jail psychologist, and jail psychiatrist, alleging violations under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The trial court granted a directed verdict in favor of defendant county and the appellate court affirmed. On review, the court vacated the judgments and remanded the case to the trial court for consideration of defendant county's liability under § 1983. A reasonable jury could have found that the failure of defendant county to implement appropriate safeguards to protect against such exposure, in the face of a court-ordered suicide prevention plan mandating the removal of certain garments, constituted a deliberate indifference to and moving force behind the deprivation of plaintiff's constitutional rights. The evidence could have supported the conclusion that the failure to adequately instruct employees and to formulate any policy actually caused and was closely related to the deprivation of plaintiff's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the directed verdict in favor of defendant county and remanded for further consideration of defendant county's liability for violations of plaintiff pretrial detainee's constitutional rights. The jury could have concluded that the complete failure of policymakers to formulate a policy regarding the handling of stripped inmates manifested a deliberate indifference to constitutional rights.

 

99 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jamieson


Docket No. 83446


Supreme Court of Michigan


436 Mich. 61; 461 N.W.2d 884; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 3064; 9 A.L.R.5th 1085


April 4, 1990, Argued  
September 12, 1990, Decided  
September 12, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended November 20, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: 168 Mich App 332; 423 NW2d 655 (1988).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Michigan, which affirmed the trial court's dismissal of charges of unlawful delivery of controlled substances against defendants.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, who were deputy sheriffs, were charged with delivery of cocaine after they were caught distributing the drug to jail inmates in a reverse sting operation. The trial court dismissed the charges on the ground that defendants were entrapped because the state used an inmate to solicit and coordinate defendants' participation. The lower appellate court affirmed. On appeal the court held that 1) Michigan used the objective approach to determining whether an accused had been entrapped, which focused on whether the police conduct was likely to encourage the commission of crime that would not otherwise have been committed, rather than on the predisposition of the accused to commit the crime, 2) although the procedures employed by the state may not have been the only or most desirable methods by which to control drug trafficking in the prison, that was an insufficient basis upon which to support a finding of entrapment, 3) the record did not support the conclusion that the inmate preyed on the weaknesses of his captors to the extent that they were induced beyond a readiness to make contact with a drug supplier outside the jail and to transport drugs into the jail.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, which affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the controlled substances charges against defendants.

 

100 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hall


Docket No. 85050


Supreme Court of Michigan


435 Mich. 599; 460 N.W.2d 520; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 2541


November 8, 1989, Argued  
September 11, 1990, Decided  
September 11, 1990, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed defendant's conviction on the ground that hearsay testimony was erroneously admitted at the preliminary examination.


OVERVIEW: State challenged a lower court judgment that reversed defendant's conviction due to the fact that hearsay testimony was admitted during the preliminary examination. The court reversed the lower court judgment and remanded for an analysis of whether the admission of the hearsay testimony constituted harmless error. The court determined that an evidentiary deficiency at the preliminary examination was not a ground for the lower court to vacate a subsequent conviction where defendant received a fair trial and was not otherwise prejudiced by the error. The court held the automatic reversal rule should not be applied because to require automatic reversal of an otherwise valid conviction based on an error that was harmless would be an inexcusable waste of judicial resources.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court judgment and remanded for an analysis of whether the admission of the hearsay testimony at the preliminary hearing of defendant constituted harmless error.

 

101 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Milbourn


Docket No. 80475


Supreme Court of Michigan


435 Mich. 630; 461 N.W.2d 1; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 2546


May 3, 1988, Argued  
September 11, 1990, Decided  
September 11, 1990, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Sentence vacated and case remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in trial court of breaking and entering a residence with the intent to maliciously destroy property. Defendant was sentenced to 10-15 years imprisonment. The appellate court (Michigan) affirmed. Defendant appealed and argued that the sentence was excessive.


OVERVIEW: After breaking up with his girlfriend, defendant broke into her apartment and destroyed some personal items. Defendant also had two confrontations with the girlfriend. The court held that the sentencing decision of the trial court was reviewed for abuse of discretion. The court determined that its prior holding, that the sentence must shock the conscience of the appellate court to rise to the level of abuse of discretion, was deficient. The court held that sentences should be reviewed for proportionality in relation to the seriousness of the circumstances of the offense and the criminal history of the offender. The court held that when a particular case did not present a combination of circumstances which placed the offender in the most serious or least threatening class with respect to the particular crime, the trial court was not justified in imposing the maximum or minimum penalty, and the sentence should be vacated. The court held that although the Michigan Sentencing Guidelines were not mandatory, a departure from them which was not adequately explained would garner close appellate scrutiny. The court held that defendant's sentence was an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the sentence and remanded the case to the trial court for resentencing.

 

102 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ewing


Docket No. 84137


Supreme Court of Michigan


435 Mich. 443; 458 N.W.2d 880; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 2284


June 6, 1989, Argued  
August 14, 1990, Decided  
August 14, 1990, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The case is remanded to the trial court for an indication of which facts were relied upon in imposing sentence.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from a decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) that reversed the life sentence imposed for defendant's conviction for first-degree criminal sexual conduct on the grounds that the sentencing judge improperly considered facts underlying prior convictions, pending charges, and uncharged offenses in concluding that defendant engaged in a course of conduct involving attacks on young women over a period of five years.


OVERVIEW: The trial court imposed a life sentence upon defendant's conviction for first-degree criminal sexual conduct after finding that defendant engaged in a course of conduct involving attacks on women over a period of five years based on facts underlying prior convictions, pending charges, and uncharged offenses. Defendant objected to the consideration of arrests or any charged offense not resulting in conviction as evidence of guilt in imposing sentence. The court of appeals reversed on the grounds that the sentencing judge made unsupported assumptions of guilt. The state appealed. The court held that the sentencing judge should not be precluded from basing a sentence on the facts underlying pending charges, uncharged offenses, and prior acquittals, where those facts were developed through sworn testimony and defendant was afforded an opportunity to test the accuracy of the facts. The court found that it was clear that the judge considered the testimony of the similar acts witnesses, but that it was unclear as to which allegations were considered. The court remanded for the trial court to explain with greater specificity, which allegations played a role in determining the sentence.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the case to the trial court for an indication of which facts were relied upon in imposing a life sentence. The court held that the sentencing judge should not be precluded from basing a sentence on the facts underlying pending charges, uncharged offenses, and prior acquittals.

League General Ins. Co. v. Michigan Catastrophic Claims Ass'n


Docket No. 82417


Supreme Court of Michigan


435 Mich. 338; 458 N.W.2d 632; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 2282


December 6, 1989, Argued  
July 16, 1990, Decided  
July 16, 1990, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Michigan Catastrophic Claims Association (MCCA) challenged a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the trial court's ruling that the MCCA was a state agency, that its operating plan was a "rule" subject to the Administrative Procedures Act (Act), pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 24.201 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.560(101) et seq.), and that it could not levy premiums against appellee insurer.


OVERVIEW: The MCCA was created to serve as a means of reimbursing each member insurer for all catastrophic losses following implementation of a no-fault act in Michigan. Pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.3104(7)(d), (e) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 24.13104(7)(d), (e)), the MCCA was authorized to make and collect premium assessments from member insurers. However, on appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the appellate court holding that the MCCA was a state agency and that the MCCA could not assess premiums to member insurers. In pertinent part, the court held that the appellate court erred by not engaging in a dual analysis required to determine whether the MCCAs board was a "state" board, that the funds sought to be collected by the MCCA were assessments, and not taxes, and that the characteristics of the MCCA as a whole lead the court to recognize it as a private association. As a result, the court held that the MCCA was not a state agency subject to the Act, and that premiums assessed to MCCA member insurers were valid. Accordingly, the court remanded the case to the trial court for a determination of the proper fees and assessments due.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court holding that the MCCA was a state agency and that the MCCA could not assess premiums to member insurers. As a result, the court remanded the case to the trial court for a determination of fees and assessments due.

Stefanac v. Cranbrook Educational Community


Docket No. 82317


Supreme Court of Michigan


435 Mich. 155; 458 N.W.2d 56; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 1630; 55 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P40,571


March 8, 1989, Argued  
July 5, 1990, Decided  
July 5, 1990, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee brought an action against defendant employer for wrongful termination after she had executed a severance settlement agreement. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed a trial court dismissal based on the release and determined that the employee would have a reasonable time to tender the amount of severance to the employer. The employer sought review.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee brought an action against defendant employer for wrongful termination after she had executed a severance settlement agreement. Her action was dismissed by the trial court because the employee had failed to tender the amount of the severance. The court of appeals remanded the case to allow the employee a reasonable time to tender the severance payment. The court reversed because it determined that the tender rule required that whenever a legal claim was raised in contravention of a settlement agreement, even if joined with an equitable claim, tender was a precondition. The court further found, as a matter of law, that a plaintiff had to, in all cases where a legal claim was raised in contravention of an agreement, tender the consideration recited in the agreement prior to or simultaneously with the filing of suit.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and reinstated the dismissal with prejudice of plaintiff employee's claim for wrongful termination against defendant employer because the court determined that the employee failed to tender the consideration recited in the agreement prior to or simultaneously with the filing of suit.

Plymouth-Canton Community Schools v. State Tenure Com.


Docket No. 83160


Supreme Court of Michigan


435 Mich. 76; 457 N.W.2d 656; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 1628


May 1, 1989, Argued  
July 2, 1990, Decided  
July 2, 1990, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed a circuit court ruling that the tenure commission's decision that teacher did not receive a fair hearing was not a substantial and material error of law and remanded to the tenure commission for an evaluation of the merits of the disciplinary action taken against teacher.


OVERVIEW: Teacher was accused of violating the school district's policy regarding the use of physical force in the restraint of an 11-year-old student. Appellee school board appointed an attorney from the same firm as that of the board representing attorney to act as the pretermination hearing officer. Teacher objected to this procedure to the tenure commission, which found that teacher had to be reinstated as the taint of the initial decision by a biased tribunal could not be removed. The circuit court affirmed, but the appeals court reversed and remanded to the commission. On appeal, the court held that teacher did not demonstrate that the engagement of the hearing officer violated his pretermination due process rights or that he was not provided an opportunity to present his side of the story. There was no evidence that the hearing officer was a decisionmaker in the case or that he had a pecuniary interest in the outcome.


OUTCOME: Concluding that teacher was not deprived of his property interest in continued employment without due process of law, the court affirmed the decision of the appeals court and remanded the case to the tenure commission for review of the merits of teacher's termination de novo.

Scheurman v. Department of Transportation; Prokop v. Wayne County Board of Road Commissioners


Docket Nos. 81739, 82567


Supreme Court of Michigan


434 Mich. 619; 456 N.W.2d 66; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 1126


October 5, 1989, Argued  
May 7, 1990, Decided  
May 7, 1990, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In the first of two cases, consolidated for appeal, defendant Department of Transportation (DOT) appealed from the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which ruled in favor of plaintiff personal representative on her claim on a defect in the road. In the second case, plaintiff, an injured bike rider, appealed from the judgment in favor of defendant Wayne County Road Commission (commission) on her personal injury claim.


OVERVIEW: In the first case, a personal representative filed suit against the DOT, after the decedent was killed when she was struck by a car as she tried to cross a road. The personal representative claimed that the DOT was responsible for the lack of adequate street lighting along the road. In the second case, an injured bike rider brought suit against the Wayne County Road Commission, alleging that it failed to keep a street safe for travel by allowing a hedge to exist. The court held that the personal representative and the injured bike rider were not entitled to judgment. The court ruled that the duty imposed upon the state and the counties by the legislature pursuant to § 2 of the governmental immunity act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.402 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.996(102)), extended only to the improved portion of the highway designed for vehicular travel. Neither the alleged lack of adequate street lighting nor vegetation growing on private property had any connection to the traveled portion of the roadbed designed for public vehicular traffic. Therefore, the highway exception statute was inapplicable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the personal representative in the first case, and affirmed the judgment in favor of the commission in the second case.

People v. Heflin


Docket Nos. 79423, 83994


Supreme Court of Michigan


434 Mich. 482; 456 N.W.2d 10; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 1128


June 8, 1989, Argued  
April 30, 1990, Decided  
April 30, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied July 3, 1990.



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant one was convicted of second-degree murder of his son-in-law. Defendant two, a prostitute, was convicted of second-degree murder of her customer. Both were convicted under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.317. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed and remanded for new trials because jury instructions did not include lesser manslaughter offenses. The State appealed. The appeals were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: Defendant one argued on appeal that statutory involuntary manslaughter was a cognate lesser-included offense of murder and that the trial court should have instructed the jury on the lesser offense of statutory involuntary manslaughter because the evidence would have supported a conviction under the statute. Defendant two argued that after the trial judge instructed the jury, sua sponte, on voluntary manslaughter, he should have also instructed the jury on common-law involuntary manslaughter. The court reversed the decisions of the appeals court, concluding that (1) in the case of defendant one, the trial court did not err when it refused to instruct the jury on the lesser offense of statutory involuntary manslaughter even though it instructed the jury regarding voluntary manslaughter; (2) in the case of defendant two, the trial court's instruction to the jury adequately presented her theory of self-defense pursuant to Mich. Crim. Jury Instructions 7:9:01. Involuntary manslaughter was neither supported by the evidence nor presented to the jury by defendants or the prosecutor. Defendant two did not object to the instruction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appeals court as to the finding that the jury instructions did not include lesser offenses of statutory, involuntary manslaughter in the case of defendant one and common-law involuntary manslaughter in the case of defendant two.

 

108 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Li v. Feldt


Docket Nos. 84303, 85365


Supreme Court of Michigan


434 Mich. 584; 456 N.W.2d 55; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 1127



April 30, 1990, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured parties brought separate actions against defendants, cities and engineers (cities). One action was brought under the highway liability exception to the governmental tort liability act, Mich. Comp. Law § 691.1402 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.996(102)) and the other alleged negligence per se and negligence in fact. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) found that the cities were excepted from governmental immunity, and the cities appealed.


OVERVIEW: In one case, the injured party asserted that the city intentionally created a nuisance in setting the cycle for the traffic light. In the other case, the injured party alleged nuisance per se and nuisance in fact where a second city allegedly knew of danger after a first drowning and should have taken more precautions. The cases were consolidated and leave to appeal was granted to determine if the cities were immune from liability as government entities, and more specifically, whether the nuisance exception to governmental immunity entailed intentional nuisance. Reviewing the law of governmental immunity, the court held that the historical approach, which defined the scope of the nuisance exception to governmental immunity, mandated a finding that intentional nuisance was not an exception to governmental immunity. Thus, the court held that the cities were immune for an intentional nuisance because intentional nuisance was not a recognized common-law exception to governmental immunity. The court noted that intentional nuisance occurred when one acted for the purpose of causing harm or knew that harm was substantially certain to occur.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decisions of both judgments and held that the cities were immune for an intentional nuisance. Further, the court remanded both cases for consideration the existence of nuisance per se and public nuisance exceptions to immunity and their applicability to the extent that they were properly raised in the court of appeals.

Detroit v. Qualls


Docket No. 83408


Supreme Court of Michigan


434 Mich. 340; 454 N.W.2d 374; 1990 Mich. LEXIS 806


June 7, 1989, Argued  
April 3, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied May 24, 1990.



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed in part and vacated in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city brought suit against defendant retailer for violating the storage provision of a fireworks ordinance. Detroit, MI., Code § 19-3-70. The district court held that the ordinance was unconstitutional and dismissed the charge. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) affirmed holding that the storage limitation in the fireworks ordinance was not rationally related to public safety.


OVERVIEW: Two years prior to this suit, the city brought an action against the retailer for forfeiture of fireworks seized from his premises, alleging a violation of the ordinance. The court found that the storage provision in the ordinance was a valid and reasonable exercise of the city's police power. The retailer did not appeal based on that ruling. In this case, the city appealed from the dismissal of the misdemeanor charge against the retailer. After review, this court found that the city failed to persuade it that the ruling holding the storage provision valid was necessary to the previous judgment concerning the forfeiture. Therefore, it held that the retailer was not precluded from raising the issue as an affirmative defense when prosecuted in this action. In addition, this court concluded that the state did not preempt local communities from regulating the quantity of fireworks a retailer can store within its boundaries and that the storage provision in the ordinance did not conflict with the statutory scheme. The storage limitation provision was a valid and reasonable exercise of the city's police power within the ambit of due process and equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court of appeal's holding that the storage provision in the ordinance did not conflict with state law was affirmed. The part of the decision that did not address the collateral estoppel issue was vacated. And the part of the decision that affirmed the district court's dismissal of the misdemeanor charge on the basis that the storage provision was unconstitutional was reversed.

Macenas v. Michiana


Docket No. 81601


Supreme Court of Michigan


433 Mich. 380; 446 N.W.2d 102; 1989 Mich. LEXIS 1945


October 5, 1988, Argued  
September 20, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: 160 Mch App 72; 407 NW2d 634 (1987) affirmed in part and reversed in part.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a zoning case, plaintiff landowner requested a building permit, which was denied by defendant village's council, sitting as the zoning board of appeals, because the landowner's lot failed to meet the width requirement of the local zoning ordinance. The board's decision was affirmed by the trial court, which was then reversed by the Court of Appeals (Michigan).


OVERVIEW: The landowner applied for a permit to construct a house on his parcel. A building permit was refused on the ground that the landowner's parcel did not measure at least 50 feet in width at the "front setback line" as the inspector interpreted the zoning ordinance. The appeals court determined the standard of review to be de novo but they accorded great weight to the findings of the trial court and zoning board of appeals due to their opportunity to see and hear the witnesses. On review, the court affirmed the court of appeals' determination of its standard of review. The court went on to determine what the court's scope of review should operated in the fact of mixed questions of fact and law. Because the dispute focused on a question of pure law, what the definition of "front setback line" meant, the court relied on the constraints of the rules of statutory construction. The court held that it was to give deference to the village's interpretation of its own ordinance. However, the court found that the village's ordinance was ambiguous in the sense that its meaning was not readily apparent, but not so ambiguous that it could accommodate the meaning attributed to it by the village.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded the decision.

Trinova Corp. v. Dep't of Treasury


Docket No. 82673


Supreme Court of Michigan


433 Mich. 141; 445 N.W.2d 428; 1989 Mich. LEXIS 1842


November 2, 1988, Argued  
August 21, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied post, 1203. Certiorari granted by the Supreme Court of the United States on March 5, 1990,    U.S.    (1990).


PRIOR HISTORY: 166 Mich App 656; 421 NW2d 258 (1988).

 Jones & Laughlin Steel Corp v Dep't of Treasury, 145 Mich App 405; 377 NW2d 397 (1985) overruled.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed with modification.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Taxpayer appealed from a judgment of the court of appeals (Michigan), which reversed the decision of the court of claims. The court of appeals concluded that relief under § 69 of the Single Business Tax Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 208.1 et seq., was available only where application of the apportionment formula resulted in an unfair representation of the taxpayer's Michigan-based business activity taken as a whole.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals held that the taxpayer was not entitled to relief under § 69 of the Single Business Tax Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 208.1 et seq., and the taxpayer sought review. The reviewing court agreed with the court of appeals' rejection of Jones & Laughlin Steel Corp. v. Dep't. of Treasury, 145 Mich. App. 405; 377 N.W.2d 397 (1985), and expressly overruled that case. The court agreed with the court of appeals' conclusion that relief under § 69 was available only where application of the apportionment formula resulted in an unfair representation of the taxpayer's Michigan-based business activity taken as a whole. The court concluded that the taxpayer had failed to show that its total business activity attributed to Michigan was out of all appropriate proportion to the taxpayer's intrastate business or had led to a grossly distorted result. To properly judge the propriety of a taxpayer's apportioned business activity, the court held that courts must view the activity as a whole. The court did not adopt all of the reasoning of the court of appeals but the court agreed with its conclusion that the taxpayer failed to demonstrate an entitlement to tax-base relief under § 69.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, with modification, the judgment that the taxpayer was not entitled to tax-base relief.

Electro-Tech, Inc. v. H F Campbell Co.


Docket No. 81866


Supreme Court of Michigan


433 Mich. 57; 445 N.W.2d 61; 1989 Mich. LEXIS 1847


October 6, 1988, Argued  
August 1, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 161 Mich App 622; 411 NW2d 800 (1987).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner challenged a judgment from a Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of the landowner in his action against defendant city under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OVERVIEW: The landowner applied to the city for permission to construct a manufacturing plant. The city approved the plant site, but imposed several conditions, one of which was the dedication of a strip of land. The landowner refused to comply, but did not submit a revised plan or seek a final decision. The city eventually secured the land through condemnation. The landowner then brought an action against the city under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 for an alleged taking caused by the wrongful denial of the building permit. The trial court entered judgment in favor of the landowner and the appellate court reversed. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court's decision and found that the landowner could not bring an action under § 1983 because the city had not made a final determination regarding the disposition of the landowner's property. The court found that there was no requirement under § 1983 that the landowner exhaust his administrative remedies. The court reasoned that the landowner failed to meet the other conditions of the city's approval or secure a final determination before bringing the action and, therefore, the landowner's action was not ripe for adjudication.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court, which reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of the landowner in his civil rights action against the city.

 

114 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Brittany Park Apartments v. Harrison Charter Township


Docket Nos. 82162, 82163


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


432 Mich. 798; 443 N.W.2d 161; 1989 Mich. LEXIS 1417


December 7, 1988, Argued  
July 24, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied September 8, 1989, 433 Mich 1201. Reported at 1989 MICH. LEXIS 2614.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township sought review of the decision of the Michigan Court of Appeals, which reversed the trial court's order that granted summary judgment for the township in appellee apartment owners' action that challenged the constitutionality of the township's rate structure for the municipality's water and sewage system.


OVERVIEW: The apartment owners' contended that the rate structure that charged them a base rate per residential unit was a violation of equal protection of the law. The court reversed the appellate court, finding that the ordinance was reasonable and rationally related to the objectives of the township. The township had the right to charge for services it provided to the community, which included the right to rationally impose classifications upon its users so long as all persons within the class were treated alike. The rate system was not based on the structure to which water was pumped, but on the type of occupant and the purposes and nature of its use. The court found that the apartment owners' received both a disadvantage and an advantage from the rate system. All the users of water within the classification were treated equally, and any inequality in the ultimate fee charged was tolerable. Thus, the inclusion of multiunit housing within the residential category was reasonable and supported by sufficient evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court, and held that the township's rate structure of water and sewage was constitutional and not a violation of equal protection.

 

115 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


In re Certified Question


Docket No. 78200


Supreme Court of Michigan


432 Mich. 438; 443 N.W.2d 112; 1989 Mich. LEXIS 1416; 4 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 673; 112 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P56,091


March 5, 1987, Argued  
June 6, 1989, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer sought review of a judgment that was entered in favor of appellee employee in an action that arose out of the employee's termination in an employment at will situation that had been modified by the employer's stated "for cause" policy. The United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit certified the dispositive question of unilateral policy change to the court because state law governed the issue.


OVERVIEW: The employee filed a complaint that alleged that the employer had a policy that it would not terminate its employees without just cause. The employer revised its handbook to eliminate any "for cause" requirement for discharge and made employment at the will of the company. The trial court found as a matter of law that the earlier handbook created a "for cause" employment contract, which the employer could not unilaterally alter as to existing employees to permit discharge at will. On appeal, the question was certified to the court, which held that: (1) the employer's handbook policy, while not expressing a contract, might be "instinct with obligation," (2) the employer could, without an express reservation of the right to do so, unilaterally change its written policy from one of discharge for cause to one of termination at will if the employer gave affected employees reasonable notice of the policy change.


OUTCOME: The court held that an employer may, without an express reservation of the right to do so, unilaterally change its written policy from one of discharge for cause to one of termination at will if the employer gives affected employees reasonable notice of the policy change.

 

116 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. LaLone


Docket No. 79221


Supreme Court of Michigan


432 Mich. 103; 437 N.W.2d 611; 1989 Mich. LEXIS 448


March 8, 1988, Argued  
March 30, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied May 26, 1989, Reported at 1989 MICH. LEXIS 995  [***1] post, 1236.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Court of Appeals (Michigan) convicting him of first-degree criminal sexual conduct.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree criminal sexual conduct. The complainant was his stepdaughter. On appeal, the court found that the exclusion of evidence of the complainant's sexual history did not violate either the rape-shield statute or defendant's Sixth Amendment right of confrontation. However, the court found that the trial court erroneously admitted statements made by the complainant to a psychologist, which identified defendant as her abuser. The statements did not fall within either the literal or intended purpose of Mich. R. Evid. 803(4). The statements made to the psychologist did not have the same measure of reliability as would a normal psychological therapy session because the complainant had already made the accusations and she was aware that a case against defendant was being prepared. Finally, the court held that defendant should have been permitted to introduce evidence that because he interfered with the complainant's efforts to have sexual relationships with teenage boys, she fabricated the charges to protect her opportunity to continue or to have sexual relationships.


OUTCOME: The order convicting defendant of first-degree criminal sexual conduct was reversed and the case was remanded for a new trial.

Askew v. Ann Arbor Public Schools


Docket No. 78996


Supreme Court of Michigan


431 Mich. 714; 433 N.W.2d 800; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 2307


October 8, 1987, Argued  
December 12, 1988, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff applicant sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) which agreed with defendant employer and held that the applicant's claim for continuing workers' compensation benefits on the basis of a change in physical condition that occurred while an appeal from the Workers Compensation Appeal Board's first award was pending was barred by the doctrine of res judicata.


OVERVIEW: An applicant for workers' compensation benefits obtained an initial award of benefits. While that award was pending on appeal to the Workers' Compensation Appeal Board, the applicant had a change in physical condition, which occurred before the hearing and decision by the board on review of the award. The applicant argued that the change supported a further and separate claim of benefits arising from the same injury. The court of appeals agreed with the employer that the applicant should have sought to present the evidence of the change in physical condition during the board's review in the first proceedings and held that the applicant was precluded by the doctrine of res judicata from obtaining continuing benefits. Upon review the court reversed the court of appeals. The court held that the administration of justice would not have been advanced by the adoption of a rule which required the applicant to present evidence of a change in physical condition that occurred after the award in order to avoid the bar of res judicata and to preserve the applicant's right to a hearing on the further and separate claim.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision which held that the applicant was precluded by the doctrine of res judicata from obtaining continuing workers' compensation benefits. The cause was remanded.

Pike v. Wyoming


Docket No. 78746


Supreme Court of Michigan


431 Mich. 589; 433 N.W.2d 768; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 2079


October 7, 1987, Argued  
November 10, 1988, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city and appellant injury fund filed a petition, alleging that the wife of appellee claimant was not a dependent in fact and requesting that the claimant's workers' compensation disability benefits be reduced. The Worker's Compensation Appeal Board dismissed the petition, and the Michigan Court of Appeals denied leave to appeal. The injury fund appealed.


OVERVIEW: The claimant received an increased benefit because his wife was conclusively presumed to be dependent under Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.353(1)(a)(i); Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(353)(1)(a)(i). Because the court struck down as unconstitutional a similar gender-based presumption of a widow's dependency set forth in Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.331(a); Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(331), the injury fund argued that the presumption of dependency in the claimant's case was also unconstitutional. The court agreed and held that the gender-based conclusive presumption of a wife's dependency violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. The court concluded that res judicata did not bar redetermination of the unappealed finding that the claimant's wife was a dependent at the time of the claimant's injury because res judicata principles were not applicable to a change in the amount of benefits received by the claimant. Finally, the court concluded that, in the interests of justice, its holding would not affect dependency payments already made should it be determined that the claimant's wife was not in fact a dependent.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision and remanded to the Worker's Compensation Appeal Board for further proceedings.

Riley v. Northland Geriatric Center


Docket Nos. 81129, 81325, 81689


Supreme Court of Michigan


431 Mich. 632; 433 N.W.2d 787; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 2080


May 4, 1988, Argued  
November 10, 1988, Decided

 161 Mich App 724; 411 NW2d 839 (1987).

 163 Mich App 130; 414 NW2d 160 (1987).



DISPOSITION: Riley, reversed. Juncaj and Moore, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted leave in three workers' compensation cases to resolve a conflict among panels of the Court of Appeals concerning the application of a case, which held that the adjustment provisions of § 355 of the Workers' Disability Compensation Act (act), Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.355 (Mich. Stat. Ann. §17.237(355)) applied only to the maximum, and not to the minimum, weekly rates established in § 351(1) of the act.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment in one case and affirmed the judgments in the two other workers' compensation cases. The court held that by directing that its "new rule" was to apply to any benefits due and not yet paid, the court implicitly stated that res judicata would not preclude adjustment of benefits after the date of its opinion. The court held that an adjustment of compensation rates in cases heard after the rule was set forth was not barred by res judicata. The precedent applied to workers' compensation awards made after the date the precedent was set forth and to all benefits due and not yet paid after that date where the award preceded the date of the opinion. The court held that, according to the language of the precedent, payments made December 30, 1981, were to be adjusted in accordance with the precedent regardless of whether the award was made before or after the precedent.


OUTCOME: The court refused to overturn the precedent, reversed one appellate court judgment, and affirmed two other judgments in accordance with the precedent.

Guider v. Smith

OVERVIEW: The arrestee filed a complaint against the city and the officer alleging that the officer violated his federal civil rights by using excessive force when making the arrest. People v. Finley


Docket No. 81221


Supreme Court of Michigan


431 Mich. 506; 431 N.W.2d 19; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 1696


May 4, 1988, Argued  
October 17, 1988, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied post, 1211.


PRIOR HISTORY: 161 Mich App 1; 410 NW2d 282 (1987).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which declined to reverse defendant's conviction for first-degree criminal sexual conduct in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.520b(1) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.788(2)(1)), and armed robbery in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.529 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.797), and held that an issue of improper impeachment by prior convictions was not preserved for review.


OVERVIEW: A trial court convicted defendant of first-degree criminal sexual conduct in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.520b(1) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.788(2)(1)), and armed robbery in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.529 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.797). A lower court declined to reverse defendant's conviction and held that an issue of improper impeachment by prior convictions was not preserved for review. Defendant appealed the lower court's decision The court affirmed, holding that review of the impeachment issue would have been preserved if defendant had in fact testified at trial, or if defendant had expressed his intention to testify in the event that his prior record was excluded and had stated the nature of his expected testimony. Because defendant did not testify, nor express his intention to testify, he waived review of the issue. The court rejected defendant's argument that the Michigan rule requiring the impeachment question to be decided before the accused took the stand weakened the rationale of the rule adopted by the court. The court held that defendant was required to testify in order to preserve for review the issue of improper impeachment by prior convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a lower court's decision, which declined to reverse defendant's conviction for first-degree criminal sexual conduct and armed robbery and held that an issue of improper impeachment by prior conviction was not preserved for review because defendant waived review of the issue of improper impeachment by prior conviction where he did not testify, nor express his intention to testify or state the nature of his expected testimony.

Union Carbide Corp. v. Public Service Com.


Docket Nos. 79148, 79150


Supreme Court of Michigan


431 Mich. 135; 428 N.W.2d 322; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 1407


March 8, 1988, Argued  
August 23, 1988, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: 153 Mich App 217; 395 NW2d 292 (1986).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed the trial court's judgment and reinstated the decision of appellee commission, finding that the commission had acted properly and within its authority in ordering appellant utility to cease the noneconomic operation of certain units and to limit acceptance of oil deliveries from a corporation to those necessary for economic operation. The utility appealed.


OVERVIEW: Shortly after the utility applied for an increase in electric rates, the commission noted that it had commenced a broad investigation into the utility's practices. The commission thereafter exercised its authority to regulate the utility's rates and charges by precluding the utility's recovery of those increased charges incurred as a result of the utility's noneconomic operation of certain units. Although it did not challenge the above action, the utility did argue that the commission exceeded its ratemaking authority by ordering the utility to cease certain noneconomic management practices. The state supreme court agreed, holding that the commission lacked statutory authority to order the utility to stop operating certain units out of economic order and to limit its acceptance of oil deliveries from the corporation to those required for economic operation. The statutory provisions offered by the commission either did not apply to the facts of the case or did not confer the power to make management decisions with the same degree of specificity that the state legislature had used in conferring upon the commission the authority to set utility rates.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment and reinstated the trial court's judgment.

State Bd. of Education v. Houghton Lake Community Schools


Docket No. 80686


Supreme Court of Michigan


430 Mich. 658; 425 N.W.2d 80; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 1118


March 9, 1988, Argued  
June 27, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 157 Mich App 396; 403 NW2d 561 (1987).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor state attorney general sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which upheld an order of the circuit court that denied appellant state board of education's request for a writ of mandamus that would have compelled appellee local school and local school board to provide 180 days of instruction in the school year.


OVERVIEW: The local school board approved a decision by the local school to not make snow days. The state board of education filed a complaint for mandamus and alleged that the local school was not in compliance with state law, which compelled 180 days of instruction. The circuit court denied the writ, and the state board of education appealed to the appellate court. The appellate court affirmed the trial court, and the state attorney general sought leave in the court. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court's decision. The court found that § 1284 of the School Code of 1976 and § 101 of the School Aid Act of 1979 provided only a financial "carrot" to school districts who complied with the 180-day instructional standard. The court held that there was no clear legal duty on the part of local districts to provide 180 days of instruction, and those districts that were willing to forego state financial aid might exercise their discretion to provide a lesser number. The court found it unnecessary to address the local school and the local board's argument that it had in fact complied with the 180 standard and did not address any constitutional issues which were raised.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision, which upheld the circuit court's order that denied the state board of education's request for a writ of mandamus, and adopted a statutory analysis similar to that of the circuit court. The court found no clear legal duty on the part of the local school and the local school board to provide 180 days of instruction.

Williams v. Hofley Mfg. Co.

Amend. XIV and Mich. Const. art. 1, § 17.


OVERVIEW: An employee was awarded disability benefits by the Bureau of Workers' Disability Compensation (bureau). The employer filed an appeal of the decision contesting the bureau's findings regarding the injury, the date of injury, and the effect of the employer's offer of favored work. In re Finlay Estate


Docket No. 79783


Supreme Court of Michigan


430 Mich. 590; 424 N.W.2d 272; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 1002


December 8, 1987, Argued  
June 7, 1988, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal Denied October 19, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: 154 Mich App 350; 397 NW2d 307 (1986).

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, sisters of the decedent, sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed a probate court decision ordering intestate distribution of the decedent's estate. The appellate court held that the estate's residue passed under the terms of the will to appellees, the children of the decedent's ex-husband.


OVERVIEW: The decedent's pre-divorce will named the children as the residual beneficiary of her will, which was not changed by the decedent prior to her death. The sisters argued that the decedent's divorce decree revoked the decedent's will and, under the former probate code, caused the children's rights to be extinguished.
