Laws § 700.992(a); (3) Mich. Comp. Law § 700.992(c) was inapplicable because no court "act" was impaired and the parties did not acquire any rights in the estate until the decedent died; and (4) the parties' stipulation to apply the former probate code did not bind a court.

126 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ramos


Docket No. 76612


Supreme Court of Michigan


430 Mich. 544; 424 N.W.2d 509; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 1007


October 9, 1986, Argued  
June 7, 1988, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his convictions for perjury and welfare fraud in favor of appellee state pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.423 (Mich. Stat. Ann. 28.665); Mich. Comp. Laws § 400.60(1) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 16.460(1)); and the Social Welfare Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 400.1 et seq (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 16.425), rendered in the trial court (Michigan).


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of his convictions for welfare fraud and perjury in favor of appellee state. Appellant contended his perjury conviction for filing a false application for aid to dependent children was in error because an element of perjury, an oath, was not administered. The court affirmed appellant's welfare fraud conviction and reversed the perjury conviction. The court explained reversal was required because administration of an oath was an element of perjury under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.423 (Mich. Stat. Ann. 28.665), and an oath was not administered to appellant when he simply signed the application. The court explained the signing of the application even under written penalty of perjury did not constitute an "oath." The court reasoned the form of an "oath" sufficient to be the basis of a perjury conviction included an oral administration, acknowledgement of the oath, and the raising of the oathtaker's right hand. The court observed this format was designed for the purpose of being distinct and recognizable enough to alert the oathtaker to the assumption of the obligation and responsibility to be truthful.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's welfare fraud conviction in favor of appellee state and reversed perjury conviction based on the filing of a false application of aid to dependent children because an element of perjury was an oath and the signing of the application, even under written penalty of perjury, was insufficient to constitute an oath which required oral administration and raising of the right hand to signify acknowledgment.

Hadfield v. Oakland County Drain Comm'r


Docket Nos. 75494, 76815, 77011, 78233


Supreme Court of Michigan


430 Mich. 139; 422 N.W.2d 205; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 358


November 14, 1986, Argued  
March 29, 1988, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied in Landry, post, 1202.


PRIOR HISTORY: 143 Mich App 16; 371 NW2d 466 (1985).

 143 Mich App 694; 373 NW2d 193 (1985).



DISPOSITION: Hadfield, affirmed and remanded for further proceedings. Veeneman and Landry, reversed. McCaul, affirmed and remanded for trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In four consolidated cases, plaintiffs, landowners, a survivor, and witnesses, filed suit against defendant governments and alleged nuisance, among other things. Three cases resulted in summary judgments in favor of the governments. The court of appeals (Michigan) reversed. In one case, the landowners were awarded damages, and the court of appeals affirmed. Appeals were taken.


OVERVIEW: In two cases, water flowed onto landowners' land, allegedly because of the governments' conduct. In the other cases, the survivor's decedent was fatally injured in a dune buggy accident in the government's park, and the witnesses were injured by a knife attack in the government's courtroom. The landowners, survivor, and witnesses claimed that the government was not immune from suit for nuisance. The governments claimed that § 7 of the governmental tort liability act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.1407 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.996(107)), confined governmental liability to the exceptions specified in the statute. The court held that there was a limited exception to governmental immunity under § 7 for trespass-nuisance, or interference with the use or enjoyment of land caused by a physical intrusion resulting in damage. The landowners' claims fell within the trespass-nuisance exception, but the trial court erred in one case by failing to make adequate findings of fact under Mich. Gen. Ct. R. 517.1 (1963) regarding the award of damages. In the dune buggy accident and courtroom attack cases, no basis was alleged for holding the governments liable under the trespass-nuisance exception.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reversal of summary judgment in one landowners' case and remanded the case for trial. The court reversed the rulings of the court of appeals in the survivor's and witnesses' cases and held that the entry of summary judgment in favor of the governments was proper. The court remanded the case of the landowners who were awarded damages for further findings of fact and a damage award based on those findings.

Advisory Opinion on Constitutionality of 1986 PA 281


Docket No. 80210


Supreme Court of Michigan


430 Mich. 93; 422 N.W.2d 186; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 357


June 2, 1987, Argued  
March 22, 1988, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to Mich. Const. art 3, § 8 (1963), the governor and the state senate requested an advisory opinion on two particular questions regarding the constitutionality of certain provisions of 1986 Mich. Pub. Act 281, Mich. Comp. Laws. § 125.2151 et seq.; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.540(351) et seq., which entitled the Local Development Financing Act (LDFA).


OVERVIEW: The questions presented to the court inquired whether the capture of revenue by a local development finance authority and use of that revenue by the authority for purposes authorized by the LDFA unconstitutionally diverted tax revenues from the taxing entities in violation of Mich. Const. art. 9, § 6, or if that same capture of revenue unconstitutionally lent the credit of the state in violation of Mich. Const. art. 9, § 18, or art. 7, §26. However, the court did caution that its decision was not based on the policy merits of tax increment financing and that its holdings were limited. Further, the court warned that the particular facts of future litigation, or questions premised upon other constitutional provisions or questions of statutory construction not presented in the present request for advisory opinion, might present different problems that were not addressed here.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the proposed public act was constitutional with respect to the limited questions posed by the governor and senate.

 

129 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Allen


Docket Nos. 70740, 71633, 72346, 72753, 73297


Supreme Court of Michigan


429 Mich. 558; 420 N.W.2d 499; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 518; 56 U.S.L.W. 2482


October 7, 1986, Argued  
January 19, 1988, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Amended February 16, 1988, post, p 1216. Rehearing Denied February 16, 1988.
Motion granted by People v. Pedrin, 430 Mich. 1201, 423 N.W.2d 215, 1988 Mich. LEXIS 698 (1988)


PRIOR HISTORY: 130 Mich App 86; 343 NW2d 243 (1983).
People v. Gray, 422 Mich. 972, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2404 (1985)
People v. Smith, 422 Mich. 973, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2455 (1985)
People v. Allen, 422 Mich. 972, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2501 (1985)
People v. Pedrin, 130 Mich. App. 86, 343 N.W.2d 243, 1983 Mich. App. LEXIS 3402 (1983)
People v. Brooks, 341 N.W.2d 468, 1983 Mich. LEXIS 289 (Mich., 1983)



DISPOSITION: Allen and Gray, reversed and remanded. Brooks and Smith, affirmed. Pedrin, judgment order held in abeyance April 26, 1988. See 430 Mich 1201.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In unrelated cases, five defendants each testified on their own behalf and were convicted by the trial courts (Michigan). In consolidated appeals, they sought review of their convictions and challenged the trial courts' admissions of their respective prior convictions to impeach their testimony pursuant Mich. R. Evid. 609(a).


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court reversed as to three defendants because the prejudice of the prior convictions outweighed the probative value. The court affirmed as to two defendants because the erroneous admission of the prior convictions was harmless. Where a conviction turned on the conflicting testimony of the accused and the accuser, knowledge of a previous conviction would bias a jury against a defendant's testimony. The court found that Rule 609(a) undermined a defendant's right to testify and hindered a jury's ability to evaluate his credibility as a witness. The court amended the rule to provide a bright line rule that prior convictions were not admissible unless the crime contained either an element of dishonesty or false statement or an element of theft and was punishable by imprisonment in excess of one year or death and the probative value outweighed its prejudicial effect. The clarified balancing test required an objective analysis of probativeness and a consideration of the importance of defendant's testimony to the decisional process. The amendment applied prospectively, and the court reviewed the instant cases using the new balancing test, but not using the bright-line rule.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the convictions of three defendants because the erroneous admission of the prior convictions outweighed the probative value. The court affirmed the convictions of two defendants because the erroneous admission of the prior convictions was harmless. The court amended the rule of evidence, which provided for the admission of prior convictions.

 

130 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Beach


Docket Nos. 75058, 76536


Supreme Court of Michigan


429 Mich. 450; 418 N.W.2d 861; 1988 Mich. LEXIS 26


October 7, 1986, Argued  
January 19, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Beach, 422 Mich. 977, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2427 (1985)
People v. Edwards, 422 Mich. 977, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2503 (1985)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged two separate judgments by the trial court (Michigan), which convicted them of first-degree murder and attempted murder in the second-degree and conspiracy to commit armed robbery, respectively. Both defendants argued that instructions on cognate lesser included offenses should have been given to the jury.


OVERVIEW: Two defendants argued on appeal that instructions on cognate lesser included offenses should have been given to the jury in their respective trials. For the defendant convicted of first-degree murder, the court held that the trial court properly did not give the cognate lesser offense instruction for involuntary manslaughter because it was not supported by the evidence. An element of involuntary manslaughter was the commission of an act not amounting to a felony and not naturally tending to cause death or great bodily harm. The court found this element missing because defendant had burned a dwelling house while residents were sleeping. For the defendant convicted of conspiracy to commit armed robbery, the court held that an instruction on the cognate lesser offense of conspiracy to commit larceny in a building was supported by the evidence, but the failure to give such an instruction was harmless. The court noted that the jury had the option to convict on the lesser included offense of conspiracy to commit unarmed robbery. The court held that the jury's conviction, when it had a lesser option, showed that the jury had no doubt about defendant's guilt on the armed robbery charge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions, finding no reversible error in their trials. One defendant was not entitled to a cognate lesser offense instruction because the evidence did not support the instruction. The other defendant was entitled to a cognate offense instruction, but the failure to give such an instruction was harmless.

 

131 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


El Souri v. Department of Social Services


Docket No. 78544


Supreme Court of Michigan


429 Mich. 203; 414 N.W.2d 679; 1987 Mich. LEXIS 8899


May 5, 1987, Argued  
October 23, 1987, Decided  
October 23, 1987, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 150 Mich App 380; 388 NW2d 702 (1986).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Michigan Department of Social Services (DSS), challenged a judgment from the appellate court (Michigan), which affirmed the judgment of the trial court reversing a judgment from the DSS denying appellee claimant benefits after the DSS held that the claimant was not denied public assistance benefits on the basis of being a resident alien per se, but on the basis of excess income imputed from the claimant's sponsor.


OVERVIEW: A DSS policy stated that the income and assets of an alien's sponsor had to be considered in determining benefits eligibility. The claimant argued that the policy created a classification based on alienage, which violated the Michigan and the United States Constitutions. The appellate court agreed with the trial court that the DSS policy in question violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution, but did not address whether the policy also violated the Michigan Constitution. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court. First, the court held that the policy was subject to strict judicial scrutiny since the policy, by its very terms, was directed at sponsored resident aliens. Next, the court stated that the policy did not survive strict scrutiny because it had not been shown that the means adopted to achieve the goal of the policy were necessary and precisely drawn. Thus, the court concluded that the policy unconstitutionally discriminated against resident aliens. Accordingly, the court affirmed the appellate court's judgment and stated that the court did not need to reach the state constitutional issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court finding that the policy violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of judgments from a trial court (Michigan), which sentenced them on felony convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were both convicted of felony offenses, and on resentencing, the trial court increased their sentences. Smith v. State


Docket Nos. 71016, 76838


Supreme Court of Michigan


428 Mich. 540; 410 N.W.2d 749; 1987 Mich. LEXIS 8323


March 6, 1986, Argued  
August 7, 1987, Decided  
August 7, 1987, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearings denied October 12, 1987, 429 Mich 1207.


PRIOR HISTORY: 122 Mich App 340; 333 NW2d 50 (1983).

 145 Mich App 214; 377 NW2d 826 (1985).
Smith v. State, 122 Mich. App. 340, 333 N.W.2d 50, 1983 Mich. App. LEXIS 2780 (1983)
Will v. Department of Civil Service, 145 Mich. App. 214, 377 N.W.2d 826, 1985 Mich. App. LEXIS 2900 (1985)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two consolidated cases, plaintiff individuals appealed from judgments of the Michigan Court of Appeals which disallowed their claims under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 on the ground that the State was not a "person" for purposes of a damage suit under § 1983.


OVERVIEW: Two individuals brought claims against the State under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. In the first case, a state employee alleged that he was not promoted because of the political activities of his brother. In the second case, an individual, born to a woman committed to a mental hospital in 1925, had been shuffled between state institutions for 48 years. XIV. In a terse opinion, the Supreme Court of Michigan held that the State and its officials, sued in their official capacities, were not "persons" in a suit for damages under § 1983, that there was no intentional tort exception under the governmental immunity act, and that there was no implicit right to sue the State for damages on the basis of violations of Mich. Const. art 2, §§ 1, 16 (1908).


OUTCOME: The judgments were affirmed in part because the state and officials were entitled to summary judgment dismissing the claims under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The judgments were reversed in part and remanded for a determination whether a violation of the Michigan Constitution by virtue of a governmental custom or policy had occurred and, if so, whether it was one for which a damage remedy was proper.

Kent County Prosecutor v. Kent County Sheriff


Docket No. 74055


Supreme Court of Michigan


428 Mich. 314; 409 N.W.2d 202; 1987 Mich. LEXIS 8194


January 13, 1987, Argued  
July 1, 1987, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This matter was before the court on a motion for rehearing filed by intervening appellant defender's office to reconsider the court's earlier decision regarding the constitutionality of the County Jail Overcrowding Act, 1982 Mich. Pub. Act 325, Mich. Comp. Laws § 801.51 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1748 (1) et seq.), which the lower court had declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellee county prosecutor argued that, the effect of the County Jail Overcrowding Act , regardless of its purpose, resulted in the usurpation of the executive power to commute sentences in violation of Const. 1963, art 4, § 14. The court rejected the county prosecutor's argument and reversed the lower court's decision and affirmed the trial court's decision regarding constitutionality of the statute. The court noted that the statute in question authorized sentence reductions only in response to generalized conditions of the jail or jails within a county and not by the unique characteristics that authorize commutations of individual prisoners by the executive branch of government. The court also noted that the legislature had the power to incidentally reduce determinate sentences because the statute ensured enforcement of the regulations governing maintenance of jails had implemented pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 791.262 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.2322). Finally, the statute properly avoided cruel and unusual punishment concerns along with potential liability for violation of prisoners' civil rights under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment that determined that the statute was unconstitutional.

 

136 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


McKinstry v. Valley Obstetrics-Gynecology Clinic, P.C.


Docket Nos. 77531, 78140


Supreme Court of Michigan


428 Mich. 167; 405 N.W.2d 88; 1987 Mich. LEXIS 7796


January 14, 1987, Argued  
May 8, 1987, Decided  
May 8, 1987, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 146 Mich App 307; 380 NW2d 93 (1985).

 149 Mich App 420; 385 NW2d 805 (1986).
McKinstry v. Valley Obstetrics-Gynecology Clinic, P.C., 146 Mich. App. 307, 380 N.W.2d 93, 1985 Mich. App. LEXIS 2961 (1985)
Guertin v. Marrella, 149 Mich. App. 420, 385 N.W.2d 805, 1986 Mich. App. LEXIS 2433 (1986)



DISPOSITION: McKinstry, affirmed. Guertin, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, mother and child, challenged a Michigan appeals court order enforcing an arbitration agreement with appellee providers under the Medical Malpractice Arbitration Act (MMAA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.5040 et seq.; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 27A.5040 et seq., while other appellants, also providers, challenged a different appellate order requiring providers to prove the validity of an agreement with appellee surgical patient.


OVERVIEW: Lower courts differed as to the manner in which a statutory presumption of enforceability of an agreement to arbitrate claims arising from health care operated, as well as to the evidence that was required to rebut the presumption. In one case, the lower court had imposed the burden of proving invalidity on the claimant while another lower court imposed the burden of proving invalidity on the medical provider. On review, the court held that the burden of establishing the existence of an agreement that conformed to the strict MMAA requirements rested with the party seeking enforcement. Once prima facie evidence of the existence and complying character of the agreement was presented, a statutory presumption of validity accrued, and the burden of going forward with evidence to rebut that presumption shifted to the party seeking to avoid enforcement. The court also held that execution of such an agreement by the parent of an unborn child bound the child, after birth, to arbitrate disputes arising out of prenatal care and delivery, and the enforcement of such agreements did not violate the constitutional right to a jury trial because no such absolute right existed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an appellate court order holding that both a mother and child were bound by an arbitration agreement signed by the mother prior to the child's birth, while reversing an appellate court order denying enforcement of an arbitration agreement as to a malpractice claim brought by a surgical patient.

 

137 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Olmstead v. Anderson


Docket No. 77271


Supreme Court of Michigan


428 Mich. 1; 400 N.W.2d 292; 1987 Mich. LEXIS 7132


November 13, 1986, Argued  
February 6, 1987, Decided  
February 6, 1987, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 145 Mich App 160; 377 NW2d 853 (1985).
Olmstead v. Anderson, 145 Mich. App. 160, 377 N.W.2d 853, 1985 Mich. App. LEXIS 2783 (1985)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff administrator brought a wrongful death action against defendant administrator. Under the doctrine of lex loci delicti, the trial court found that Wisconsin law governed. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed, holding that Michigan law applied under the doctrine of lex fori. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The case arose out of an automobile accident occurring in Wisconsin. Plaintiff's decedents, Minnesota citizens, were struck and killed by defendant's decedent, a Michigan resident. Wisconsin law limited damages for wrongful death to $ 25,000, but Michigan did not limit recoverable damages. The state supreme court held that the law of Michigan, the forum state and defendant's state of residence, was applicable. The court saw no rational reason to displace Michigan law because applying lex loci delicti would not promote the reputed advantages of the doctrine and because Wisconsin had no interest in seeing its law applied. Because there was no reason to apply Wisconsin law, it was therefore unnecessary to undertake an analysis of the interests of Michigan. Furthermore, no unfairness would result to the parties in applying Michigan law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision.

Gackler Land Co. v. Yankee Springs Twp.


Docket No. 75246


Supreme Court of Michigan


427 Mich. 562; 398 N.W.2d 393; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 5728


November 12, 1985, Argued  
December 30, 1986, Decided  
December 30, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 138 Mich App 1; 359 NW2d 226 (1984).
Gackler Land Co. v. Yankee Springs Township, 138 Mich. App. 1, 359 N.W.2d 226, 1984 Mich. App. LEXIS 2959 (1984)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner appealed the order of the Court of Appeals ( Michigan), which affirmed the decision of the trial court that upheld the zoning ordinances of defendants, township and homeowners' association (township). The landowner argued that the zoning ordinance was unconstitutional, that he had established a vested nonconforming use as a single-wide mobile home plat, and that the zoning ordinance was preempted by federal and state law.


OVERVIEW: When he bought the property, the landowner was able to use it for mobile, prefabricated, and site-built homes. After the property was platted, restrictions were recorded that excluded mobile homes. However 11 single-wide mobile homes had moved onto some of the lots on the plat. The court affirmed and held that the homeowner did not establish that the ordinance was unconstitutional on its face or as applied. The ordinance was reasonable. The landowner did not establish confiscation or a nonconforming use or that enforcement of the ordinance would preclude use of the land for a purpose to which it was reasonably adapted. The zoning ordinance was not preempted by the National Manufactured Housing Construction and Safety Standards Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 5401 et seq., or Mich. Comp. Laws §§ 125.1519, 125.1520 (Mich. Stat. Ann. §§ 5.2949(19), 5.2949(20)), or the Mobile Home Commission Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's affirmance of the trial court's decision that upheld the constitutionality of a zoning ordinance that restricted the landowner's use of his property by requiring that single-wide mobile homes meet certain requirements.

People v. Catania


Docket Nos. 76742, 76743


Supreme Court of Michigan


427 Mich. 447; 398 N.W.2d 343; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 5726


April 10, 1986, Argued  
December 30, 1986, Decided  
December 30, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing, denied 428 Mich 1206.
Rehearing denied by People v. Catania, 428 Mich. 1206, 1987 Mich. LEXIS 7804 (1987)
On remand at People v. Catania, 159 Mich. App. 57, 406 N.W.2d 473, 1987 Mich. App. LEXIS 2391 (1987)


PRIOR HISTORY: 140 Mich App 755; 366 NW2d 38 (1985).
People v. Catania, 140 Mich. App. 755, 366 N.W.2d 38, 1985 Mich. App. LEXIS 2498 (1985)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed a decision of the Michigan Court of Appeals that probable cause was required to support a ruse entry into a defendant's home, requiring reversal of a conviction based upon evidence resulting from the entry.


OVERVIEW: Using false pretenses, an agent gained entry into defendant's home. Based upon evidence obtained as a result of that entry, defendant was convicted of possession of drugs with intent to deliver. In reversing the ruling that probable cause was required before such an entry, the court held that the agent's entry into defendant's home without a warrant was not a search under either the Fourth Amendment of the United States Constitution or Mich. Const. art 1, § 11 (1963). The agent entered the home with permission, albeit for a hidden purpose, and did not exceed the scope of the invitation extended. There was no forcible entry effected or attempted exercise of governmental authority or power. Defendant had no reasonable expectation of privacy to the extent he exposed his activities to the agent. Because no search occurred, no warrant was required, and no showing of cause was necessary.


OUTCOME: Because it found no violation of the Michigan and United States Constitutions in a ruse entry into the defendant's home, the court reversed the decision and reinstated defendant's conviction, remanding the case for consideration of other issues raised by defendant.

Supreme Court of Michigan

(MSA) §§ 28.354(1), .548), and aiding and abetting an assault with intent to commit murder, MCL § 750.83 (MSA § 28.278), but remanded for resentencing, finding that a life sentence, while within the sentencing discretion of the trial court, was not mandatory. OVERVIEW: Unlike his wife, defendant did not want a divorce. After they separated, defendant threatened to kill the wife or throw acid in her face. Finally, the court held that a mandatory life sentence, even assuming arguendo that it was nonparolable, imposed for conspiracy to commit first-degree murder did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment, nor did it violate the Equal Protection Clauses of the Michigan or United States Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the lower court's judgment.

Rouch v. Enquirer & News


Docket No. 75044


Supreme Court of Michigan


427 Mich. 157; 398 N.W.2d 245; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 5725; 13 Media L. Rep. 2201


January 15, 1986, Argued  
December 26, 1986, Decided  
December 26, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 428 Mich 1207.


PRIOR HISTORY: 137 Mich App 39; 357 NW2d 794 (1984).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed and remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff suspect brought an action against defendant newspaper alleging false newspaper defamation. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the newspaper. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) reversed the trial court and held that there was no federal constitutional privilege to report on matters of public interest and that the suspect only had to show that the newspaper was negligent in printing the article. The newspaper appealed.


OVERVIEW: The suspect was arrested but not formally charged for rape. The newspaper published an article stating the suspect had been arrested and charged. The newspaper reporter obtained his information by telephoning the police department in the morning to receive information about newsworthy police activities during the preceding 24 hours. Upon review, the court concluded that an arrest that amounted to no more than an apprehension was not a proceeding under Mich. Comp. Law § 600.2911(3), Mich. Stat. Ann. § 27A.2911(3). The court held that the information orally furnished to the newspaper in support of its article did not enjoy the privilege afforded by the public and official proceeding statute. The court held that the newspaper report of the suspect's arrest was speech of public concern and that therefore the newspaper was not required to prove the truth of the article at its defense but rather the suspect had to prove that the statements at issue were false in addition to the newspaper's fault. The court concluded that the report of the arrest and the facts used to establish the probable cause for the arrest amounted to speech of public concern.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court reversing the trial court's grant of summary judgment and remanded the case for further proceedings.

Parks v. Employment Sec. Com.


Docket Nos. 73010, 75474, 75964


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


427 Mich. 224; 398 N.W.2d 275; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 5731


June 5, 1986, Argued  
December 26, 1986, Decided  
December 26, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari denied in Parks by the Supreme Court of the United States on May 18, 1987, 481 U.S.   ; 95 L Ed 2d 838 (1987).
Writ of certiorari denied Parks v. Michigan Employment Sec. Com., 481 U.S. 1050, 107 S. Ct. 2182, 95 L. Ed. 2d 838, 1987 U.S. LEXIS 2117 (1987)


PRIOR HISTORY: 139 Mich App 515; 362 NW2d 771 (1984).
Parks v. Employment Sec. Comm'n, 423 Mich. 854, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 1755 (1985)



DISPOSITION: Parks and Lindquist, affirmed. Dolce, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees and appellee Michigan Employment Security Commission (Commission) and employers challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which, in consolidated actions, affirmed the denial of unemployment benefits to the employees under the disqualifying provisions of § 29 of the Michigan Employment Security Act. Each employee denied that they engaged in misconduct or voluntarily left their employment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employees were denied unemployment benefits pursuant to § 29 of the Michigan Employment Security Act and claimed that they had neither engaged in job related misconduct nor had left voluntarily. One was fired when she refused to pay union shop fees, another was discharged for not complying with her employer's residency requirement, and the third was discharged when he reached the mandatory retirement age. The court held that failure to comply with the residency requirement was misconduct within the statute because the employer had an interest in and had the right to expect that its employees would abide by the residency requirement. In addition, failure to pay the union fees was clearly a willful disregard of the employer's interest that would have resulted in serious union problems for it had the employee not been discharged. However, the court held that simply growing old was neither "voluntary" nor "misconduct" under the statute, and that compulsory retirement was a discharge based on factors beyond the employee's control. The employee's collective bargaining agreement that required mandatory retirement was too remote to be interpreted as a "voluntary leaving."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court as to appellant employees discharged for failing to pay union shop fees and for failing to comply with appellant employer's residency requirement but reversed the judgment as to the appellant employee who was denied unemployment benefits when he was forced to retire because growing old was neither voluntary nor misconduct under the Michigan Employment Security Act.

People v. Johnson


Docket No. 75775


Supreme Court of Michigan


427 Mich. 98; 398 N.W.2d 219; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 5729


April 10, 1986, Argued  
December 26, 1986, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 428 Mich 1206.
Rehearing denied by People v. Johnson, 428 Mich. 1206, 1987 Mich. LEXIS 7802 (1987)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Johnson, 422 Mich. 974, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2507 (1985)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) affirming his conviction for second-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with open murder at the preliminary hearing and later convicted of second-degree murder. After the appeals court affirmed the conviction, defendant sought further review based on the State's failure to specify the degree of murder charged at the preliminary hearing. The court first noted that the right to a preliminary hearing was not constitutionally-based, but rather arose from Mich. Comp. Laws § 766.1766 (Mich. Stat. Ann. §§ 28.919-28.940). The court then held that neither statute nor case law required specification of the degree of murder at a preliminary examination where open murder was charged in the information. Because Mich. Comp. Laws § 767.71 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1011) did not require specification of the degree of murder in the information, such detail was not required at the earlier preliminary hearing stage. The court also held that the State's failure to specify the degree of murder at the preliminary hearing did not violate defendant's Due Process Clause or Equal Protection Clause rights, and that sufficient evidence supported the premeditation and deliberation rulings at the preliminary hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment upholding defendant's conviction for second-degree murder.

Automatic Music & Vending Corp. v. Liquor Control Com.


Docket No. 76285


Supreme Court of Michigan


426 Mich. 452; 396 N.W.2d 204; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 5434


October 9, 1986, Argued  
November 24, 1986, Decided  
November 24, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Automatic Music & Vending Corp. v. Michigan Liquor Control Com., 481 U.S. 1009, 107 S. Ct. 1880, 95 L. Ed. 2d 488, 1987 U.S. LEXIS 1599 (1987)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 141 Mich App 458; 367 NW2d 413 (1985).
Automatic Music & Vending Corp. v. Liquor Control Com., 141 Mich. App. 458, 367 N.W.2d 413, 1985 Mich. App. LEXIS 2537 (1985)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Michigan Liquor Control Commission challenged an order of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed an order in plaintiff vending company's action to review the commission's actions that held that a "draw poker" video machine was not an illegal gaming device under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.303.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court affirmed a judgment that held that the vending company's "draw poker" video machine was not an illegal gaming device. The appellate court also concluded that Mich. Comp. Law § 750.303(2) was unconstitutional. The court reversed that judgment on appeal. The court concluded that the machine was a gaming device under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.303 because the award of a free game constituted a reward or a prize because it had value, noting that it cost money to play a game. The court further determined that the machine did not qualify for the exemption under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.303(2) because it allowed more than 15 replays to accumulate, because there was a button that could have discharged all of the free replays, and because a permanent record was kept of the number of the free replays that were awarded. The court also found that a rational basis could have been found for the legislature's decision to pick a particular number as the dividing line for the number of replays that were allowed under the exemption in Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.303(2) and that § 750.303(2)'s comprehensible language was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment that held that gambling statute was unconstitutional and that affirmed a judgment that held that the "draw poker" video machine was not an illegal gaming device.

 

147 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Sheridan Rd. Baptist Church v. Department of Educ.


Docket No. 73665


Supreme Court of Michigan


426 Mich. 462; 396 N.W.2d 373; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 5528


November 13, 1985, Argued  
November 24, 1986, Decided  
November 24, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 427 Mich 1202. Leave of Appeal Denied March 17, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: 132 Mich App 1; 348 NW2d 263 (1984).
Sheridan Rd. Baptist Church v. Dep't of Educ., 132 Mich. App. 1, 348 N.W.2d 263, 1984 Mich. App. LEXIS 2438 (1984)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeals is affirmed by an equally divided Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, schools, pastors, faculty members, and parents, brought an action against defendants, state and its officials, alleging that Mich. Comp. Laws § 388.551 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. 15.1921 et seq.), violated U.S. Const. amends. I, IX, XIV and Mich. Const. art. 1, § 4, art. 8, § 1. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) held that the statutes were constitutional. The schools, pastors, faculty members, and parents appealed.


OVERVIEW: The schools, pastors, faculty members, and parents refused to provide the state with information on student enrollment and teacher qualifications. The schools, pastors, faculty members, and parents argued that the statutory regulations were unconstitutional. The state argued that it had a compelling interest in the education of its citizens. The court concluded that: (1) the state's interest in ensuring that all teachers met certain minimum requirements outweighed the slight burden imposed on the free exercise of religion; (2) the teacher certification requirements were not discriminatory and did not require adherence to any particular religious beliefs; (3) there was no Establishment Clause violation because the regulations did not lead to excessive entanglement between church and state; (4) there was no evidence that the schools could not find acceptable certified teachers; (5) the infringement on free exercise rights was minimal; (6) there was no evidence that any applicant who had fulfilled the objective requirements had been denied a certificate due to religious beliefs; and (7) there was no less intrusive method for the state to satisfy its interest in educating its citizens.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment from the appellate court holding that the statutory provisions regarding teacher certification did not violate the Free Exercise or Establishment Clauses of the First Amendment.

Koski v. Vohs


Docket No. 75140


Supreme Court of Michigan


426 Mich. 424; 395 N.W.2d 226; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 5397


January 15, 1986, Argued  
November 10, 1986, Decided  
November 10, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 428 Mich 1201. Leave of Appeal Denied February 3, 1987.
Rehearing denied by Koski v. Vohs, 428 Mich. 1201, 1987 Mich. LEXIS 7798 (1987)


PRIOR HISTORY: 137 Mich App 491; 358 NW2d 620 (1984).
Koski v. Vohs, 137 Mich. App. 491, 358 N.W.2d 620, 1984 Mich. App. LEXIS 2918 (1984)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant investigator appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) that reversed the trial court's directed verdict for defendants as to the investigator only. Plaintiff publisher's agent suit alleged malicious prosecution against seven defendants: five police officers, the county prosecutor, and the prosecutor's investigator.


OVERVIEW: The agent entered into a contract with the police officers association (POA) to publish a police magazine. Advertisements were sold to local businesses, all revenues were deposited in the POA account, and the agent was paid separately. Two years later, several police officers met with prosecutors to allege that the agent was converting checks made out to the POA. After investigation, charges were authorized but were later dismissed when the POA witness failed to appear. The agent brought suit for malicious prosecution, and the trial court directed a verdict for the defendants, including the investigator. The appellate court affirmed except as to the investigator, whose case was remanded to determine whether he had probable cause to bring the charges against the agent when he had knowledge of the agent's assertion of his right to the funds. The court reversed the appellate court, finding that the investigator did have probable cause because there were other facts which disputed the agent's defense. In addition, the investigator had disclosed the agent's defense to the prosecutor. The court ruled that the trial court's decision of the case as a matter of law was correct.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court with regard to the investigator and reinstated the trial court's order granting the defendants' motion for a directed verdict.

 

149 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Ferrario v. Escanaba Bd. of Education


Docket No. 75602


Supreme Court of Michigan


426 Mich. 353; 395 N.W.2d 195; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 5396


April 9, 1986, Argued  
October 31, 1986, Decided  
October 31, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Ferrario v. Escanaba Bd. of Educ., 422 Mich. 974, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2500 (1985)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded for review de novo on the merits.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school board challenged the judgment from a court of appeals (Michigan), which found substantial evidence to support a finding that appellee teacher's right to due process had been violated at his discharge hearing. The court of appeals granted the teacher back pay.


OVERVIEW: A teacher was discharged for alleged improper conduct with a student and was awarded back pay. The court reversed the judgment. While the court of appeals properly remanded the case to the Michigan State Tenure Commission (commission) for consideration of the merits, it erred in affirming the commission's finding of a due process violation and in awarding back pay to the teacher. The Michigan Teacher Tenure Act (act), Mich. Comp. Laws § 38.71 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 15.1971 et seq.), protected teachers from arbitrary and unreasonable practices. A discharge could be made only for reasonable and just cause and only after following the procedures established by the act. The court held that it was the duty of the commission to review the school board's findings de novo before ordering reinstatement or back pay. The court found that mere familiarity with the facts of a case gained by an agency in the performance of its statutory role did not disqualify a decisionmaker. The teacher was required to show that risk of unfairness was intolerably high.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment that found the teacher's due process rights violated. The court held that the tenure commission failed to properly review the record as to whether bias existed and that a de novo review was required before back pay could be ordered.

Schwartz v. Flint


Docket No. 70806


Supreme Court of Michigan


426 Mich. 295; 395 N.W.2d 678; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 5395


March 5, 1985, Argued  
October 28, 1986, Decided  
October 28, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by Schwartz v. Flint, 187 Mich. App. 191, 466 N.W.2d 357, 1991 Mich. App. LEXIS 38 (1991)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 120 Mich App 449; 329 NW2d 26 (1982).

 Ed Zaagman, Inc v Kentwood, 406 Mich 137; 277 NW2d 475 (1979).
Schwartz v. Flint, 120 Mich. App. 449, 329 N.W.2d 26, 1982 Mich. App. LEXIS 3557 (1982)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner requested that defendant city rezone his property. Both parties submitted proposals for rezoning. The trial court did not adopt either party's proposal but instead created its own solution prescribing different uses for various tiers. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) modified the trial court's order as to one of the tiers and affirmed the remainder of the order. The landowner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The landowner wanted his land rezoned. The city did not rezone it in the manner requested. The trial court under Zaagman developed its own solution. Upon review, the court held that the Zaagman procedure impermissibly offended the separation of powers by allowing a trial court to replace its judgment for that of local zoning boards. The court determined that the procedure established in Zaagman for providing relief in zoning cases once an ordinance had been found unconstitutional should be repudiated and replaced with a rule that better comported with the role of the judiciary and the separation of powers doctrine. The court found that after a zoning ordinance had been declared unconstitutional, in addition to that declaration, a trial court could provide relief in the form of a declaration that the landowner's proposed use was reasonable, assuming the landowner's burden had been met, and an injunction preventing the city from interfering with that use. The court further held that the city was always free to rezone consistent with the limiting conditions of the landowner's proposed use, or not so limited, where the landowner's use had not been declare reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court overruled Zaagman, reversed the order of both the appellate and trial courts, and remanded to the trial court for consideration of the question of relief under the new standard set forth.

Kent County Prosecutor v. Kent County Sheriff


Docket No. 74055


Supreme Court of Michigan


425 Mich. 718; 391 N.W.2d 341; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 4419


December 18, 1985, Argued  
August 7, 1986, Decided  
August 7, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing granted 426 Mich 1201.


PRIOR HISTORY: 133 Mich App 611; 350 NW2d 298 (1984).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county prosecutor brought an action against defendant county sheriff seeking to prevent utilization of the county jail overcrowding act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 801.51 et seq (Mich. Stat. Ann. 28.1748 et seq.). The county defendant intervened, and sought review of an order of Court of Appeals (Michigan), which had reversed the decision of the circuit court and declared the act unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The county jail overcrowding act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 801.51 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. 28.1748 et seq.), allowed the early release of prisoners serving jail sentences when the jail population exceeded a certain percentage of rated design capacity. The sheriff was authorized to declare a state of emergency. If the population was not reduced to 90 percent of capacity within 14 days after that declaration, the sheriff was required to reduce the sentences of prisoners who would not present a high risk to public safety. The prosecuting attorney brought an action against the sheriff seeking a declarative judgment that the act was unconstitutional, and sought an injunction barring release of prisoners before the expiration of their sentences. The office of the county defender intervened as a defendant. The preliminary injunction was denied by the circuit court, as was the request for declaratory relief. The court of appeals reversed those decisions, finding that the act was unconstitutional on the ground that it usurped the governor's power of commutation. On further review, four justices found that the act was unconstitutional; three found that it was constitutional.


OUTCOME: Three justices of the court concurred in an opinion that the county jail overcrowding act was unconstitutional, and four concurred in a separate opinion that the act was constitutional. Therefore, the ruling of the appellate court that the act was unconstitutional was affirmed.

Walker v. Wolverine Fabricating & Mfg. Co.


Docket No. 75335


Supreme Court of Michigan


425 Mich. 586; 391 N.W.2d 296; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 4423


November 13, 1985, Argued  
August 5, 1986, Decided  
August 5, 1986, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 138 Mich App 660; 360 NW2d 264 (1984).
Walker v. Wolverine Fabricating & Mfg. Co., 138 Mich. App. 660, 360 N.W.2d 264, 1984 Mich. App. LEXIS 3037 (1984)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former employer sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed and remanded the decision of the trial court affirming the decision of the Civil Rights Commission (CRC) dismissing appellee former employee's discrimination complaint against the former employer.


OVERVIEW: The intermediate appellate court held that the former employee was entitled to a new trial of his underlying discrimination claim before the trial court and that the proper party defendant in the suit was the former employer, the respondent named in the administrative complaint. The court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's judgment in part, reversed it in part, and remanded the case to the trial court. The court held that the proper party to defend against an appeal brought by the original complainant was the respondent named in the action before the Civil Rights Commission (CRC) and not the CRC itself. The court found that there was no common understanding of the phrase "tried de novo" in Mich. Const. art. 5, § 29 (1963), which provided for appeals from the CRC's final decisions. The court held that the intent of the constitution's framers would best be effected by requiring circuit courts to review final decisions of the CRC de novo by taking a fresh look at the evidence and testimony in the record produced before the commission and determining whether the CRC's factual findings and legal conclusions were supported by competent, material, and substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that part of the intermediate appellate court's judgment which held that the proper party to defend an appeal brought by an original complainant was the respondent named in the action before the CRC. The court reversed that part of the intermediate appellate court's judgment which held that the former employee was entitled to a new trial of his discrimination claims, and remanded the cause to the trial court.

Committee for Constitutional Reform v. Secretary of State


Docket No. 78117


Supreme Court of Michigan


425 Mich. 336; 389 N.W.2d 430; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 4405



July 11, 1986, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff ballot question committee sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan) in the committee's action against defendants, Michigan Secretary of State and Michigan Board of Canvassers, which held that Mich. Const. art. VI, § 2 (1963) did not require the legislature to provide for nonpartisan nomination of candidates for the Michigan Supreme Court.


OVERVIEW: The committee was a group consisting of members who sought to amend the Michigan Constitution to require that justices of the Michigan Supreme Court be nominated in nonpartisan primary elections. The committee brought an action for mandamus requesting the Court of Appeals to extend the provision of Mich. Const. art. VI, § 2 (1963) requiring nonpartisan elections for justices of the Michigan Supreme Court to the process for nominating candidates. The Court of Appeals entered judgment against the committee, holding that Mich. Const. art. VI, § 2 (1963) did not require nonpartisan nominations of candidates for the Michigan Supreme Court.On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. Giving the constitutional language a meaning that would naturally be conveyed to the popular mind and reviewing the circumstances surrounding the adoption of the constitutional provision and the purpose sought to be accomplished, the court held that the requirement of nonpartisanship applied to elections and did not preclude nomination of candidates by party conventions. The court also held that declaratory relief was not authorized under Mich. Ct. R 2.605(A) without a case of actual controversy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment holding that the Michigan Constitution did not require the legislature to provide for nonpartisan nomination of candidates for the Michigan Supreme Court.

Butcher v. Department of Treasury


Docket No. 75282


Supreme Court of Michigan


425 Mich. 262; 389 N.W.2d 412; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 4355


December 18, 1985, Argued  
June 27, 1986, Decided  
June 27, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 426 Mich 1201. Appeal dismissed by the Supreme Court of the United States on January 12, 1987, 93 L Ed 2d 821.


PRIOR HISTORY: 141 Mich App 116; 366 NW2d 15 (1984).
Butcher v. Department of Treasury, 141 Mich. App. 116, 366 N.W.2d 15, 1984 Mich. App. LEXIS 3164 (1984)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which upheld § 520(8) of the Michigan Income Tax Act (Act), Mich. Comp. Laws § 206.520(8) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 7.557(1520)(8)), in the taxpayers' action against appellees, Michigan Department of Treasury, Michigan State Treasurer, and Revenue Commissioner for the State of Michigan, challenging the constitutionality of the statute.


OVERVIEW: Section 520(8) of the Act provided for an income-graduated reduction in an existing property tax credit for each $ 1,000 of household income in excess of $ 65,000. The taxpayers filed an action against appellees, claiming that § 520(8) of the Act violated Mich. Const. art. 9, § 7, which prohibited a state income tax graduated as to rate or base. The trial court held that Mich. Comp. Laws § 206.520(8) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 7.557(1520)(8)) was unconstitutional. The court of appeals reversed the trial court's decision. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals. The court held that Mich. Const. art. 9, § 7 did not prohibit different rates of tax imposed upon different classes of taxpayers. The court determined that the property tax credit in Mich. Comp. Laws § 206.520 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 7.557(1520) was a property tax rebate that employed the state income tax as a vehicle for its reconciliation, and that the credit was paid to taxpayers regardless of their income tax liability. Therefore, the court ruled that Mich. Const. art. 9, § 7 was inapplicable to the income-based reduction in that credit in Mich. Comp. Laws § 206.520(8) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 7.557(1520)(8)).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals upholding the statute providing for the income-graduated reduction in the property tax credit in the taxpayers' action challenging the statute.

Civil Service Com. v. Department of Labor


Docket No. 77816


Supreme Court of Michigan


424 Mich. 571; 384 N.W.2d 728; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 4338

March 28, 1986, Decided

OVERVIEW: Appellee state civil service commission filed an injunction against appellant state department of labor challenging the constitutionality of 1985 Mich. Pub. Building Owners & Managers Asso. v. Public Service Com.


Docket Nos. 73552, 73553


Supreme Court of Michigan


424 Mich. 494; 383 N.W.2d 72; 1986 Mich. LEXIS 4333


November 14, 1985, Argued  
March 17, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 131 Mich App 504; 346 NW2d 581 (1984).
Building Owners & Managers Asso. v. Public Service Com., 131 Mich. App. 504, 346 N.W.2d 581, 1984 Mich. App. LEXIS 2413 (1984)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff ratepayers appealed the trial court's (Michigan) refusal to order a refund of a three-year steam rate increase allotted to defendant steam service company by defendant commission, allegedly without sufficient statutory or constitutional notice.


OVERVIEW: The ratepayers filed a class action to determine whether a three-year steam rate increase allotted to the steam service company by the commission, allegedly without sufficient statutory or constitutional notice, was refundable to the ratepayers even though the rate increase was subsequently upheld as reasonable following a full notice and a hearing. The ratepayers appealed the trial court's refusal to order a refund. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of the steam service company and the commission, which refused to order a refund to the ratepayers of a three-year steam rate increase that had been allotted to the steam service company by the commission.

People v. Petrella


Docket Nos. 71556, 73731


Supreme Court of Michigan


424 Mich. 221; 380 N.W.2d 11; 1985 Mich. LEXIS 1015


June 25, 1985, Argued  
December 30, 1985, Decided  
January 10, 1986, Released


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 124 Mich App 745; 336 NW2d 761 (1983).

 132 Mich App 259; 347 NW2d 215 (1984).
People v. Petrella, 124 Mich. App. 745, 336 N.W.2d 761, 1983 Mich. App. LEXIS 2952 (1983)
People v. Simpson, 132 Mich. App. 259, 347 N.W.2d 215, 1984 Mich. App. LEXIS 2469 (1984)



DISPOSITION: Petrella, affirmed. Simpson, reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In separate unrelated cases that were consolidated for appeal, defendants sought review of decisions of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the convictions of both defendants for first-degree criminal sexual conduct in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.520a(j) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.788(1)(j)).


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted in unrelated cases of first-degree criminal sexual conduct (CSC) in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.520a(j) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.788(1)(j)). On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction of one defendant and reversed the conviction of the other defendant. The court held that the term mental anguish as used in the CSC statute was not unconstitutionally vague and that it meant extreme or excruciating pain, distress, or suffering of the mind. The court held that there was sufficient evidence to support a finding that the victim of the first defendant suffered mental anguish as a result of the sexual assault. The court held that there was insufficient evidence that the second defendant's victim suffered mental anguish when the only evidence was that she cried and was upset after the assault. The court also held that the trial court erred in giving to the jury the definition of mental anguish found in Mich. Crim. Jury Instructions 20:2:11(5) because the CSC statute did not limit the mental anguish element to mental suffering that occurred at the time of the assault.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the first defendant's conviction because the criminal sexual conduct statute was not unconstitutionally vague and there was sufficient evidence to support a finding of mental anguish. The court reversed the second defendant's conviction because there was insufficient evidence that the victim suffered mental anguish and directed the entry of a judgment of conviction of third-degree criminal sexual conduct.

Durant v. State Bd. of Education


Docket Nos. 72895, 73245


Supreme Court of Michigan


424 Mich. 364; 381 N.W.2d 662; 1985 Mich. LEXIS 1019


June 25, 1985, Argued  
December 30, 1985, Decided  
February 11, 1986, Released


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Adair v. State, 250 Mich. App. 691, 651 N.W.2d 393, 2002 Mich. App. LEXIS 595 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 129 Mich App 517; 342 NW2d 591 (1983).

 130 Mich App 614; 344 NW2d 19 (1983).
Waterford School Dist. v. State Bd. of Education, 130 Mich. App. 614, 344 N.W.2d 19, 1983 Mich. App. LEXIS 3450 (1983)
Durant v. Department of Education, 129 Mich. App. 517, 342 N.W.2d 591, 1983 Mich. App. LEXIS 3278 (1983)



DISPOSITION: Durant, affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded. Waterford, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, taxpayers, local school boards and districts, brought two actions against defendants, State Board of Education and State Treasurer, that asserted the local schools received less financial aid from the state than was permissible under Mich. Const. art. 9, § 29. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) granted summary judgment to the state board and treasurer. The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: After the property values and the property tax in the districts increased, the local schools received less financial assistance from the state. They argued that the reduction in state funding violated Mich. Const. art. 9, § 29 (1963) because the schools were an existing service required of local government by state law. The court held that the mandate of a free public education of Mich. Const. art. 8, § 2 (1963) was not intended to be included in the obligations under Mich. Const. art. 9, § 29 (1963), which applied only to state statutes and agency rules. Further, education was not an activity or service required by state statute or agency rule. However, use of Mich. Comp. Laws § 21.233(6)(d) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 5.3194(603)(6)(d)) to reduce aid to local schools below the level of funding in 1979 was unconstitutional. Finally, the court noted that Mich. Const. art. 9, § 32 (1963) provided the remedy of mandamus by direct petition to the court of appeals because the local government claims review board was not yet established.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment entered by the lower court in favor of the state board treasurer as to the application of Mich. Const. art, 9. § 29, but reversed and remanded for a determination of reduction of state financial aid under statutory law.

People v. Buckey


Docket Nos. 73929, 74232


Supreme Court of Michigan


424 Mich. 1; 378 N.W.2d 432; 1985 Mich. LEXIS 1004


June 25, 1985, Argued  
December 4, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearings denied post, 1205.
Rehearing denied by People v. Buckey, 424 Mich. 1205, 1986 Mich. LEXIS 6505 (1986)
On remand at People v. McWhorter, 150 Mich. App. 826, 389 N.W.2d 499, 1986 Mich. App. LEXIS 2546 (1986)


PRIOR HISTORY: 133 Mich App 158; 348 NW2d 53 (1984).
People v. Buckey, 133 Mich. App. 158, 348 N.W.2d 53, 1984 Mich. App. LEXIS 2505 (1984)



DISPOSITION: Buckey, reversed and remanded. McWhorter, reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed and defendants cross-appealed two judgments of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed defendants' convictions because the prosecutor in both cases argued that defendant's presence at trial was an opportunity for him to fabricate testimony, because in one case the prosecutor asked defendant whether prosecution witnesses were lying, and because in the other the prosecutor questioned defendant about his finances.


OVERVIEW: In one case the prosecutor argued to the jury that defendant knew before he testified what the State's proofs were going to be and had plenty of time to try to figure a way around those proofs and prepare to tell his story in a convincing manner. In the other case, the prosecutor argued that defendant knew where his testimony had to go to avoid any implications and walked the line very carefully. Defendants claimed the comments penalized them for exercising their constitutional right to be present at trial. The court reversed the judgment because the arguments were only indirectly related to defendants' right to be present at trial; any resulting inference was not directly of guilt, but rather that defendants had the opportunity to conform their testimony because they heard other witnesses testify; opportunity and motive to fabricate testimony were permissible areas of inquiry; and in both cases the evidence supported the inference that defendants fabricated their testimony. The court agreed that it was error for the prosecutor to ask defendant to comment on witnesses' credibility, but held that the error was harmless and that a timely objection would have cured any prejudice.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals in both cases, remanded both cases to the court of appeals for consideration of the new issues raised in defendants' cross-appeals, and remanded one case for consideration of whether the prosecutor's questioning of defendant as to his financial condition was reversible error.

Employees & Judge of Second Judicial Dist. Court, Second Div. v. Hillsdale County


Docket Nos. 73180, 73952


Supreme Court of Michigan


423 Mich. 705; 378 N.W.2d 744; 1985 Mich. LEXIS 1010; 27 Wage & Hour Cas. (BNA) 748


March 6, 1985, Argued  
December 2, 1985, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Hillsdale, reversed. Cheboygan, administrative order vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant counties challenged orders of the Hillsdale County Circuit Court (Michigan) and the Cheboygan County Circuit Court (Michigan), which, respectively, 1) denied the county relief from the district court's administrative order to fund a retirement plan for its employees, and 2) required the county to pay wages and benefits to a court employee.


OVERVIEW: The trial courts issued orders compelling the counties to pay wages and benefits to the courts' employees in excess of appropriations. In the consolidated cases, the issues were whether a local control unit had the authority to decline to fund salary increases for district court employees which were not alleged or proven to be necessary to maintain a statutory function of the court or to provide for the overall administration of justice, whether a court could direct a county to appropriate funds by means of an administrative order, and whether a circuit judge could employ counsel to defend its interest in related litigation. After examining pertinent statutes and court rules, the court held that 1) a local control unit was required to approve salaries set by a court if they were reasonable and within appropriations, 2) if a dispute arose, a court could commence an action and would be required to prove that the appropriation was necessary to fulfill a duty imposed by statute, 3) a court could not compel payment of requested funding via an administrative order, 4) the Hillsdale county order was not based on a constitutionally conferred power or impairment of a statutory function.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the Hillsdale county order and vacated the Cheboygan county proceedings and remanded for a determination of attorney fees.

Livonia v. Department of Social Services


Docket Nos. 70315, 70316, 71155, 71377


Supreme Court of Michigan


423 Mich. 466; 378 N.W.2d 402; 1985 Mich. LEXIS 1016


May 8, 1984, Argued  
November 21, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 119 Mich App 806; 328 NW2d 1 (1982).

 123 Mich App 1; 333 NW2d 151 (1983).

 123 Mich App 767; 333 NW2d 350 (1983).
Greentrees Civic Asso. v. Pignatiello, 123 Mich. App. 767, 333 N.W.2d 350, 1983 Mich. App. LEXIS 2819 (1983)
Livonia v. Department of Social Services, 123 Mich. App. 1, 333 N.W.2d 151, 1983 Mich. App. LEXIS 2718 (1983)
Livonia v. Department of Social Services, 119 Mich. App. 806, 328 N.W.2d 1, 1982 Mich. App. LEXIS 3586 (1982)



DISPOSITION: Livonia I, affirmed, except as to the order of remand. Livonia II, affirmed. Greentrees, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In this consolidated appeal, both plaintiff cities and homeowners' association and defendant Department of Social Services (DSS) sought review of the judgments from the court of appeals (Michigan), which upheld licensure of group homes located in the applicable cities and subdivision. In one case, the court forbade DSS from placing mentally ill adults in such homes.


OVERVIEW: When the Department of Social Services (DSS) sought to create group homes to place mentally ill adults, the cities where such homes were situated as well as a homeowners' association sought to prevent such placement, both through administrative procedure and by injunctive relief in different actions. The trial courts upheld DSS' right to create such homes, although one court prohibited DSS from placing mentally ill adults therein. The court of appeals affirmed. The court affirmed the court of appeals' holdings, except to allow placing of mentally ill adults. The court held there was no legislative intent to subject such facilities caring for six or fewer adults to local zoning or deed restrictions. The City and Village Zoning Act (CVZA) did not divest home rule cities of their constitutional and statutory authority to enact zoning ordinances, in violation of Mich. Const. art 4, § 24 or the Due Process Clause. Although DSS failed to comply with a technical notice requirement, no one was prejudiced. Operation of such homes would not violate building and use restrictions contained in the owners' deeds. Motions for summary judgment were properly granted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the holdings of the court of appeals upholding DSS' creation of group homes in the towns and subdivisions in question. The court held that the procedure did not violate due process, there was no legislative intent to let local zoning laws or deed restrictions prevent such housing, and the relevant statutes allowed for "emotionally disturbed," and therefore mentally ill, persons to be placed in such houses.

Woodland v. Mich. Citizens Lobby


Docket Nos. 72625, 72332, 72333


Supreme Court of Michigan


423 Mich. 188; 378 N.W.2d 337; 1985 Mich. LEXIS 983


January 9, 1985, Argued  
November 13, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearings Denied 424 Mich 1204.
Rehearing denied by Woodland v. Mich. Citizens Lobby, 424 Mich. 1204, 1986 Mich. LEXIS 6500 (1986)


PRIOR HISTORY: 128 Mich App 649; 341 NW2d 174 (1983).
Equitable Life Assur. Soc'y of the United States v. Michigan Citizens Lobby, 418 Mich. 955, 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1785 (1984)
Woodland v. Michigan Citizens Lobby, 128 Mich. App. 649, 341 N.W.2d 174, 1983 Mich. App. LEXIS 3344 (1983)



DISPOSITION: Woodland, affirmed. Equitable Life, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens' group challenged an order from the court of appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the order from the trial court that granted appellee shopping mall an injunction to prevent the gathering of signatures for an initiative petition. Appellant shopping mall challenged an order from the Genesee Circuit Court (Michigan), which ruled that under Mich. Const. art. 1, §§ 3, 5, right of access to a private shopping center could exist.


OVERVIEW: Two separate cases came before the court. In the first case, the citizens' group sought to solicit signatures in the shopping mall for an initiative petition. The shopping mall denied the group access since it had a policy prohibiting noncommercial activities within the mall. The shopping mall sought and received an injunction against the citizens' group. In the second case, the citizens' group, after attempting to solicit in other malls, received a declaration from a county court that in certain cases, the state constitution provided a right of access for such signatures. The case was appealed prior to the evidentiary hearing. The court ruled that Mich. Const. art. 1, §§ 3, 5 did not prohibit large shopping malls from denying or restricting access to private individuals, finding that the Michigan free speech guarantees were impliedly limited to state action. The court also ruled that Mich. Const. art. 2, § 9 did not proscribe the conduct of private entities and impliedly confer a right of access to private property for the purpose of obtaining signatures for an initiative petition. The court found that the "public function" concept did not apply to the case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order from the court of appeals that granted the injunction to the shopping mall and reversed the order from the county court that ruled that a constitutional right of access could exist to solicit signatures for an initiative petition at a large private shopping mall.

State Treasurer v. Wilson


Docket No. 73877


Supreme Court of Michigan


423 Mich. 138; 377 N.W.2d 703; 1985 Mich. LEXIS 1005


June 4, 1985, Argued  
November 12, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at State Treasurer v. Wilson, 150 Mich. App. 78, 388 N.W.2d 312, 1986 Mich. App. LEXIS 2514 (1986)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 132 Mich App 648; 347 NW2d 770 (1984).
State Treasurer v. Wilson, 132 Mich. App. 648, 347 N.W.2d 770, 1984 Mich. App. LEXIS 2529 (1984)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state treasurer appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the trial court's holding that the Prison Reimbursement Act (Act), 1935 Mich. Pub. Acts 253, was void for vagueness and violated equal protection, in the treasurer's action to obtain reimbursement under the Act for defendant inmate's workers' compensation benefits paid by defendant carrier to the inmate's mother.


OVERVIEW: Despite the inmate's status at the time of his workers' compensation redemption with the carrier, and despite the trial court's temporary restraining order enjoining the carrier from paying the redemption, the carrier sent a check to the inmate's mother who cashed it and disbursed the funds. The trial court took under advisement the treasurer's motion for an order finding the carrier in contempt for violating the restraining order, rejected the contention of the inmate and the carrier that the nonalienability provision of the Workers' Disability Compensation Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.821 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(821)), prevented recovery, but agreed with their constitutional challenges that the Prison Reimbursement Act (Act) was unconstitutional. Reversing the decisions of the appellate and trial courts, the court reasoned that the Act named only three prison facilities because they were the only ones in existence in 1935 and thus such delineation did not violate constitutional provisions regarding equal protection in the face of clear legislative intent to require all wardens to provide information to the auditor general for reimbursement purposes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court, which affirmed the trial court judgment holding that the Prison Reimbursement Act was unconstitutional, and remanded the cause.

People v. Ramsey


Docket Nos. 67269, 68636


Supreme Court of Michigan


422 Mich. 500; 375 N.W.2d 297; 1985 Mich. LEXIS 955; 71 A.L.R.4th 661


December 6, 1983, Argued  
September 17, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied in Ramsey, 424 Mich 1201. Rehearing denied in Boyd, 424 Mich 1202.
Rehearing denied by People v. Ramsey, 424 Mich. 1201, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2038 (1985)
Rehearing denied by People v. Boyd, 424 Mich. 1202, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2037 (1985)



DISPOSITION: Ramsey, affirmed. Boyd, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two separate cases, defendants were found guilty, but mentally ill. One was convicted of second-degree murder and the other was convicted of armed robbery and assault with intent to commit robbery while armed. Both challenged the constitutionality of Mich. Comp. Laws § 768.36; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1059, which permitted the guilty, but mentally ill verdict. The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed the convictions. The defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Both defendants contended that the guilty, but mentally ill verdict denied them the due process of law guaranteed by the Fourteenth Amendment. The court concluded that Mich. Comp. Laws § 768.36; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.1059 was constitutional because it did not violate principles of fairness. The court also concluded that the verdict did not deflect a jury's attention from the issues of guilt or innocence by adding an irrelevant verdict which brought the risk of impermissible jury compromise. The court found that the inclusion of the guilty, but mentally ill verdict was not so confusing to the jury that it denied defendants a fair trial. The court also rejected the assertions of the defendant who was convicted of murder that a finding of mental illness negated malice aforethought as a matter of law and that a trial court must, in its findings of fact following a bench trial, affirmatively state that the mental illness did not negate the necessary intent for second-degree murder.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions.
