County of Washtenaw v. State Tax Com.


Docket Nos. 72445, 72446, 72447, 72862, 72863


Supreme Court of Michigan


422 Mich. 346; 373 N.W.2d 697; 1985 Mich. LEXIS 946


June 7, 1984, Argued  
September 4, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 126 Mich App 535; 337 NW2d 565 (1983).
County of Washtenaw v. State Tax Com., 126 Mich. App. 535, 337 N.W.2d 565, 1983 Mich. App. LEXIS 3057 (1983)



DISPOSITION: Washtenaw, Lapeer, and Oakland, affirmed in part, reversed in part, and modified. Livingston and Ingham, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff counties appealed the final state equalized valuations adopted by the State Tax Commission (Michigan) for each county that was used for their 1982 property tax levy, challenging the method defendant tax commission used in arriving at the state equalized value of real property.


OVERVIEW: Counties sued tax commission over the method it used in arriving at the state equalized value of real property in two respects: (1) the effect of land contracts and other creatively financed transactions on true cash value; and (2) the need to employ a trending factor to the data base of sales-ratio studies to accurately pinpoint the true cash value of property on tax day. The court held that when the seller of real property has contributed in a significant way to the buyer's ability to obtain financing at an interest rate that is artificially below the prevailing cost of borrowing, a method of valuation that separates the cost of the artificially low financing from the sales price to achieve the true cash value of the property is required to comport with the constitution. Finally, commission properly refused to adopt counties' proposed method of trending for the sales-ratio studies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, reversed in part, and modified the ruling of the tax commission.

 

167 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rehkopf


Docket Nos. 69219, 69819


Supreme Court of Michigan


422 Mich. 198; 370 N.W.2d 296; 1985 Mich. LEXIS 941


May 8, 1984, Argued; April 18, 1985, Resubmitted  
June 25, 1985, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Rehkopf, reversed. Snyder, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On consolidated appeals defendant one sought review of the denial of her motion to quash, and defendant two sought review of his conviction and sentence which the Court of Appeals (Michigan) affirmed. Defendants were charged with inciting, inducing, or exhorting another person to commit murder under § 157b of the Penal Code codified at Mich. Comp. Laws Ann. § 750.157b (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 27.354(2)).


OVERVIEW: The court held that § 157b enlarged the scope in certain limited situations and a codification of the common-law solicitation under Mich. Comp. Laws Ann. § 750.505 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.773) and enlarged the punishment when certain felonies and misdemeanors were involved; however § 157b was not dependent on the actual commission of the crime. The court held that if a person solicited another to commit an offense, and the listener acted on the solicitation, the speaker was liable as an accomplice under Mich. Com. Laws Ann. § 767.39 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.979) and could be found guilty of the substantive offense committed by the actor except of attempted murder under Mich. Com. Laws Ann. § 750.91 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.286). The court held further that if a listener agreed to commit the offense, both parties could be found guilty of conspiracy under Mich. Comp. Laws Ann. § 750.157a (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.354(1) and that if the listener had no intent to commit the offense, the speaker could be found guilty of common-law solicitation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of defendant one's motion to quash and remanded for trial. The court vacated the appellate court's decision to sentence defendant two to mandatory life imprisonment and affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Blue Cross & Blue Shield v. Milliken


Docket No. 68903


Supreme Court of Michigan


422 Mich. 1; 367 N.W.2d 1; 1985 Mich. LEXIS 781


October 4, 1983, Argued  
April 16, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied post, 1206. Appeal dismissed by the Supreme Court of the United States on October 7, 1985, 474 U.S.   ; 88 L Ed 2d 33 (1985).
Rehearing denied by Blue Cross & Blue Shield of Mich. v. Governor, 422 Mich. 1206, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2619 (1985)
Appeal dismissed by Blue Cross & Blue Shield v. Milliken, 474 U.S. 805, 106 S. Ct. 40, 88 L. Ed. 2d 33, 1985 U.S. LEXIS 3118 (1985)



DISPOSITION: Remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff health care corporation filed an action against defendants, Governor, Attorney General, and Insurance Commissioner of the State of Michigan, challenging the constitutionality of the Nonprofit Health Care Corporation Reform Act (act), 1980 Mich. Pub. Act 350, Mich. Comp. Laws § 550.1101 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 24.660(101) et seq.). The Ingham Circuit Court (Michigan) entered an injunction and certified questions of law for review.


OVERVIEW: The health care corporation alleged numerous constitutional violations. The lower court enjoined the enforcement of the act and ordered the parties to comply with the previously existing enabling acts. The court used a balancing approach to weigh the degree of the impairment of the contractual rights and obligations of the parties against the justification for the impairment as an act of the state's police power to implement legislation for a legitimate public purpose, and held that the provisions of the act alone, as well as their cumulative effect, did not violate the contract clause. The court held that none of the provisions of the act constituted a taking in violation of the Fifth Amendment, but that the act was unconstitutional in the following three particulars (1) § 205(6) of the act unconstitutionally delegated legislative power because it lacked standards; (2) the act's arbitrary restrictions on administrative services only contracts violated equal protection; and (3) § 402(7) of the act established an improper burden of proof. The court held that the unconstitutional provisions were easily severable and did not affect the constitutionality of the remainder of the act.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the action to the lower court with instructions to dissolve the injunction and to take such further action that was consistent with the court's opinion.

In re Midland Pub. Co.


Docket No. 68862


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


420 Mich. 148; 362 N.W.2d 580; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1282; 11 Media L. Rep. 1337


January 4, 1984, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
January 9, 1985, Released


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 113 Mich App 55; 317 NW2d 284 (1982).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff newspaper appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the suppression orders that defendant, district court judge, issued in two criminal cases, holding that there was no prior restraint, that the public did not have constitutional rights of access to pretrial proceedings, and that there was no constitutional right to inspect public records.


OVERVIEW: The newspaper contended that the suppression orders that the district court judge issued pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.520k (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.788(11)) were unconstitutional prior restraints on publication and that it had a constitutional right of access to pretrial proceedings in criminal cases. The court held that the suppression orders were not unconstitutional prior restraints because the suppression orders did not prohibit the newspaper from publishing information that it had concerning the criminal matters. According to the court, the statute, by its explicit terms, imposed no restraints on any person. Rather, the court held that the statute merely directed that he names of the victim, actor, and details of the alleged offense be suppressed for an event-terminable time. The court also held that neither the press nor the public possessed any federal or state constitutional rights of access to probable cause pretrial proceedings. Furthermore, the court held that while the public and press generally did possess a statutory right of access to all courtroom proceedings, the issued suppression orders were valid restrictions on that right.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court, which held that no prior restraint on publication existed and that there was no constitutional right of access to pretrial proceedings.

People v. Hayes


Docket No. 67551


Supreme Court of Michigan


421 Mich. 271; 364 N.W.2d 635; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1317


October 4, 1984, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
February 1, 1985, Released



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order by the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the defendant's conviction for first-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: An individual was charged with the murder of his wife. The trial court ordered defendant to submit to psychiatric evaluation. Defendant refused to cooperate on three separate occasions. Pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 768.20a(4) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.104(1)(4), the prosecution moved to bar any testimony regarding defendant's insanity on the basis of his failure to cooperate with the court ordered examinations. The trial court granted the motion and a jury convicted defendant and sentenced him to life in prison. The appellate court affirmed the conviction and defendant sought review. The court determined that the preclusion sanction in the statute at issue did not violate defendant's Sixth Amendment right to present a defense because the right to assert the insanity defense was not an absolute right and did require compliance with certain rules of procedure. Moreover there was an overriding policy consideration requiring a defendant to cooperate if he intended to assert the insanity defense. Additionally the court found that the statute was not vague in that it provided fair notice of the requirement to cooperate with court ordered psychiatric evaluations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant.

Tallman v. Department of Natural Resources


Docket No. 71187


Supreme Court of Michigan


421 Mich. 585; 365 N.W.2d 724; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1328


May 9, 1984, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
February 11, 1985, Released


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 123 Mich App 132; 333 NW2d 193 (1983).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state department of natural resources challenged the judgment of the trial court (Michigan), which held that it unconstitutionally revoked appellees' licensed fishermen's fishing license after they refused to allow appellant's employees to board their fishing boats without a warrant or probable cause of wrongdoing.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state department of natural resources attempted to board the fishing boats of appellee licensed fishermen to inspect for illegal acts. Appellees refused to allow appellant's employees to board without a warrant. Appellee stripped appellants of their commercial fishing license, and appellees filed suit. The court held that appellant could not force appellees to submit to a search without warrant, and therefore improperly deprived them of their fishing license. Appellant challenged the ruling, and the court held that appellant could validly seek to suspend the licenses because the legislation and license did not require the waiver of the constitutional right against unreasonable search as a condition to the issuance of a license. The court stated that a search without a warrant in the commercial fishing industry was not unreasonable because the fishing industry has historically been pervasively regulated; the fishing industry was a peculiar industry dealing with food and hence required operations open to public scrutiny to search without warrant; and the inspections were limited to business operations and excluded private and non-business operations and areas.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, and held that appellant state department of natural resources could strip appellee commercial fishermen of their fishing licenses for refusing to allow appellant's employees to board their fishing boats to inspect for illegal acts without a warrant.

Johnson v. Robinson


Docket No. 69342


Supreme Court of Michigan


420 Mich. 115; 359 N.W.2d 526; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1280


January 5, 1984, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township sought review of the decision from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed the circuit court's decision that the Zoning Board of Appeals erred in denying appellee landowner a variance.


OVERVIEW: The township argued that the variance was properly denied by the Zoning Board of Appeals. On appeal, the court held that the landowners did not persuade the court that the 99-foot width requirement was unconstitutional throughout the zoned district. The court held that there was no abuse of discretion in denying the request for an area variance. The court held that the division of the property caused the landowners' problems. The court held that because prior to the split, the land was being properly used in conformance with the zoning ordinance, the township did not unconstitutionally deprived the landowners of their property rights. The court held that the facts were undisputed and served as a competent, material, and substantial evidentiary basis for the decision of the Zoning Board of Appeals.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments of the circuit court and the appeals court, which held that the Zoning Board of Appeals erred in denying the landowner a variance. The court reinstated the decision of the Zoning Board of Appeals.

Shaughnesy v. Michigan Tax Tribunal


Docket No. 68612


Supreme Court of Michigan


420 Mich. 246; 362 N.W.2d 219; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1286


October 4, 1983, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
January 14, 1985, Released



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals, which dismissed their complaint seeking an order of superintending control over the tax tribunal, concluding that superintending control was an inappropriate remedy in the case.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers filed an action for superintending control after receiving property tax bills, which disclosed that the state equalized values assigned to their properties resulted in the properties being assessed at an amount in excess of 50 percent of their true cash values. The taxpayers claimed that they received their bills after the deadline for filing an appeal to the tax tribunal and that the tax tribunal dismissed the appeals of the taxpayers who filed an appeal because the appeals were past the deadline. The court reversed the dismissal of the taxpayers' action, holding that the taxpayers were entitled to maintain an action for superintending control. The availability of an appeal was not an absolute bar to obtaining superintending control because such appeal would not have been an adequate remedy. Although the taxpayers might have obtained adequate relief individually by appealing the tax tribunal's dismissals, that avenue would not have provided adequate relief for the class as a whole.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the complaint and remanded the case for plenary consideration.

People v. Mallory


Docket Nos. 64270, 65203, 65206


Supreme Court of Michigan


421 Mich. 229; 365 N.W.2d 673; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1316


June 8, 1983, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
February 1, 1985, Released


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearings denied 422 Mich 1201.


PRIOR HISTORY: 97 Mich App 359; 296 NW2d 22 (1980).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed their convictions for first degree felony murder in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.316; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.548, and their statutorily mandated sentences of life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of felony murder in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.316; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.548, and sentenced to the statutorily mandated term of life imprisonment. Defendants sought review by the appellate court, which confirmed the convictions. Defendants appealed. On appeal, the court reversed the convictions. The court held that defendants had the right to a prompt arraignment under Mich. Comp. Laws § 764.13;Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.871(1) and Mich. Comp. Laws § 764.26; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.885, which was violated because the reverse writ procedure under which they were arrested was without legal effect and could not be employed to justify the detention of defendants. Due to the unlawful detentions, the shoes, blood tests, and statements by one defendant were wrongfully admitted at trial because they were fruit of the poisonous tree. The admission of such evidence was reversible error. The court did not abuse its discretion by allowing the jury a view of the scene even though conditions were different. It was error not to allow defendants to be present at the jury view because it was part of the trial and no security risk was articulated. The case was remanded.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' convictions because the trial court committed reversible error by admitting evidence that was acquired during an unlawful detention. The case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

176 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hackett


Docket Nos. 67291, 69249


Supreme Court of Michigan


421 Mich. 338; 365 N.W.2d 120; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1319


January 4, 1984, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
February 1, 1985, Released


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 114 Mich App 773; 319 NW2d 390 (1982).



DISPOSITION: Hackett, reversed. Paquette, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following their individual convictions of rape by the trial court (Michigan), both defendants challenged the constitutionality of the rape-shield statute, Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.520j (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.788(10)), which excluded evidence of the victim's prior sexual conduct with persons other than the accused. Defendants claimed that the statute violated the Sixth Amendment right of confrontation and cross-examination.


OVERVIEW: In ruling on the admissibility of the proffered evidence, the court held that the trial court was required to rule against the admission of evidence of a complainant's prior sexual conduct with third persons unless that ruling unduly infringed on defendants' constitutional right to confrontation. Accordingly, the court ruled that the trial court was required to maintain an in camera evidentiary hearing to afford defendants an opportunity to offer proof of the evidence and to demonstrate its relevance for that purpose. The trial court had the responsibility to restrict the scope of cross-examination to prevent questions which would harass, annoy, or humiliate sexual assault victims and to guard against mere fishing expeditions, as well as to ascertain the probative value of the proposed evidence. Finding sufficient evidence in the record, the court declined to remand the cases for in camera hearings. It examined the facts of the two cases in light of the stated constitutional standard, concluding that the application of the statute under the particular facts of these cases violated neither defendant's right to confrontation.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the application of the rape-shield statute by the trial court under the particular facts in each case did not violate either defendant's right to confrontation.

December 28, 1984, Decided  
January 15, 1985, Released

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners filed a claim against defendant city in the Tax Tribunal (Michigan) and alleged that a special assessment imposed on their properties for the purpose of paying the streets abutting their properties was unconstitutional. The Tax Tribunal ruled that it lacked jurisdiction. The landowners appealed.


OVERVIEW: The landowners alleged that a special assessment imposed on their properties for the purpose of paving the streets abutting their properties was unconstitutional because the value of their properties would not be enhanced by the asphalt paving. The court held that the landowners failed to file a petition with the tax tribunal within the 30-day period provided by the Tax Tribunal Act and since they pointed to no other applicable provision that granted them a longer period of time to do so, the tax tribunal was without jurisdiction to consider the petition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the tax tribunal's decision that the tax tribunal was without jurisdiction to consider the petition and therefore it was correctly dismissed.

Eyde v. Charter Township of Lansing


Docket Nos. 67135, 68112


Supreme Court of Michigan


420 Mich. 287; 363 N.W.2d 277; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1375


October 4, 1983, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
January 15, 1985, Released


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 105 Mich App 370; 306 NW2d 797 (1981).

 109 Mich App 641; 311 NW2d 438 (1981).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed decisions of the Ingham Circuit Court (Michigan), which entered summary judgments in favor of defendants, township and drainage board. The landowners had brought an action against the defendants claiming procedural defects in the establishment of a drainage system and challenging the constitutionality of § 536 of the Drain Code.


OVERVIEW: The landowners brought an action against defendants, claiming procedural defects in the establishment of a drainage system and challenging the constitutionality of § 536 of the Drain Code, Mich. Comp. Laws § 280.536; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 11.1536. The trial court entered summary judgments in favor of defendants. The court of appeals affirmed the judgments of the trial court. The court affirmed the judgments of the court of appeals. The court held that: (1) challenges to special assessments were within the exclusive jurisdiction of the tax tribunal, and the trial court correctly granted defendants' motion for accelerated judgment on all but that portion of the action that challenged the constitutionality of § 539 of the Drain Code; (2) the tax tribunal did not have the authority to invalidate acts of the legislature; thus, the trial court did not err in retaining jurisdiction over that portion of the complaint that challenged the constitutionality of § 539; (3) because the challenge to § 539 presented purely questions of law and no genuine issue of material fact, the trial court did not err in deciding the constitutionality of the provision on a motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals, which affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendants.

 

179 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Ross v. Consumers Power Co.


Docket Nos. 64241, 68861, 68885, 69672, 70177, 70246, 70456, 70598, 71266


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


420 Mich. 567; 363 N.W.2d 641; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1301


August 16, 1983, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
January 22, 1985, Released


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied in Zavala, 421 Mich 1202.


PRIOR HISTORY: 93 Mich App 687; 287 NW2d 319 (1979).

 113 Mich App 30; 317 NW2d 273 (1982).

 117 Mich App 179; 323 NW2d 642 (1982).

 114 Mich App 792; 319 NW2d 674 (1982).

 119 Mich App 418; 326 NW2d 528 (1982).

 120 Mich App 506; 328 NW2d 70 (1982).

 121 Mich App 61; 328 NW2d 570 (1982).

 123 Mich App 352; 333 NW2d 278 (1983).



DISPOSITION: Ross, reversed in part. Willis, affirmed. Siener, affirmed. Rocco, affirmed. Regulski, reversed in part. Trezzi, affirmed. Disappearing Lakes, affirmed. Zavala, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On appeal of nine cases from the Courts of Appeals (Michigan), the court was required to reexamine the extent of immunity from tort liability which the governmental tort liability act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.1401 et seq.; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.996(101) et seq., and the common law provided to the state and its agencies, non-sovereign governmental agencies, and the officers, agents, and employees of these state and local governmental agencies.


OVERVIEW: In one of nine cases, plaintiffs sued defendant power company for injuries resulting from contact with overhead power lines maintained by the company. The parties settled, but the power company filed a third party complaint against appellants, district and drain commissioner. The appellate court concluded, among other things, that the district was not immune from tort liability, as the construction of a drain was not of the "essence of governing." The district appealed. Its appeal involved only the direct liability of a non-sovereign governmental agency for its negligence in contracting out, supervising, and inspecting the construction of a drain. The court found that any right to supervise the actual construction of a drain was impliedly authorized by the district's general power over the establishment, construction, and maintenance of drains. The court reversed in part, noting that the trial court correctly found that the district was immune from tort liability. The court held, among other things, that all governmental agencies, state and local, were immune from tort liability for injuries arising out of the exercise or discharge of a non-proprietary, governmental function.


OUTCOME: The decisions were affirmed in six cases and reversed in the others, based on the finding that the governmental agencies were not immune from tort liability.

Adrian v. State


Docket No. 71739


Supreme Court of Michigan


420 Mich. 554; 362 N.W.2d 708; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1300; 27 Wage & Hour Cas. (BNA) 335


October 2, 1984, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
January 17, 1985, Released


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 124 Mich App 72; 333 NW2d 582 (1983).
Adrian v. State, 124 Mich. App. 72, 333 N.W.2d 582, 1983 Mich. App. LEXIS 2829 (1983)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the state of Michigan and its officers and departments, sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which held that plaintiffs, 23 cities, were entitled to reimbursement for overtime payments made to their firefighters pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 408.384a. The state alleged that it was not liable for such payment because it was not required by statute and would be improper.


OVERVIEW: The cities filed their action in the court of claims, which determined that the state was liable for reimbursement for the firefighters' overtime pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 408.384a(7), but denied them a money judgment reimbursing them for costs incurred as a result of compliance. The court of appeals reversed and remanded for a determination of a monetary award, and the state took an appeal. On review, the court found that for all years at issue, § 408.384a(7) created a legal obligation upon the state to reimburse the cities even if such funds were not specifically appropriated for that purpose during a particular year. The court found without merit the state's claim that such payments would be unconstitutional. The court found that the cities were not asking it to compel the legislature to appropriate moneys, but merely requesting a money judgment to be enforced pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.6458. The court affirmed the judgment, ruled that it was within the jurisdiction of the court of claims to award them a money judgment, and remanded the action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals, which found in favor of the cities and against the state, and remanded the action to the court of claims for a determination of the cities' monetary award for reimbursement of overtime payments to their firefighters.

Eastway v. Eisenga


Docket Nos. 71532, 71533


Supreme Court of Michigan


420 Mich. 410; 362 N.W.2d 684; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1295


May 9, 1984, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
January 17, 1985, Released


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 421 Mich 1202.

PRIOR HISTORY: Eastway v. Eisenga, 418 Mich. 881, 1983 Mich. LEXIS 1772 (1983)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, employer and injury fund, sought review of a judgment of the trial court (Michigan), which awarded workers' compensation disability benefits to plaintiff employee.


OVERVIEW: The employee occasionally worked for the employer, mainly during haying and potato seasons. While the employee was working for the employer for a day, the employee was caught in a self-loading wagon and became a quadriplegic. A hearing referee awarded disability benefits to the employee on the basis of the referee's understanding that the agricultural exclusion of Mich. Comp. Laws § 418.115(e) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.237(115)(e)) was unconstitutional. The appeals board and the appellate court affirmed the referee's decision. The court reversed and remanded the case. The court held that the classifications drawn by the statute were rationally related to the permissible goal of recognizing the economic uniqueness of Michigan's agricultural employers. The court found that the legislature could have reasonably concluded that agricultural employers needed to be protected from the additional overhead of workers' compensation insurance premiums. The court noted that farmers had no power to raise their prices to absorb additional overhead costs. The court held that the classification was constitutionally sound and did not violate the employee's right to equal protection of the law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's judgment that was against the employer and injury fund and that awarded workers' compensation disability benefits to the employee.

Snyder v. Charlotte Public School Dist.


Docket No. 71152


Supreme Court of Michigan


421 Mich. 517; 365 N.W.2d 151; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1325; 43 A.L.R.4th 745


October 5, 1983, Argued

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, parents of a sixth grade child, sought review of a judgment from a court of appeals (Michigan), which affirmed a trial court's judgment entered in favor of appellee school district in the action filed by the parents that sought an order compelling the school district to enroll their child in sixth grade band at the public school.


OVERVIEW: A sixth grade child was enrolled in a private nondenominational Christian academy and her parents attempted to enroll her in a band course offered at a public school. The public school refused to enroll the student because it did not offer shared time courses and limited its enrollment to only full time students. There was no showing that the shared time instruction would result in decreased full time public school enrollment nor that it would place an unreasonable burden on the public school system. The court held that the nonessential elective courses offered to public school students should also be offered to resident nonpublic school students.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment.

Baker v. General Motors Corp.


Docket Nos. 59861, 59862, 59863


Supreme Court of Michigan


420 Mich. 463; 363 N.W.2d 602; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1298; 120 L.R.R.M. 2741


June 9, 1983, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
January 17, 1985, Released


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied 422 Mich 1201.
Rehearing denied by Baker v. GMC, 422 Mich. 1201, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2618 (1985)
Jurisdiction noted by Baker v. General Motors Corp., 474 U.S. 899, 106 S. Ct. 224, 88 L. Ed. 2d 223, 1985 U.S. LEXIS 3924 (1985)
Affirmed by Baker v. General Motors Corp., 478 U.S. 621, 106 S. Ct. 3129, 92 L. Ed. 2d 504, 1986 U.S. LEXIS 137 (1986)


PRIOR HISTORY: Baker v. General Motors Corp., 409 Mich. 639, 297 N.W.2d 387, 1980 Mich. LEXIS 253 (1980)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant workers challenged a decision of the Board of Review (Board) (Michigan), which on remand held that a "meaningful connection" existed between the payment of emergency union dues and a labor dispute which caused the workers' unemployment. The Board denied the workers unemployment benefits under the Michigan Employment Security Act (MESA) § 29(8)(a)(ii), Mich. Comp. Laws § 421.29(8)(a)(ii) (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 17.531(8)(a)(ii)).


OVERVIEW: The workers were not entitled to unemployment benefits if they were directly involved in the labor dispute that led to their termination. Direct involvement included financing. The workers paid increased strike dues during the labor dispute. The court held that the extraordinary dues constituted a "meaningful connection" to the labor dispute and therefore financed the dispute. The court found three elements of a meaningful connection between the financing and the labor dispute: (1) whether the purpose of the financing foreseeably included the labor dispute that caused the unemployment; (2) whether the financing was significant; and (3) whether the financing occurred at a time proximate to the labor dispute that caused the unemployment. The court found a meaningful connection between the increased dues and the strike that led to the workers' termination. The workers challenged the constitutionality of MESA and the court concluded that the National Labor Relations Act did not pre-empt MESA and that MESA did not violate the Supremacy Clause of the U.S. Constitution. The court also found no violation of the workers' First Amendment freedom of association rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Board's decision and found that it properly held that the workers were disqualified from receiving unemployment benefits.

People v. Reid


Docket Nos. 67451, 68939


Supreme Court of Michigan


420 Mich. 326; 362 N.W.2d 655; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1291


May 5, 1983, Argued  
December 28, 1984, Decided  
January 15, 1985, Released

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In separate cases, defendants were charged with kidnapping, armed robbery, and criminal sexual conduct. Defendants entered conditional guilty pleas with the agreement that they be allowed to appeal the denial of their motions to suppress evidence. On appeal the court consolidated the actions to consider whether such conditional appeals were permissible.

Defendants entered guilty pleas with the proviso that they be entitled to appeal the denial of their motions to suppress evidence. The court affirmed the convictions. However, the court held that Michigan law permitted defendants to enter conditional guilty pleas, appeal adverse rulings, and withdraw their guilty pleas if they prevailed on their appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions but held that a defendant was entitled to offer a conditional plea of guilty, and, after a conviction on such a plea, he was entitled to appeal from the adverse ruling on a search and seizure claim. If the defendant's claim was sustained on appeal, he was entitled to withdraw his guilty.

People v. Wesley


Docket Nos. 66597, 67161, 67162, 69160, 69195, 69196, 69532, 69533

May 4, 1983, Argued

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearings denied in Gerald Phillips, Phillip Phillips, and Threet, post, 1202.
Later proceeding at People v. Phillips, 421 Mich. 1202, 1985 Mich. LEXIS 2270 (1985)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Taormina v. Foltz, 831 F.2d 297, 1987 U.S. App. LEXIS 13392 (6th Cir. Mich., 1987)


PRIOR HISTORY: 103 Mich App 240; 303 NW2d 194 (1981).

 112 Mich App 98; 315 NW2d 868 (1982).
People v. Threet, 414 Mich. 867, 1982 Mich. LEXIS 1626 (1982)
People v. Phillips, 112 Mich. App. 98, 315 N.W.2d 868, 1982 Mich. App. LEXIS 2829 (1982)
People v. Wesley, 103 Mich. App. 240, 303 N.W.2d 194, 1981 Mich. App. LEXIS 2698 (1981)



DISPOSITION: Wesley, Taormina, Gerald Phillips, and Phillip Phillips, affirmed. Threet and Dopp, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, defendants sought review of their convictions by the trial courts (Michigan) pursuant to the Michigan kidnapping statute, Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.349 (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 28.58).


OVERVIEW: Defendants were all convicted of kidnapping. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the appellate court to uphold the convictions of the first defendant and the second set of defendants. However, the court reversed the appellate court and reinstated the convictions of the third set of defendants. The court, in reviewing § 750.349 (§ 28.58), held that kidnapping fell into two categories. The court found that forcible confinement involved an element of asportation, or movement of the victim. As to secret confinement, the court found that a specific intent requirement obviated the need for an asportation element. The court reviewed the jury instructions in all defendants' cases and found that the jury was adequately instructed regarding the asportation element of forcible confinement involved in all three cases. The court noted that kidnapping did not result in overcharging because murder or attempted murder was also involved in each case. The court held, regarding the third set of defendants, that a jury instruction was not erroneous because it stated that a kidnapping conviction could be based on movement incidental to the crime of assault with intent to commit murder.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court which sustained the kidnapping convictions of the first defendant and the second set of defendants. The court, however, reversed the judgment of the appellate court regarding the third set of defendants and reinstated their kidnapping convictions.

Armco Steel Corp. v. Department of Treasury, Corp. Franchise Fee Div.


Docket Nos. 68441, 68983, 68984, 68985, 68986


Supreme Court of Michigan


419 Mich. 582; 358 N.W.2d 839; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1271


October 5, 1983, Argued  
December 3, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 111 Mich App 426; 315 NW2d 158 (1981).

 113 Mich App 679; 318 NW2d 537 (1982).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, State of Michigan, sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which affirmed that 1978 Mich. Pub. Acts 392, constituted a denial of equal protection as to appellees, domestic and foreign corporations, who had timely requested refunds for the tax years prior to 1975. In various tax years preceding 1975, the domestic and foreign corporations were issued assessments following unauthorized audits and recomputations.


OVERVIEW: The domestic and foreign corporations were among those corporate taxpayers who first paid their deficiencies and subsequently filed refund requests with the state's Franchise Fee Division. The corporations alleged 1978 Mich. Pub. Acts 392, as applied to them, violated the Equal Protection Clause of the U.S. Constitution, and the Equal Protection and Uniformity of Taxation Clauses of the Michigan Constitution. The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court. The court determined that corporate taxpayers who had chosen to pay their deficiencies and then requested refunds pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 450.310, Mich. Stat. Ann. § 21.210(1), were consistently denied refunds. The court found that other corporations who withheld payment of their deficiencies and petitioned for a redetermination pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 450.309, Mich. Stat. Ann. § 21.210, were treated more favorably. The only distinction was that one group paid their deficiencies while the other group did not. The court ruled that this was not a natural distinguishing characteristic that bore a reasonable relationship to the object of classification.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court and held that the state legislature was not empowered to validate the Franchise Fee Division's persistent discrimination between two groups of taxpayers who were in reality but one class.

Sziber v. Stout


Docket Nos. 68466, 68467, 68468, 68469, 68485, 68486, 68487, 68488, 68489


Supreme Court of Michigan


419 Mich. 514; 358 N.W.2d 330; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1266


May 5, 1983, Argued  
October 31, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 111 Mich App 450; 315 NW2d 166 (1981).

 Morgan v McDermott, 382 Mich 333; 169 NW2d 897 (1969), overruled.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After third-party plaintiffs, drivers and owner, entered consent judgments in a wrongful death action, they filed third-party actions against third-party defendant county road commissions for contribution pursuant to Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.2925; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 27A.2925. The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed the Tuscola County Circuit Court's accelerated judgments dismissing the third-party actions. The drivers and owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The accident that resulted in the loss of decedents' lives occurred at an intersection which divided two counties. The trial court concluded that the two-year statute of limitations established in Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.1411; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.996(111) barred the third-party complaints. The court of appeals concluded that the third-party actions were not within the scope of the waiver of government immunity found in Mich. Comp. Laws § 691.1402; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 3.996(102). The court held that both of the lower courts erred. The court concluded that the contribution statute, Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.2925; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 27A.2925, established an independent, substantive cause of action. Therefore, the court held that the drivers and owner had a substantive right to contribution against the road commissions that was not barred by statutory governmental immunity. Moreover, the right to contribution was not barred by the statute of limitations governing the underlying tort actions. Because the administratrix could have proceeded directly against the road commissions, the drivers and owner were entitled to prosecute their claims for contribution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

Michigan Council 25, AFSCME, AFL-CIO v. Wayne Cnty.


No. 74521


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


419 Mich. 943; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 2226

PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Court of Appeals No. 65902.

 

189 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cooke


Docket Nos. 68974, 69972


Supreme Court of Michigan


419 Mich. 420; 355 N.W.2d 88; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1183


June 8, 1983, Argued  
September 18, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 113 Mich App 272; 317 NW2d 594 (1982).

 118 Mich App 220; 324 NW2d 795 (1982).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals of Michigan, which dismissed two of the state's appeals. In the first appeal, the state appealed the order of the trial court, which acquitted defendant one on the basis of insufficiency of the evidence, and in the second appeal, the state appealed the order of the trial court, which dismissed the charges against defendant two on the basis of entrapment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant one proceeded to trial on the charge of burning insured property. Following the jury's verdict of guilty, the trial court entered a directed verdict for defendant on the basis of insufficiency of the evidence. Defendant two was charged with delivery of LSD. Following the presentation of all evidence, the trial court dismissed the action on the basis of entrapment. The state appealed both orders in separate appeals, and the court of appeals dismissed the appeals, reasoning that it had no jurisdiction thereof. On the state's appeal, the court affirmed the judgments of the court of appeals. Based on amendments to § 3 of the Code of Criminal Procedure (code), the court determined that § 12 of the code exclusively governed the state's right to appeal an adverse decision in a criminal case. The only provision in § 12 which would have allowed an appeal only provided for such an appeal when defendants had not been placed in jeopardy. Because both defendants were placed in jeopardy, this provision did not apply. Because the state's right to appeal was governed by statute only, the court of appeals had no jurisdiction absent a provision in § 12 to consider the state's appeals.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the court of appeals, which dismissed the state's appeals of an adverse decision in the criminal prosecution of defendant one and of an adverse decision in the criminal prosecution of defendant two.

People v. Robideau


Docket Nos. 64548, 64549, 66010, 67114, 67760


Supreme Court of Michigan


419 Mich. 458; 355 N.W.2d 592; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1185


June 7, 1983, Argued  
September 18, 1984, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied in Griffin, 420 Mich 1201.


PRIOR HISTORY: 94 Mich App 663; 289 NW2d 846 (1980).

 105 Mich App 58; 306 NW2d 392 (1981).

 106 Mich App 798; 308 NW2d 703 (1981).
People v. Brown, 105 Mich. App. 58, 306 N.W.2d 392, 1981 Mich. App. LEXIS 2964 (1981)
People v. Bouknight, 106 Mich. App. 798, 308 N.W.2d 703, 1981 Mich. App. LEXIS 3003 (1981)
People v. Robideau, 94 Mich. App. 663, 289 N.W.2d 846, 1980 Mich. App. LEXIS 2409 (1980)



DISPOSITION: Robideau, affirmed. Griffin, reversed and remanded. Brown, affirmed, and the cause remanded to the trial court for further proceedings. Bouknight, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of the decisions in the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which had decided whether the prohibition in either the United States or Michigan Constitution against placing a person twice in jeopardy prohibited, in a single trial, convictions of both first-degree criminal sexual conduct under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.520b(1)(c) and the underlying "other felony" of armed robbery or kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: In four separate cases, the court of appeals upheld defendants' convictions of both first-degree criminal sexual conduct under Mich. Comp. Laws § 750.520b(1)(c) and the underlying felony of armed robbery or kidnapping used to prove the charge of first-degree criminal sexual conduct. In each case, the court of appeals decided whether the prohibition in either the United States or Michigan Constitution against double jeopardy prohibited, in a single trial, convictions. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction in which the court of appeals had found no violation of the prohibition against double jeopardy, and reversed those convictions that had found a violation. The court held that convictions of both first-degree criminal sexual conduct and the underlying felony of armed robbery or kidnapping in a single trial were not barred by either constitution. The court held that because first-degree criminal sexual conduct carried a maximum penalty of life imprisonment, and the predicate crimes also carried maximum penalties of life imprisonment, the legislature intended that the compound crime and the predicate crimes be separately punished.


OUTCOME: The court held that convictions of both first-degree criminal sexual conduct and the underlying felony of armed robbery or kidnapping in a single trial were not barred by either the Michigan or U.S. Constitutions. Thus, the decisions finding a violation of double jeopardy protections were reversed and those in which no violation of double jeopardy was found were affirmed.

Charter Township of Delta v. Dinolfo


Docket No. 67485


Supreme Court of Michigan


419 Mich. 253; 351 N.W.2d 831; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1060


October 4, 1983, Argued  
July 19, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 106 Mich App 1; 308 NW2d 437 (1981).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Charter Township of Delta (township) filed actions for injunctive relief against defendant homeowners. The Court of Appeals (Michigan) affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which granted summary judgment in favor of the township. The homeowners appealed.


OVERVIEW: The homeowners contended that the definition of "family" in Delta Township, Mich., Ordinance § 2.2.0(28) both prohibited and allowed property uses in an unreasonable, arbitrary, and capricious manner in violation of the Due Process and Equal Protection Clauses of the United States and Michigan Constitutions. The court stated that to limit residentially zoned property to use by a traditional family and a number of unrelated persons was permissible under the United States Constitution. The court found that there had been no evidence presented that unrelated persons had any less a need for the advantages of residential living or that they had as a group behavior patterns that were more opprobrious than the population at large. The court held that in the absence of such demonstration to justify the classification, § 2.2.0(28) was arbitrary and capricious under the Due Process Clause of the Michigan Constitution. Thus, § 2.2.0(28) was capricious, arbitrary, and in violation of the Due Process Clause of the Michigan Constitution in that it limited the composition of groups in a manner that was not rationally related to the stated goals of the zoning ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals.

Burie v. Sec'y of State


No. 74559


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


419 Mich. 1207; 355 N.W.2d 375; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 2559

193 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Anderson v. Oakland County Clerk


Docket No. 74338


Supreme Court of Michigan


419 Mich. 313; 353 N.W.2d 448; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1064


June 15, 1984, Argued  
June 19, 1984, Order Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Amendatory order entered June 21, 1984. Order denying other motions entered June 27, 1984. (Reported ante, p 142.) Opinions filed August 20, 1984.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Secretary of State and Attorney General of Michigan, disputed a decision of the Oakland Circuit Court (Michigan) that ruled that 1983 Mich. Pub. Acts 256, which provided for the reapportionment of the legislature, was unconstitutional. The court considered whether, based on the last-minute substitution of a bill, the law violated either Mich. Const. of 1963, art. 4, § 24, or Mich. Const. of 1963, art. 4, § 26, or both.


OVERVIEW: H.B. 4481, 1983 Leg. (Mich. 1983), then a fairly innocuous bill to make housekeeping changes in the Mich. Election Law, 1954 Mich. Pub. Acts 116, was on the verge of passage, when, after a third reading of the bill, the Michigan Senate substituted another bill reapportioning the legislature, which was enacted as 1983 Mich. Pub. Acts 256. On appeal, the court noted that concentration of analysis on the title of a bill was inappropriate; reference had to be made also to the bill's content. The court held that H.B. 4481 as finally passed served none of the objectives of the original bill. It reflected neither the purposes contained in the act purportedly amended nor those of the original bill that it substituted. The court held that the function of the change of purpose provision in art. 4, § 24 was to fulfill the command of the five-day rule in art. 4, § 26. The court noted that the only similarities between the original H.B. 4481 and the substitute bill were the numbers and the clause, which read "The People of the State of Michigan enact." The court held that there was a clear violation of art 4, § 24, an evasion and nullification of the five-day rule. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the circuit court. The court issued an order detailing how the upcoming elections should be conducted in the absence of a reapportionment of the legislature.

Jarosz v. Detroit Auto. Inter-Insurance Exchange


Docket No. 68003


Supreme Court of Michigan


418 Mich. 565; 345 N.W.2d 563; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1042


May 3, 1983, Argued  
March 12, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 109 Mich App 86; 310 NW2d 903 (1981).
Jarosz v. Detroit Auto. Inter-Insurance Exchange, 109 Mich. App. 86, 310 N.W.2d 903, 1981 Mich. App. LEXIS 3203 (1981)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from the Court of Appeals of Michigan, which affirmed the trial court's granting of summary judgment for defendant insurance company that reduced plaintiff's work-loss benefits after plaintiff began receiving Social Security benefits.


OVERVIEW: Defendant insurance company began paying plaintiff work-loss benefits under the No-Fault Insurance Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.3101 et seq. (Mich. Stat. Ann. § 24.13101 et seq.), after plaintiff was injured in automobile accident. Several months later plaintiff turned 65 and began receiving full Social Security benefits. Defendant deducted from the work-loss benefits the amount by which the social security benefits would have been reduced had plaintiff not been injured and continued working. Plaintiff filed suit and the trial court granted summary judgment for defendant, which the court of appeals affirmed. The court reversed. The court held that plaintiff's benefits should not have been reduced after applying a two-part test. The court held the government benefits should not be deducted from the no-fault benefits because they 1) did not serve the same purpose as the no-fault benefits, and 2) they were not provided as a result of the same accident.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals, holding that defendant should not have reduced plaintiff's no-fault benefits, after applying a two-part test.

 

195 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Perez v. State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co.


Docket No. 67122


Supreme Court of Michigan


418 Mich. 634; 344 N.W.2d 773; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1044


April 6, 1983, Argued  
March 12, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 105 Mich App 202; 306 NW2d 451 (1981).
Perez v. State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co., 105 Mich. App. 202, 306 N.W.2d 451, 1981 Mich. App. LEXIS 2978 (1981)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured parties challenged the decision of the court of appeals which held that the amount required to be paid as workers' compensation that had not been and would not be provided to a worker injured in a motor vehicle accident because the employer did not have workers' compensation coverage, was required to be subtracted from work-loss benefits otherwise required to be paid under the no-fault automobile liability act.


OVERVIEW: Injured parties were using a truck in connection with employment when it collided with a tractor trailer, causing injury. Appellee insurance company refused to pay no-fault medical and work-loss benefits. On review, the court determined that workers' compensation benefits that would not be paid because the employer failed to purchase insurance were not the kind of governmental benefits the legislature intended to be subtracted from no-fault benefits. The court found that the "required to be provided" clause of Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.3109(1) meant that the injured person was obliged to use reasonable efforts to obtain payments that were available from a workers' compensation insurer. If workers' compensation payments were available to an employee and he did not seek them, the no-fault insurer was entitled to subtract the available payments because of the failure of the injured person to use reasonable efforts to obtain them. The court concluded that the amount of workers' compensation payments could not be subtracted from injured parties' no-fault work-loss benefits because the payments were unavailable due to the employer's failure to provide coverage.


OUTCOME: The order requiring that the payable but unpaid amount of workers' compensation be subtracted from work-loss benefits was reversed.

 

196 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Morris v. Metriyakool


Docket Nos. 67480, 68208


SUPREME COURT OF MICHIGAN


418 Mich. 423; 344 N.W.2d 736; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1039


March 9, 1983, Argued  
March 1, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 107 Mich App 110; 309 NW2d 910 (1981).

 110 Mich App 202; 312 NW2d 212 (1981).
Jackson v. Detroit Memorial Hospital, 110 Mich. App. 202, 312 N.W.2d 212, 1981 Mich. App. LEXIS 3306 (1981)
Morris v. Metriyakool, 107 Mich. App. 110, 309 N.W.2d 910, 1981 Mich. App. LEXIS 3066 (1981)



DISPOSITION: Morris is affirmed; Jackson is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court consolidated two cases that involved the Medical Malpractice Arbitration Act of 1975 (MMAA), Mich. Comp. Laws § 600.5040 et seq. In one case, appellant patient sought review of the Michigan Court of Appeals' holding that MMAA did not violate the patient's due process rights. In the second case, appellants, physician and hospital, sought review of the court of appeals' holding that MMAA did violate a patient's due process rights.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that the MMAA did not violate the Due Process Clause of either the federal or state constitutions by requiring that a doctor or representative of a health-care provider serve as a member of the three-person arbitration panel. Two of the justices concluded that the composition of the arbitration panel did not offend guarantees of due process and, thus, found it unnecessary to determine whether the state significantly involved itself in the challenged action. Two other justices concluded that the composition of the arbitration panel did not involve "state action" implicating the Due Process Clauses. Pursuant to the MMAA, the state did not compel the parties to arbitrate their disputes. Therefore, the justices concluded that the challenged conduct did not sufficiently receive the imprimatur of the state, did not involve the exercise of powers that were traditionally the exclusive prerogative of the state, and did not necessitate a symbiotic relationship between the state and the private party. The court also held that the arbitration agreements involved in the instant case were not contracts of adhesion and were not based upon unconscionable constructive fraud.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment which held that the MMAA did not violate the patient's due process rights. The court reversed the judgment which found that the MMAA unconstitutionally violated the patient's due process rights. The court reinstated the order of the trial court submitting the matter to arbitration.

 

197 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Tebo v. Havlik


Docket Nos. 68033, 70881


Supreme Court of Michigan


418 Mich. 350; 343 N.W.2d 181; 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1036


April 6, 1983, Argued  
February 6, 1984, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied March 26, 1984, as to Tebo, 419 Mich 1201.
Rehearing denied by Tebo v. Havlik, 419 Mich. 1201, 1984 Mich. LEXIS 2547 (1984)


PRIOR HISTORY: 109 Mich App 413; 311 NW2d 372 (1981).
Tebo v. Havlik, 109 Mich. App. 413, 311 N.W.2d 372, 1981 Mich. App. LEXIS 3282 (1981)



DISPOSITION: The summary judgment in Burns is reversed, and the case is remanded for trial. In Tebo, the motion for summary judgment is denied, the decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed, and the case is remanded for trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed, asking for a grant of summary judgment in one case, and plaintiff appealed the grant of summary judgment in the other in claims pursuant to the Dram Shop Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 436.22.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs in two cases alleged injuries resulting from automobile collisions caused by defendants' and appellees' bars serving alcohol in violation of the Dram Shop Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 536.22. In both cases, the actual tortfeasors were released from liability. One lower court found that plaintiff no longer had a claim because of the release, as the Supreme Court of Michigan had decided in another case, while these cases were pending. The court disagreed because it would have been unjust to apply the requirement to persons other than those in the court in that case, given that the previous case overruled the earlier decision. No fault insurance law did not limit recovery from defendants and appellees because it was intended to limit stacking of insurance policies to keep rates low, and the insured should not have been limited in recovery merely because he had the foresight to purchase insurance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded one Dram Shop case and affirmed another decision, as it would have been unjust to apply a requirement to persons other than those in court in that case, given that the previous case overruled an earlier decision, and the law limited recovery only for injuries that benefitted a no fault insurer.

 

198 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nash


Docket No. 68280


Supreme Court of Michigan


418 Mich. 196; 341 N.W.2d 439; 1983 Mich. LEXIS 265


May 4, 1983, Argued  
December 19, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 110 Mich App 428; 313 NW2d 307 (1981).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing and remanding for a new trial is affirmed; but its holding on the search and seizure issue is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted the prosecutor's application for leave to appeal the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Michigan), which reversed defendant's conviction and remanded the case for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of second degree murder for the shooting death of her husband. The court of appeals reversed, holding that evidence of the body of the victim should have been suppressed at trial because it was discovered as a result of an unconstitutional search of defendant's property and the fact that the evidence was seized from the attorney could not be made known to the jury. The court reversed on both holdings. Under the totality of circumstances, the court concluded that the cardboard box was not searched within the meaning of the Fourth Amendment. Whatever expectation of privacy defendant had in the contents of the box while it was in the trailer was defeated once the box was moved outside the trailer. Defendant's expectation of privacy was no longer objectively reasonable or legitimate under federal law. The officer's testimony that the evidence was seized from defense counsel's office was proper. The attorney-client privilege did not extend to protect situations where the attorney in some way altered the character of evidence, including where the attorney took the evidence into his possession.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals.

 

199 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


Bray v. Department of State


Docket No. 65164


Supreme Court of Michigan


418 Mich. 149; 341 N.W.2d 92; 1983 Mich. LEXIS 262


April 7, 1983, Argued  
December 1, 1983, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing denied post, 1202. Certiorari Denied by the Supreme Court of the United States on June 18, 1984.
Rehearing denied by Bray v. Dep't of State, 418 Mich. 1202, 1984 Mich. LEXIS 1667 (1984)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bray v. Michigan, 467 U.S. 1252, 104 S. Ct. 3537, 82 L. Ed. 2d 842, 1984 U.S. LEXIS 2694 (1984)


PRIOR HISTORY: 97 Mich App 33; 294 NW2d 236 (1980).
Bray v. State, 97 Mich. App. 33, 294 N.W.2d 236, 1980 Mich. App. LEXIS 2621 (1980)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, a class of Michigan residents (residents), brought an action against appellant State seeking a declaration of their rights and a refund of a portion of a fee they were required to pay in order to register their uninsured vehicles in 1973. The Wayne Circuit Court (Michigan) entered a judgment in favor of the residents, holding that they were entitled to a refund. The Michigan Court of Appeals affirmed. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The residents claimed that they paid for a full year's privilege of driving an uninsured motor vehicle and that the inauguration of the State's mandatory no-fault insurance act on October 1, 1973, midway through the registration year, entitled them to a refund. The court concluded that there was no factual basis for finding that the Motor Vehicle Accident Claims Act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 257.1101 et seq.; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 9.2801 et seq., was in the nature of an insurance policy for the residents or that the required fee established any contractual right. The court found that the uninsured motorist fee was more in the nature of a tax for the privilege of operating an uninsured motor vehicle than either a license fee or an insurance premium. Further, the court found no federal or state constitutional infringement of the residents' rights occasioned by the enactment of the no-fault insurance act, Mich. Comp. Laws § 500.3101 et seq.; Mich. Stat. Ann. § 24.13101 et seq. Therefore, the court held that there was no contractual, statutory, or constitutional basis for concluding that the State was liable to the residents for a refund.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment.

 

200 of 2443 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wakeford


Docket No. 64385


Supreme Court of Michigan


418 Mich. 95; 341 N.W.2d 68; 1983 Mich. LEXIS 255


March 9, 1983, Argued  
November 23, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 94 Mich App 249; 288 NW2d 381 (1979).



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed, sentences vacated, and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the trial court (Michigan) that convicted him of two counts of armed robbery and one count of possession of a firearm during the commission of a felony. Defendant claimed he was denied the right to counsel at trial and sentencing, improper admission of prior conviction evidence, and violation of double jeopardy provisions of the U.S. Const. amend. V and Mich. Const. art. 1, § 15.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction for two counts of armed robbery and one count of possession of a firearm during the commission of a felony. Defendant represented himself after his motion for different counsel was denied. On appeal, defendant claimed that he was denied the right to counsel, that prior conviction evidence was improperly admitted, and that punishment for two armed robbery counts constituted double jeopardy in violation of U.S. Const. amend. V and Mich. Const. art. 1, § 15. The court held the legislature intended to permit multiple armed robbery convictions and that defendant was not unconstitutionally punished for the same offense but was convicted of two separate and distinct offenses. The court held that the denial of the motion for new counsel was proper because defendant failed to demonstrate the actual conflict of interest necessary to establish a denial of the right to counsel. The court held that although defendant's waiver of his right to appointed counsel at trial was voluntary, it did not constitute a waiver of counsel at sentencing. The court vacated defendant's sentences because no affirmative waiver of counsel at sentencing was found on the record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment that convicted defendant of two counts of armed robbery and one count of possession of a firearm during the commission of a felony, but held that the trial court's failure to obtain a knowing and intelligent waiver of the right to counsel at the time of sentencing required resentencing. The court vacated the sentences and remanded for resentencing.
